The Red Page
The Drame
Arthur Wing Pinero's The Big Drum,
(H.eittettuitm, per Dymock’s Library) was
written before'the war. but produced only
last September- ip London. He calls it a
comedy; but the war-audience considered it
a tragedy. For it had what the average
audience wrongly terms “an unhappy end-
ing”—which means merely that the hero and
the heroine don’t decide to marry as the
curtain falls. Pinero explains that he
regarded the ultimate break between his
hero and his heroine as the happiest thing
that could happen to them. “But comedy
of this order has a knack of cutting rather
deeply, of ceasing, in some minds, to be
comedy at all.” So pressure was immedi-
ately put upon the dramatist—presumably
by the manager, who preferred a long run
to artistic consistency—and at the third
performance the curtain fell upon the pic-
ture, “violently and crudely brought about,”
of heroine in hero’s arms. Pinero says lie
made the alteration against his principles
and against his conscience; yet not alto-
gether unwillingly. The times are too
depressing for “ironic comedy”; and perhaps
in such times “it is the first duty of a writer
for the stage to make concessions to his
audiences, and. above everything, to try to
afford them a complete, if brief, distraction
from the gloom which awaits them outside
the theatre.” _ There is in this sad confession
of one who, judged by the quantity and the
technical quality of his long record, is Eng-
land’s premier dramatist, a hint for the 5396
Australian dramatists who are now seeking
the suffrages of the public through the
medium of J.C.W., Ltd., and other patriotic
Australian theatrical firms. Remember “So
Long. I.etty” and chase gloom.
*
However, Pinero refuses to stultify him-
self in print. He publishes The Big Drum
as it was first written. The big drum is not
military: it is the big drum of personal
advertisement beaten so loudly by the mem-
bers of that curious London society to which
Pinero, wise man. confines himself. Roope,
the stereotyped Cayley Drummle who
appears in all his plays, says to a brilliant
but unpopular novelist: “Advertising minus
talent goes a long way; advertising plus
talent is irresistible. Feed the papers. The
more you do for them, the more they’ll do
for you. Newspaper men are the * nicest
chaps in the world. Feed them gratis with
bright and amusin’ ‘copy,’ as you term it,
and they'll love and protect you for ever....
Dash it, they’ve got to advertise soap, and
soap's essential! Why not literature, which
isn’t?'’
Philip, the poor novelist, loves Ottoline,
the daughter of a newly-ricli man who is
drumming himself into society. The girl,
despite her dislike for her parents’ methods
of advertisement, is, the reader gradually
learns, after all their child. The infection
ot personal advertisement has got into her
blood. But she loves Phillip ; and when the
family, shocked at their discovery that
Philip is not even in Who’s Who, refuse to
countenance the match, Philip offers to wait
till his next novel is published. If that is
not a big success then he is a failure, and
will release .Ottoline, But he is quite sure
that it is going to be a best-seller.
The novel is published, and achieves an
instantaneous success. Philip gets a state-
ment showing actual sales from his pub-
lisher, and arranges a of those
cosy little dinners that Pinero invariably
provides—for the celebration of his triumph.
And then, gradually, with clever culminative
effect, Philip learns the truth. Ottoline,
fearful for Philip's success, has made \ that
success sure—by buying up edition after
edition of the novel. And Ottoline, found
out, confesses that she is “an incurably xml-
gar woman....We xvomen catch the color
of our surroundings. But xvhat xx'e xvere, we
continue to be—in the grain.” Well, there
is nothing for the lovers to do but to sepa-
rate. But an audience is composed solely of
match-makers, as Pinero discovered. The
play could logically have no other end than
that printed. The audience’s troubles! Also
the manager’s!
Jt
Technically the play is highly interesting.
The embryo playwright is always concerned
to provide dramatic speeches in his dramatic
moments. Pinero long ago found out that
what people say in emotional moments has
no connection, with literature. What really
matters is what they do. Hence his stage
directions are extraordinarily detailed. These
arc a few of the things that Ottoline does
m one little scene : Leaning her hand upon
his arm, melting plucking at the arm of
her chair. .. .leaning lack she searches
for her handkerchief ... .wiping a tear from
her cheek.. . .blowing her hose .rising and
wandering to tke writing-table... .inspecting
some of the pieces of bric-a-brac upon the
table replacing an object upon the table
and moving to the other side of the room
with abandon ... .her laughter dies out, leav-
ing her with a serious, appealing face....
pleadingly... .with a little moue brush-
ing her hair from her brow fretfully....
stamping her f00t... .contracting her
shoulder-blades .... impetuously .... check-
ing herself .... penitently .... drearily. ...
starting up as if to rid herself of disagree-
able sensations... .moving, to the chair on
the left... .sinking into the chair... .fan-
ning herself with her hand.., .glancing up
at him... .reproachfully, her eyes meeting
his... .lightly but softly... .shaking her
head pityingly resting her ' elbow
upon the arm of her chair and her chin on
her hand, musingly ... .half laughing, half
sighing... .partly in jest, partly seriously
... .falling in with his humor... .staring at
him... .rising, smiling but discomposed....
faintly putting her hand to her forehead
... .passionately etc., etc.
Here are two actual scenes of high
dramatic tension:—
Ottoline (warding off the recollection
with a gesture): Oh, don’t —!
Philip: Ha, ha. ha! A shame of me —!
Ottoline (turning from him): Do get
your hat and coat.
Philip (going into the vestibule):
Where's your car?
Ottoline ( moving towards the vestibule):
Iu South Square.
Philip (returning to her, a cape over his
shoulders, a soft hat on his head): Eigh :
o’clock!
Ottoline: Eight o’clock. (He takes he
hands and they stand looking into cac
other’s eyes.)
Philip (after a pause): Fancy!
Ottoline (faintly): Fancy! (He
drawing her to him slowly when, uttering
low cry, she embraces him wildly and pas
sionately.) Oh! (clinging to him) Oh, Phil
Oh—oh—oh— !
Philip (responding to her embrace)
Otto—Otto—!
Ottoline (. breaking ,from him) : Oh —!
The second scene is even more tense:
is chiefly “Ha, ha!” except when it is “Ho
ho!”
Philip : Ha, ha, ha, ha ! Ha, ha, ha —!
Lady Filson (dolefully): Oh, Ottoline—
Ottoline—!
Philip: Ha, ha, ha —!
Ottoline (creeping to the nearer end
the writing table): Il’ssh ! IPssh ! Philip—
Philip—!
Philip (loudly) : Ho, ho, ho—!
Ottoline: Don’t! Don’t! (making
movement of entreaty towards him): Phil—
Phil—!
(His laughter ceases abruptly and, he look
her full in the face.)
Philip (after a moment’s pause, bit
ingly) : Thank you—thank you— (turning
from her and seating himself in the chair
the smoking table and resting his chin o>
his fist) thank you.
That is how modern drama is written. Ii:
you omit the “Ho’s” and the “Ha’s,” it i
exactly how the scenario of the movies
written —or shouted.

Edward Knoblauch is always experiment
ing in drama. He is by birth a citizen o
the U.S.A., though his home is in England.
He, first came into prominece by The Fawn
produced in 1911, and running a whole
season in America. Next came his well
known Kismet, and the equally well-known
Milestones tin collaboration with Arnolc
Bennett). Discovering America, The Head
master, My Lady’s Dress and Marie Odile
followed—-a remarkably varied output. My
Lady’s Dress is now published by that use
ful organisation known as the Drama League
of America—and the publishing of plays is
but one of the activities of that society. This
play was produced in London in April, 1914
and in New York in October of the same
year. The construction is quite novel. It
is really nine complete plays within a play
each of which contributes an essential ele-
ment to Knoblauch’s theme. The audience
is first introduced to an elaborate gown
which is to be worn by one of the charac-
ters on an occasion where it will have an
important influence upon her husband’s
future. Then, in the manner of the movies,
the dramatist shows how the materials of the
dress, the lace, the silk, the artificial flowers,
the fur, etc., are manufactured or obtained.
These playlets, each complete in itself, main-
tain no chronological consistency or sequence.
Thus the third scene takes place in the
present, while a later scene, that dealing
with the old lace used on the costume, is
dated 1(550; and the localities of the plays
differ widely. All these scenes lead logically
up to the final act, in which the woman who
is to wear the dress, and who has apparently
dreamed the previous scenes, reappears. But
she is shown at the dressmaker’s, before the
gown has been. sent home. Thun this scene
is actually prior in time to the opening
scene of the play, when the dress had already
arrived at the lady’s home. One of the
characters remarks to the dressmaker, a
fashionable artist in attire, “Strikes me you
have to rummage all over the world to put a
dress together nowadays. Means a lot of
work for a lot of people, don’t it?” The
dress-artist casually replies: “Oh, the work
is nothing—nothing. It’s the putting
together that counts. The rest’s easy enough.
You see—it all lies with the artist, not with
the materials.” The audience has already
seen the amount of work that was necessary
to procure those materials.
One feature, cunningly devised by the
diamatist- in order to give the leading
actors a Chance to show their versatility—is
the fact that in each of the scenes the lead-
ing characters strikingly resemble the
heroine and hero. Thus each of the “leads”
appears in a variety of characters. In this
final scene not only does Anne, the one who
is to wear the dress, appear, but subse-
quently, as Anita, she appears as the manni-
ng who wears the dress at the shop. And
in the next scene the audience is shown the
other side of the black velvet curtain frpm
which the manniquins had appeared. The
final scene shows Anne waking up from the
sleep induced by a headache powder, and
recounting her curious dream. “The glimpses
of all those lives that go to make a flimsy
thing like my dress! The awful struggles
for .existence! And you (her husband) were
mit somehow, and I was in it, too. And
yet we weren’t ourselves, and vet we were—
and everybody was pushing, lighting, hatin^
loving—in one endless battle Oh. it was
mere headache-powders this I
feel quite queer about this dress now. To
think of the sickening expenditure of human
energy it stands for!”
. Tim play is highly dramatic and interest-
ing, but of more value as an experiment than
as a play.
The First Referendum in History
Edwin E. Slosson has unearthed a unique
Egyptian hieroglyphic script found at Sinai,
of which he publishes in the New York
Independent an approximate translation.
The '“find” has created considerable excite-
ment in Higher Criticism circles. The trans-
lation, which has been slightly altered for
Australian comprehension, runs thus :■ —
DEFEAT OF THE TEN COMMAND-
MENTS !
Mosaic Laws Voted Down on Referendum
by Decisive Majorities.
Sinai, 2000 b.c. —Some surprise has been
expressed in political circles that every
article of the Decalogue recently submitted to
popular referendum was defeated, although
each was voted upon independently and sev-
eral had a considerable measure of influen-
tial support during the recent campaign. The
first two commandments* had against them
a strong body of opinion among persons
who feared that they might lead to an alli-
ance between church and state. The first,
however, ran far ahead of the second, which
had against it the Sculptors’ Union and all
the artists, with the single exception of the
Futurists, who supported the commandment
on the ground that they were guiltless of
making anything in the likeness of anything
that is in Heaven above, or that is in the
earth beneath, or that is in the waters undei
the earth. The third commandment was
doomed from the start by the cry that it was
an interference with freedom of speech. In
the electorate of Billingsgate it did not
receive a single vote. The fourth command-
ment was downed by the untiring efforts
of the Anti-Blue Law League, supported bj
the Manly Soda-fountain Shopkeepers
Union. Like the fifth, it received a certain
amount of support in the rural districts, but
not enough to overcome the negative
majority returned by the great centres of
population.
The defeat of the sixth amendment had
not been anticipated. But there seems to
have been a feeling, even in the most conser-
vative circles, that a commandment which
was all very well in time of peace might
weaken the nation in case of war. The
seventh commandment, loudly denounced as
“wowserism,” lost out by a three to one vote.
Sodom and Gomorrah reported less than ten
votes in its favor, although a prominent man
had expressed the hope that peradventure
fifty might be found in the Twin Cities. The
eighth _ commandment had been expected to
carry in spite of the powerful opposition of
the Burglars’ Union. What seems to have
killed it was the fear lest the High Court
might construe the law in a sense broad
enough to include the operations of high
finance. The defeat of the eighth command-
ment involved the defeat of the tenth, which
also had against it the organised opposition
of the Socialist party. The ninth command-
ment seems to have been beaten on the face
of the returns, probably owing to the cam-
paign conducted against it by a large section
of the press. The professional politicians
gave it no support and are said to have
secretly instructed their supporters to vote
it down on the ground that it would take the
life out of politics.
It is not thought that the electorates still
to hear from will make a great change in
the result. The electorate is in a very con-
servative mood and is not disposed to wel-
come changes. As Rameses, Premier of
upper and Lower Egypt, told our reporter
last evening: “The present is not the time
for tinkering with the constitution.”
“Note.-—As every member of The Bulletin staff,
appealed to in turn, proved unable to recollect, in
their order, the Ten Commandments, and even the
Religious Editor forgot the Seventh Commandment,
it is presumed that several of this page’s readers
might be a little hazy about the thing. A prolonged
search m the office caverns resulted in the discovery
of an authentic text of the original decalogue, which
is here published (copyright, all rights reserved)
for public information:—

This discovery is extremely awkward.
Every nation needs some commandments:
Australia needs at least a score. Evidently
something must be done to fill this national
need, and in a hurry. Hence this page has
decided to ask its readers for a new Deca-
logue, and will pay a guinea for the best 10
Australian commandments received by June
I<. It should be noted that the command-
ments must be specially applicable to Aus-
tralasia and the moral needs of Austral-
asians. And, above all, they should be up-
to-date. For the purposes of this competi-
tion which is more of the nature of a patri-
otic national service than a mere competi-
tion for a prize—the commandments sub-
mitted need not be graven on tablets of
stone. Preferably they should be type-
written, but not in hieroglyphics.

Cartoons Magazine (Chicago, 25 cents)
remains the best illustrated monthlv maga-
zine of the war. Despite its pardonable
preoccupation with what passes for poli-
tics in the Neutrited States, it contains a
brilliant selection of the best cartoons of the
war. including the most “strafeing” of the
Hun cartoons. In the April issue Jack
Flanagan’s Bulletin front-page is repro-
duced. and Edward Dyson’s “The Church
Bells,” from this paper, is reprinted.
AUSTRALIA IN GALLIPOLI.
The blue /Egcan waves may run to thee,
Now, as of old; thy cliffs may watch the
light
Purple the east and mark, as dies the 'Night,
No change in that which they were wont to
see.
Put yet, for us, the long, long years to be
Shall not erase, through all their lab'ring
flight,
Those grave-hewn records of our Austral
. might
Which consecrate thy crags, Gallipoli!
And, though we knew defeat, was it in vain
We purchased knowledge with heroic blood ?
If thus tec learned that old-time virtues beat
Within the pulses of our sons again;
If thus we won our spurs of Nationhood,
Thanks be to God who sent us such defeat!
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.

WINCHESTER
Cartridges
Tor Rifles, Revolvers or Pistols
The most accurate shooting rifle, revolver
or pistol made will do unsatisfactory work
unless the cartridges used in it are as accurate
as the arm. For that reason it is important
when buying to get cartridges that have an
established reputation for quality. Win-
chester —the w brand of cartridges in all
calibers from .22 to .50 are unexcelled for
accuracy, penetration and uniform shooting
qualities. They cost no more than inferior
makes and are for sale everywhere. If you
want to get the most satisfactory results with
your rifle, revolver or pistol, always use
Winchester ammunition. Do not simply
ask for “some cartridges” when you buy,
but be sure to specify Winchester make
and look for the bold w on the box.

The Edison Diamond
Disc Phonograph
reproduces the original music
exactly as it was played or sung by the artist—every
overtone being reproduced.
Recently 300 phonograph experts attended a recital in Orange,
U.S.A. Alice Verlet—the famous Belgian prima donna—stood
beside the Edison Diamond Disc Phonograph.
There came the clear notes of the beautiful song “Caro Nome” from
“Rigoletto.” Which was singing—phonograph or lady? The ear
* could not distinguish. Sometimes Miss Verlet sang; sometimes the
phonograph, but the only guide as to which was singing, was the
movement—or otherwise—of Miss Verlet’s lips.
Faces were lit up with surprise—even amazement—a modern
miracle was happening.
But every day music lovers—once sceptical—are being convinced of
the unique beauty, clearness and naturalness of this wonderful
instrument. It has none of the faults common to other talking
machines. It stands alone; has no tone of its own, but is a perfect
vehicle for the re-creation of the artist’s voice—or instrument. No
needles to change; records are unbreakable.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hie King's Sweetheart.
Oh, the King’s sweetheart is more than
Queen;
Red-caped cardinals bow to her;
V omen who hate as women hate,
Prim dowagers in robes of state,
Curtsy lowly without demur.
She tramples them with her silken feet....
But outside there is the waiting street.
The King’s sweetheart can nestle down
In velvet cushions and tease the King,
Withhold her mouth from the royal kiss,
Let great kings learn what a man may
miss,
Pout displeasure across a ring;
8ut.... the day of her pride is early yet—
The man may tire, the King forget.
Oh. the King’s sweetheart rides to the chase,
And the Queen pretends that she does not
see
The royal falcon upon her wrist
And the jewelled hand that a King has
kist,
And the broidered pommel against her knee.
But... .the world remembers an ungilt sin,
The tide may turn and the wife may win.
Oh, the King’s sweetheart has wmtched the
day
Break right rosy on lake and dell,
Has heard birds twitter among the trees,
And breathed the honey of blossomed
breeze
And of heather from the fell.
But. . . .she is cold to her feet of white,
For the King came not to her door last
night.
Perhaps he wearied and slept full long,
Perhaps lie toiled at affairs of State,
Some priest has held him in serious talk,
So there was no step in the secret walk
And no light hand on the gate.
She hid is her pearls and she counts her
rings,
For men are fickle, and so are kings.
Oh, the King’s sweetheart has fed full well,
She has lain softly in satin bed,
Her hair is bright as a wild bird’s wing,
And I think ’tis true that she loved the
King
King’s love is man’s love, when all is said—
She may yet be sold for a beggar’s fee;
There are varying prices for such as she!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
In Southland.
A sun-flecked road where dusty grey sheep
pass,
A fleecy cloud flung on a dome of blue,
A white-frocked girl deep in the meadow
grass,
Grey distance where the violet hills gleam
through.
An errant ladybird with stalwart heart
Creeps to your hand, and while you chant
the spell
That bids her fly, your soft lips smile apart,
Belying all the horrors that they tell.
Fly home, O Ladybird ! And I straightway
Dream of a brown house set upon a hill,
Above a river yellow with dredged clay.
Brown curtains billow o’er the window-sill.
The beeswaxed floors are mellow in the light
Of yellow beams. A round bowl flecked
with gold
Brims o’er with petals time has creamed
from white.
Tall amber vases fragrant wallflowers
hold.
From room to room you pass, your sun-
kissed hands
Tending the treasures we have gathered
there
From furthest corners of the olden lands ;
A high shell-comb gleams in your red-
brown hair.
This was the home of dreams, O golden girl,
A picture in faint shades of yellow
browns;
Can all his gold buy you a richer pearl
In the heaped treasure houses of the
towns ?
We might have wed. Love, like a butterfly
On trembling wings, expectant of the spark
Flint strikes from steel, circled and fluttered
nigh,
But found not warmth nor light, so fled
the dark.
MAORILAND.
JOHN EGAN.
Carnival.
The glittering street is strewn with gold
And hung with quivering fold on fold
Of frosted silver. Volleying comes
The deafening lilt of little drums,
Low, liquid voices laughing, calling,
Rustle of pelted favors falling,
The keen, clear clash of slender lances
Thickening as the masque advances,
And the shrill treble of delight
That tatters all the gloom of night.
Above, the heavy banners droop
With jewelled fringe ; and loop on loop
The long, thin, glinting trails of fire
Kindle the moisture-beaded wire,
And wreathe about with blurred festoons
The lights that stare like monstrous moons.
The wind is dancing, lissom, fleet,
And swift on music-maddened feet
The joyous raindrops leap and spring,
The drunken gutters lurch and sing.
While, skirting all the sparkling throng,
Houses and shops, a shrouded row
In sable mask and domino.
With sombre brooding in their eyes,
Watch the gay tide of folly flow.
VICTORIA.
SYD C.

THURSDAY, MAY 11, 1916.
THE ENEMY WITHIN.
TiiE attempt to inspire confidence in the
Australian public by blindfolding it, turning
out the lights, silencing all the voices of the
outer world and then assuring it that every-'
thing is going well has not been a success.
From the first shriek of the guns to the
present moment the war has been one long
series of disillusionments, and those disillu-
sionments have not been due to the tragic
happenings in Europe or to any failure of
the Allied arms, but to the censorship.
Australians are not nervous folk walking in
daily fear of the terror that lurks in the
dark. Our open-air life, our joy in the sun
and our simple outlook upon life have given
us a preference for truth. If the doctor says
that the case is hopeless we want to know
it, to know in just what way it is hopeless
or where the last obscure chance lies. We
are not afraid of the dark or of the ghost
that walks in the moonlight or the devil
that gets up early to catch the soul-worm as
it turns. All we ask in time of crisis is that
we may be told the worst; and we are pre-
pared to guarantee that, facing it, our
mouths will not lose their cheerful grin. The
censorship, which during the present war
has kept us in blank ignorance of the real
happenings, has been imposed by men who
were utterly incapable of understanding the
Australian character and temper, and is in the
nature of an insult to the whole nation. At
the outset, it may be admitted, Australia
was untried ; and there was in the minds of
our own politicians, who for the most part
are men of such narrow outlook that they
fail to understand the people they profess
to represent, a fear that we might not rise
to the heights of the supreme test. But
since then the people of this continent have
proved themselves. The same spirit that
animated the men who rushed the blood-
stained heights of Gaba Tepe in the full
knowledge of the pain and death which
awaited them, has been shown by the men
and women who read the death lists and were
only strengthened in their resolve when the
evacuation of Gallipoli proved the incapacity
and folly of the statesmen who had planned
the enterprise which wounded Australia
sorely for so little gain. If Australia had
not won the right to be trusted at the start,
there can be no question about her right to
knowledge now; yet the censorship has
become stricter and the obscurity greater as
the months have gone on.
At the, beginning, when Germany had
still some show of strength in the
seas surrounding Australia, there may
have been military expediency in suppressing
certain information which might have helped
the enemy; but if the whole course of the
censorship be examined, it will be found that
the matters thus denied publication for
definite and understandable reasons were of
such small consequence that the total
amounts to nothing in the general esti-
mate ; and the knowledge of these
things by every person in the Common
wealth would be of no advantage what-
ever to the enemy. But the real trouble
does not lie in the passing irritation of a line
removed from the Comet which is allowed to
appear in the Mercury ; it lies rather in
the great fear that things which are essen-
tial to the public good are hidden under a
mere pretence of military necessity.
It is not allowable to mention the fact
that the censor has prohibited the publica-
tion of any information, or even to hint that
in any particular instance the censorship
has been exercised ; and as a result this mat-
ter is not kept before the public. The
people vaguely know that the whole truth
about the war seldom if ever reaches them,
but imagine they get a sufficient summary
to enable them to gauge something of the
position. A comparison of dates when things
happen with dates when the partial informa-
tion, grudgingly given, comes along, would
go some way tow-ards enlightening them as
to the true position. More than this, tlm
nature of war is such that nothing which
jiappens in any country engaged in ffie
struggle is wholly without relation to mlE-
rarv arrairs. Aot abound of chons is soH
in Sydney. not a suit ■9t.i M ics ordered.
fro nr" a tailor in .Melbourne, .without having
some.-remote bearing on the final issue of.
the strife or the details of local preparation
to take part in it As anything mav hg
pressed into militaTv service or use, so any-
iirmg mav bv the elasticity of war-nreca'y-
tiyn powers be; brought-under the military
The net result is that a power
of “complete dictatorship is placed in the
hands of a single individual. The freedom
of the press is gone, the mouth of criticism
is shut, and the whole democratic power of
the people ceases when the Minister for
Defence issues his ukase. It is a position
which many a tyrant in the world’s history
would have been glad to enjoy. Had W. A.
Holman been blessed with the control of
such a censorship he never could have been
beaten either within the P.L.L. or without.
It looks as if national expediency, which
after all is above military expediency, must
demand the abolition of the censorship. On
all the evidence the thing has become a grave
danger to the nation. For 18 months the people
of England and Australia have been kept
in the dark. Every week or two they have
woke up to find that they have been deceived
about something or other. There is a limit
to their endurance of that. Presently, if they
were told the whole truth, they would not
believe it. They would expect the same old
disappointments, the same old disillusion-
ments. The inevitable result of all this
will be, at some time or other, a clamor for
peace on terms that will utterly discredit
us. The Censorship was a curse from the
outset of the war. It threatens now, for the
reason just mentioned, to be a terrible
calamity at the end of it. On the other
hand, as exercised, it is of no military value
to Britain and her Allies, of no value to the
people of the Commonwealth; it serves no
real military purpose; it is but a cloak to
cover the failures of the Governments.
UNDER MILITARY NECESSITY.
The sombre fact that new-laid eggs, fresh
from the suburban Wyandotte, are
quoted at 2|d. each is one which, while it
may not be cause for alarm, is still calcu-
lated to make us pause and seriously con-
sider the situation. Eggs can, of course, be
obtained at a lower rate —Chinese, which
arrived by the s.s. Empire, have been offered
as low as lfd. —but at the moment when
national economy of the food supply seems
as imperative as trousers and as essential as
cough medicine, the question arises as to how
far the inflation of prices may be due to the
machinations of the poultry-yard. On the
showing of the various egg-laying competi-
tions, the Australian hen now holds rank
with the ablest exponents of her art; and it
seems to us that the Leghorn and the Wyan-
dotte ought to be giving better assistance to
a people engaged in a great and bitter
struggle for national existence. No one seri-
ously expects the Plymouth Rock to be a
weight-carrier, or even to enlist for home
defence; but it does seem reasonable to
demand that in. the matter of custard, ome-
lette and ordinary poacliment they should
make a serious effort to maintain their effec-
tive standard at least until the close of the
war. That they are not doing so is lament-
ably evident; and conditions are further
aggravated by the unsatisfactory nature of
the bacon trade. True, bacon has its price
fixed by law at from lOd. to Is. 2d. per
pound ; but though the former might be well
within the reach of most householders it
applies only to shoulders, and no provision
has been made by the authorities for increas-
ing the number of shoulders carried bv any
pig in response to the popular demand/
*.* * *
From such disquieting considerations the
military authorities may deem it still per-
missible for us to turn to the stronger and
more vigorous position of onions. These are
reported to be held in large quantities, and so
far, in their relation to the food campaign,
the situation may be regarded without
alarm ; but there is something ominous about
the further market statement that they are
quiet. A quiet onion may or may not be a
menace to the community. When Leonidas
and his splendid 300 stood combing their hair
in front of the mighty array of Persian
spears, their noble manhood was nurtured
upon black bread and onions. There is not
the smallest suggestion in the works of any
ancient writer that those onions were quiet.
On the contrary, there is every reason to
believe that they were the same vociferous
herbs which for ages have breathed defiance
to the foe in the forefront of the armies of
Southern. Europe. AN e trust that we shall
not be regarded as giving away a hint that
may help the enemy when we say that onions
and their strenuous relative garlic have
played a tremendous part in human history.
Possibly they sighed in the pipes of Pan and
edged the death-song of Sappho ; it is abso-
lutely certain that they went before the
marching hosts of Alexander and had a part
in the shouts of victory which heralded the
advance of Hannibal upon Rome. r In these
dark days when the nation, beginning to real-
ise all that is at' stake, saves a tramfare or
the price of a drink to purchase tickets for a
picture-show r , the quietude of onions may be
worth more than a passing thought. Silence
may be as dangerous as the speaking of open
treason; and patriotic Australians would be
well advised to watch for developments in the
onion market before it is too late.
With potatoes we may take our chance.
*lt is true that the peelings are now almost
as valuable as the complete tubers in some
previous years; but while Tasmanian red-
skins stand at £lO 10s. and Bismarks are only
worth £B, there can be no reason to despair.
The state of the roads is quite a different
matter. In all parts of Australia, but par-
ticularly the neighborhood of Sydney, the
road difficulty is a very serious one. NYhile
it may be that the railways really constitute
the arterial system of the country, it must be
admitted that the roads are its veins and
capillary vessels. Along these throbs the
slowly-toiling vehicle of the Chinese cabbage
merchant; and if the dust-hidden ruts are
deep there is a shortage of green food which
may prejudice the public health. No sophistry
can disguise the fact that without metal-
blue metal for preference —a road is pot
a road at all, but merely a carica-
ture of an elongated dust-heap. The
demands of public safety have in some
advanced countries such as Turkey and
Madagascar practically abolished roads, on
the ground that their*upkeep is costly and
that they too readily furnish ammunition for
the less-orderly elements in the community ;
but such considerations can have no weight,
in a land like Australia, where freedom of
thought links itself with the settled desire
for religious and municipal reform. The
religious aspects of roadmaking have been too
much overlooked in the past; but with the
return of earnestness, which the war now
raging has promoted, there seems no reason
why the ancient spirit which built the great
Gothic cathedrals of Europe should not mani-
fest itself even in the architecture of the
narrow lane along which the bacon gets its
first start to market or the onions receive
their first impulse towmrds the commissariat.
But all these things are of very small
consequence in comparison with the health
of the people; and when'it is remembered
that the injurious nature of tram and rail-
way travelling predisposes the public to
over-indulgence in turpentine as a remedy
for those rheumatic ailments which remain
uncured even after a long course of hot sea-
bathing, the real character of the thing that
is eating as a canker at the heart of society
is readily seen. Where there is no rest
there can be no leisure. Where there is no
leisure there can be little thought for the day
after to-morrow. The Australian Billjim
knows this—knows it and rightly fears it.
The case of bacon is not unique any more
than the case of onions is representative.
In the United States of America egg-produc-
tion and poultry-rearing is the greatest in-
dustry, entirely eclipsing cotton, tobacco,
steel or any of the other important branches
of national endeavor which succeed in keep-
ing the dollar in circulation. With the war
cloud dark above us and the echo of the
Europeon gun-fire rolling—under instruc-
tions from the Official Press Bureau- —
through our breakfast newspaper, it is well
to think of these The war may
be won by the gallantry of our troops, by
the skill and energy of our censors, by the
fine leadership of men whose brains seethe
and sizzle with acute ability in the midst
of the storm and stress;. but there are con-
tributing causes to which only a deaf man
could be entirely blind. All other things
being exactly equal, it is not, we sincerely
trust, going beyond the limit of Kitchener’s
instructions to say that the war may be won
by the nation with the most laying hens.
The poultry-keeper knows; and ‘what the
poultry-keeper knows to-day the Kaiser may
discover some wild morning.
THE MISERIES OF A SMART YOUNG
MAN.
“Quill,” for the defence :
Not long ago Lloyd George was chased with dead
cats and over-ripe hen-fruit whenever he showed his
nose in England. He may be chased again. Holman
was raised up on the shoulders of the multitude in
N. S. Wales and acclaimed as a Daniel come to judg-
ment. Now that he has been cast forth into the ash-
barrel and dragged back by the heels shamed and
repentant of his bluffs, there are few who do not
recognise his follies and his failures and his painful
limitations. Still, there is a case for Holman as
against the mob who rubbed his nose in the dust; not
for Holman the man, who has played the only game
he knew, and played it badly; but for Holman the
politician, thrown into place of power and patronage
by the machine he had helped to fashion— a machine
on which many other men in the Commonwealth
depend for their precarious foothold in public life.
Holman the Premier has failed because he was put
into a place where only failure is possible.
Banana land has yet to be heard from. But take
N. S. Wales and Westralia— Holman and Scaddan. In
both States the history of the last few years has been
practically the same—deplorable to the point of
tragedy. Or take the Commonwealth and see w r hat is
happening there. The political philosopher, the ideal-
ist, even the time-server and heeler, may find much
to hold his attention. And the fundamental cause of
what has happened and is happening and will happen
in the future is that the Labor machine, effective in
an extraordinary degree as an instrument for getting
members into Parliament, is ineffective for anything
else—even for keeping them there. The victory
gained is purely mechanical. The Parliament pro-
duced by the activities, sordid and otherwise, of par-
tisan leagues and associations does not represent the
people. In the ease of Labor the represented ■
not of the political beliefs and desires Tfth ,S
and women who toil, but of those who u
the organisation. The result is an unrepresented
deliberative body, and, in consequence of the 1?
system and its offshoot the Caucus, a body in m
public policy is to the end determined by . •
ority. Examination of polling figures shows thatT
strength of parties in Parliament under the Dr J„!
system is often a gross distortion of their stress
in the constituencies; that practically half the 1
tors have no representation at all, their votes havi
been ineffective; that a majority of a party
representing little more than a fcf.rth of Parli ’
ment, may, and does, decide the character of Wi !'
tion and control the cashbox. Any movement Z
schpme or system which aims at turning Parliam [
into an institution inhabited by delemteo ,
leagues and coteries of Libs, or Labs. Tl JZ
else, instead of maintaining it on the firm basis of
popular representation, is certain to come a crooner
It may have temporary successes of the more squalid
kind. It may secure reforms and other things that
the people approve. It can never be anything but
opportunist, cowardly and uncertain of its ground.
That is where Holman fell in. As a critical
suggestive body organised Labor had filled an im-
portant place in the field of politics. Called on to
administer and construct, it found itself gravelled
after the first flush of zeal had vanished. Holm ax
discovered himself with Parliamentary followers
whose souls were in pawn to leagues and committees
He was not even his own man. Always the wires
were there from the floor of the Assembly to the
Caucus room ; from the Caucus they radiated away
to the Trades Hall, tihe P.L.L. headquarters and
hindquarters and elsewhere. The platform was there
to be carried out, but the swift march of events
from day to day raised new questions. Tactical con-
siderations having relation to the effect of this or
that step became paramount. Ministers and mem-
bers, pledged body and bones to those outside who
held power of life and death over them,' were con-
cerned, not with the great problems affecting the
mass of men and women and the industrial and
economic fabric of the State, but with placating
special interests and keeping executives and com-
mittees “sweet.”
* * * *
Into this welter of dodge and doubt the sprightly
Holman found himself floundering like a ship dis-
mantled and leaking in a gale. Not one man in
10,000 would have done any better. A stronger
nature —a man of old Dick Seddon's fibre, say-
might have grasped the skirts of happy chance and
established a mastery over his environment. But
even then the claim to act for and on behalf of the
democracy would have been as great a jest as
Holman’s has been. That he was not of that type
is to his discredit no more than that he was weak
and vacillating is to his credit. What is to Hol-
man’s shame is that when he realised the impossi-
bility' of his position and saw that he could only
cling to it by devices which meant the betrayal not
only of his adherents but of the people, he did not
stay his hand. But to gird at him for that and offer
him up as a baked sacrifice on the altar of Indignation
is not rightfully in the mouth of those Laborites who
encouraged him in his offending, and are even now
trying to perpetuate a system which must inevitably
lead to the same weary old mess. Holman side-
stepped and did silly things because he was not
allowed to behave wisely. The P.L.L. gave its sanc-
tion to the monstrous Norton Griffiths agreement.
The P.L.L. tolerated his frantic borrowing exploits
and told him to keep on doing it. An organisation
that did these things was well served by Holman.
That is his defence. If he hadn't boomed and bloated
and pawned and squandered, the rest of the stuff he
had to offer would not have satisfied either Caucus
or the executive or the populace. Therefore he has
boomed and bloated and tried to buy his way through
until the scandal of it smelt to Heaven, and another
crowd pushed in and seized the f’.L.L. on general
principles and in half a day got cold feet and
patched up the quarrel—but always with the old
intention of a Parliament nominally subservient to
the P.L.L. Poor Holman ! But what price the
unhappy wretch who follows him ?
ON BEING PROUD OF YOUR JOB.
Out of the mists of the past comes a tradi-
tion that there was once a country known as
“Merry England.” People who read British
comic newspapers find this difficult to believe,
and others w T ho go further and study the
debates of the House of Commons scout it as
a lie ; but there is a persistency about the
belief, and when you have followed it
back the outlines of the place that
w r as given up to merriment become
clearer, until the ancient land is with
some accuracy re-constructed. On all
appearances, the gaiety of the nation reached
its height about the time of Edward III.;
and the first and outstanding feature of that
king’s reign was the war on the Continent of
Europe. \\ ar in itself is not usually a
subject for mirth ; but the conduct of that
war is the very best proof that there really
was merriment in the hearts of the people.
In the first big battle less than 20.000 Eng-
lishmen completely smashed up and routed a
better-equipped force at least three times as
numerous, and in another outstanding piece
of carnage a still smaller English army,
taken unawares and driven into a corner,
fought at odds of one to ten and made mince-
meat of its foes. The merriment of these
proceedings lay chiefly in the fact that the
war-game could be taken so lightly and with
such easy confidence. The English forces
which invaded Europe were simply picked
fighting teams. England had in the previous
reign raised an army of 100.000 for the inva-
sion of Scotland ; and, as every man was at
need a soldier, a very big mob of fighters could
have been put in the field. But the King
didn’t worry about vast hosts, preferring to
think that fewer men meant greater glory;
and considering his small loss in the big
engagements his calculations were sound
enough. However, the King was only a small
item on the national bill, and his wars are
only the decorative part of the history of the
time. What is interesting about the fight-
ing, and of permanent value to anybody who
■wants to know r how r 12 and 12 make 24, is
the source of that tremendous strength which
could afford to put out a careless left hand
and smash the best-looking military Powers
beyond the Dover Straits. The secret
appears to be that England w T as then a work-
ing nation taking pride in its job.
The pride of the job is really the founda-
tion of solid nationality—whether the job
be rushing a trench or grinding flour. A
good deal of so-called modern progress, w’hich


measures itself by output and cash returns, is
no real progress at all, for the reason that
it lessens the human factor in achievements.
In most departments of craft handwork is
still superior to that turned out by compli-
cated machinery ; and while it would be idle
to argue in a general way against the
machine, it is just as big a mistake to regard
it as the be-all and end-all of effort. Thus,
in the matter of a man’s clothing. The swift
spinning and weaving of the factory and the
rapid manipulation of the cloth with mechani-
cal aids enables the average person to buy
one or two suits of moderately decent-looking
fit and style in the course of a year. But
in the days when all such work was done by
hand, when the thread was spun on the house-
hold wheel and the loom clicked in the private
workshop, when the tailor put in every
stitch with craft pride and loving skill, a
suit of clothes would last the wearer many
years. The gain to the public has not been
so great that it altogether outweighs the loss
to the individual craftsman. That old
weaver understood his loom and his thread in
an intimate way, and his finished product
was full of character. llis work was his
life, and half his pleasure in existence was
drawn from it. He did not worry greatly
over shorter hours, though, as a matter of
fact, he could take holidays when he pleased
and had an immense capacity for enjoyment.
In the pride of his job he was his own man
and held his head high among his fellows.
To-day the factory grinds out cloth at express
speed, but the new looms represent the minds
of mechanics who have no direct connection
with their working. The person who stands
wearily by the machine has lost all joy of
craft;' and the pride has shifted from the
many workers to the few employers in the
company’s board room who see the finished
product of the factory in terms of f s. d.
As things are, we can’t well get away from
the machine ; but a saner industrialism may
yet be devised which will give the craft
workers their own souls back again.
But the pride of the job has nowhere been
so completely lost and swallowed up by the
machine as in politics. In the State of
N. S. Wales, where the wheels of the Caucus
grind the Ministry small, and the wheels of
the Labor Conference turn both, and a so-
called industrial clique within the conference
has discovered a crank by which it can
operate the lot, the mere free elector feels
himself a pretty small proposition. Theoreti-
cally, he is the master of all; in practice, he
gets a say once in three years, and his choice
is limited for him by other outlying pinions
and wheels of the complicated machinery,
until his say is of the smallest kind possible.
Real democracy doesn’t want a machine at
all and doesn’t need it. The elector must
become his own legislator and recover the
pride of his job if. the crooked places are to
be made straight. A great deal is still talked
about the man with special abilities as a
politician and Parliamentarian ; and people,
otherwise sane, who call themselves demo-
crats, cling to the old shibboleths of aristo-
cratic or autocratic government which should
have no place in modern politics. Govern-
ment by the ablest man or the most pushing
may be quite successful, and often has been ;
but it is not democratic government and has
no right to masquerade as such. Direct gov-
ernment by the people can be secured without
any very tremendous revolution by simply
putting into use an infinitely saner and
simpler system than that now employed.
Let Ministries be elective as Parliaments are ;
and let the electors have the right to recall
any member either of the Ministry or of
Parliament who does not do his work. Let
the Referendum with Initiative be placed on
the statute book ; and the whole thing is done.
Then party dies and every man becomes his
own politician, thinking out the problems of
State, and giving effect to his judgments by
petition and vote. In this way alone can
we recover the pride of our job as freemen
and nation-makers. Certainly, it would still
be possible to make a mess of things ; but the
democratic mess could be democratically
cleaned up, and at its worst it could scarcely
have the evil smell of our present machine-
made muddle.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Brown Sword and a Silver Dream.
“D.M.W.’s” Irish rhapsody :
Under the Sinn Fein banner they filled the city
with blood and died the death of the trtaitor facing
the judicial rifles which bitterly discriminate
between the enthusiast who takes risks and the dul-
lard who allows risks to be taken. So another pitiful
chapter is added to the history of Ireland, and there
are more tears and graves with no good done.
Rebellion in Ireland is a crime against the soul of
Ireland. It is not by the sword that the conquest
of the Gael is to be wrought. That fact was dis-
covered nearly 2000 years ago by leaders of the race
as enthusiastic as any Sinn Feiner of them all.
There w'as a time when that Gaelic sword —a brown
one, by the way, if the tradition of Fingal’s mighty
“Luno” has any force —was absolutely the strongest
thing in Europe. It waved round the Adriatic and
the Aegean as well as in the British Islands, but it
W’as the same Irish article and the man behind it
had the same features of mind. About 363 b.c. that
sword smashed down the defences of Rome; a little
more than a century later it swept through Greece,
and, taking the Dardanelles in its flash, established
itself in Asia Minor. Four centuries or so later two
lines of walled fortifications and the whole of the
Roman legions in Britain could not keep the same
weapon from laying London waste. On all the evi-
dence, the Gaelic sword might have mastered all
Europe and snbdued it to its owner’s will; but that
owner at some remote period definitely decided that
it was not worth while. For the Gaelic mind was
more than the Gaelic fist; and the dream and song
that were the first elements in the make-up of the
people prevailed for the breaking of the sword.
Then the Teuton arose and played the deuce with
the Roman Empire and with Europe generally;
and while the age-long throat-cutting was in progress
the Gaelic dream and song faded away, and nobody
in the riot area, where Norman and Frank and
Dane wrestled for supremacy, knew anything about
these things for which the old power of the sword
had been exchanged. Within the last 20 years or
so has developed what is called the Celtic revival.
The thought of the Gael—mostly of the Gael in
Ireland—has seized hold upon the universal mind
until almost unconsciously all the world has been
sweetened and softened with Irish thought. There
have, indeed, of recent years been two intellectual
forces in Europe, and two only—the Irish and the
German. The first stood for the fine, keen uplift of
the soul, the appreciation of the mystery of life
and the wonder of the elemental world ; the second
for the definite order and sequence of a machine-
made universe, in which man, the individual, was
but the eternal sacrifice to rigid and arbitrary law.
The Irish idea intellectually enthroned a fairy
whisper ; the German idea exalted a scientific theory.
The one pointed the road to a sweeter and gladder
world-peace; the other found its natural goal in
world-war. And the Irish thought was, and is,
winning in spite of the thunder of all the Kaiser’s
guns. Then—the pity of it! —some of Ireland’s
intellectual leaders, mistaking wholly the make and
color of things, snatched up the foolish Teutonic
sword and sought to drown in bitter blood a nation’s
dream that was soon to be a world’s noblest heri-
tage. They did not succeed, for their dream was
greater than an hour of madness ; but the mingled
sorrow and pity and shame of it all grips at our
"hearts the world over, and for the moment blinds us
to th t fe. fact that in the peace-to-come the broken
sword of Teuton force will be the first landmark on
the road of a greater Gaelic conquest. Dublin will
be re-built, and the finer thought-harmonies that
bullets cannot break will be heard again.
The Need for an Intelligent Pessimism.
From an Englishman who had no misgivings
about the result of the war when he first
talked of it: —
We are all at fault for the present state of things.
Who shall portion out the blame? At the commence-
ment of war we had never made a complete aero-
plane; we had not enough cannon works to keep pace
with the growth of even our fleet; our military ord-
nance was about equal to that of Servia, but probably
inferior in quality; and our trained artillerymen were
a much smaller proportion of our forces (small as
they were ) than those of any other European country.
Now, you cannot make cannon foundries and the
skilled mechanics to run them as the Creator made
light, however rich you may be. Nor can you make
expert artillerymen in six months. The Germans
require six years. The fact is that the wily Teuton
has discovered that his one immense advantage lay in
his artillery (not to mention his gas trick), and has
pushed it, and is pushing it, for all it is worth.
Had we possessed the artillery and ammunition at
first we should have easily won Gallipoli. It is guns,
guns, guns we want. Let it be a lesson to you Aus-
tralians, who talk only of the men required for beat-
ing the enemy. I suspect that the Central European
Powers are still making artillery, etc., at least as
fast as the Entente Powers. The attack on Verdun
now going on illustrates my assertion that artillery
is the main factor in this warfare. The Germans
advance only by pulverising everything in front of
them with shells. I take no interest in the blockade
question. Napoleon, having practically command of
the whole coast of Europe, was unable to prevent
even his own subjects from trading with England.
The blockade in the American war was a confessed
failure except that it raised the price of contraband
in the Southern States. Well, we have raised the
price of everything imported into Germany at least 50
per cent., and, on ah average, at least 75 per cent.
That’s all.
It cannot be too strongly insisted that Ger-
man artillery and our own cocksureness —
the result mostly of misinformation —are the
principal powers which the Allies are up
against, and which have not yet begun to
be subdued; and the most unpatriotic cry
that can be raised is the cry of the unreason-
able optimist who expects the Germans to
throw up the sponge this year or next year.
The real patriot is the sturdy pessimist who
reckons that although the British Empire
would be surely damned for its sins of omis-
sion if the war were bound to terminate
this year or next, there will yet be time for
it to work out its salvation by learning the
lesson of the past 21 months. The fool
optimist expects Britain to “muddle
through” as of old. The patriotic pessimist
perceives that to “muddle through” is an
impossibility this time. An undreamt-of
number of cannons and other pieces of hard-
ware must be made, and millions of intel-
ligent soldiers must be educated. The war
so systematically begun and waged by the
Huns can only be won by the Allies by being
equally systematic. Muddling is certain mur-
der for the muddled. The thing has got to be
done properly if it is to be done at all.
Australia cannot afford to wait any longer
before starting to do her bit properly. She
must dread the worst; and then and not till
then w r ill she strive her best.

Our Sinful Waste of Men.
A good Australian s so-long to Egypt:—
Men, many men, are still wanted; but they are
getting scarcer than they were 18 months ago when
I left. Therefore it is up to the able-bodied men
who have enlisted to do able-bodied men’s work.
Unhappily such is not the case in Egypt, at any rate.
The various Base Departments of the A.I.F. are over-
flowing with effectives who have never vet seen the
Front, possibly don’t want to, and probably never
will.
These Base Departments provide a large amount
of light work, which could ea>ily be done by men
who have not been able to pass the doctor on account
of some slight defect, or b3 r wounded men unfit for
further active service. As the staffs of the various
departments run football and other teams, it is quite
feasible that they could run rifle and bayonet squads
also, and do a bit of campaigning. These remarks
apply also to hospital orderlies. Nothing was finer
than the work of the A.M.O. at Gallipoli, and all of
us have the greatest admiration and respect for
them. But I know other men who have, got their
stripes in the Citizen Forces, and are simply loafing
as orderlies in base hospitals.
The Commonwealth has sent over 150,000 men to
fhe Front, all able-bodied. But the number of
“effective rifles” which the A.I.F. numbers at present
is a lot less than most people imagine, and it is
the “effective rifles” that constitute the real striking
force in any army.
What is wanted is more business management on
the job. A man running a commercial concern
employs as many “effectives” (i.e., workmen) as
possible, gnd cuts down his ineffectives {i.e., staff)
to the minimum ; and, after all, the army is only a
large business. When a, man is passed as “fit for
active service” let him be put to active service, and
let the various Base jobs, as far as possible, be filled
by those who are nut fit. When a tired Tommy
Kangaroo, who has been through the Gallipoli cam-
paign, gets 24 hours’ leave and visits Cairo and sees
the various ways of “dodging the Front” that are
practised, it is apt to damp his enthusiasm a bit,
and he begins to wonder if it’s a mug he is, or a
hero.
It is about time Parliament did meet. And
if they do their duty will no longer
hesitate to expose gross abuses and grosser
incompetence. The time has passed for
stalling off criticism with the old, “Sh ! You
mustn’t let Germany know.” The only thing
that counts in this light is the blow; and
if we are so training that we carn’t hit hard,
what does it matter what Germany knows ?
Germany will be quite happy so long as
Australia, doesn't know.

The Laborer among the Penitential
Pumpkins.
The recent Agricultural Show in Sydney
was decorated with a collection of pumpkins
and other vegetation grown on prison farms,
and much wonder was expressed by various
pers ns that individuals convicted of crime
should turn out the giant carrot and exten-
sive cabbage. Yet the point of the exhibit
was not in any revelation on this score, but
in the substantial evidence thus afforded of
a reasonably enlightened gaol policy. Never-
theless, though much may be expected from
the changes that are taking place in N.S.W.
prisons, it occurs to this paper that, far too
many people of one sort are still going into
gaol and too many of another sort are being
relieved of toil on the penitential pumpkin
patch. For instance, last year, according
to the report of the Controller-General, of
7SII persons received into gaol after con-
viction at police courts 5050 were delivered
from the Black Maria because of non-pay-
ment of fines. What had happened to most
of them was that the Bench had remarked
“£2 or 14 days,” and because they were not
able to produce the £2 they had taken the
14 days. The meaning of this in most cases
is that men are gaoled not for their offence
but for their momentary poverty. It is a
wrong system altogether, and there ought
to be someone listening to _ the Controller-
General’s remonstrance. Either the beaks
will have to get out of their habit of snap-
ping “No” at nine defendants out of 10 who
ask for time to pay their fines, or the Crown
Law office will have to draft some new rules
and regulations, and the Police Department
establish a system under which gaol can be
evaded by payment of. instalments. Of
course the police do not want to be bothered
with a crowd of frowsy citizens who owe
the State fines. They are only concerned
about getting rid of them. But that is no
reason for not giving people a fair oppor-
tunity to find the cash. And if the crush
at the gate could be reduced by' a change
of method regarding fines, there looks to be
good reason for further considering the in-
dividual who is several times convicted of
serious crime and develops into an in-and-
outer. Last year of 736 individuals con-
victed in the higher courts 245 were making
anything from a fourth to a twentieth
appearance. Whether encouraging the
growth of pumpkins is going to reduce the
number of these “repeaters” is a mystery
that will take a long time to clear up. That
a man who is liberated after three convic-
tions will come back to help with the pump-
kins is a reasonably safe assumption. Does
society gain anything by continuing to lib-
erate the man of proved criminal instinct?
The Bulletin is certain that society does
not and that habitual criminality should be
a passport to a steady job among the
pumpkins. In 10 years 64 individuals have
been judicially declared to be habituals.
About half of them have been liberated.
The law on this subject wants overhauling.
Last year scores of habitual criminals passed
through the courts without getting the brand
and scores were liberated from the gaols.
There is no protection for the community
in this. Gaols are for criminals and they
should be kept there. Mere offenders who
haven’t a couple of sovereigns should be
kept out as much as possible.
The Treaty Rights of Bung.
Bung in N. S. Wales appears to be a
generous person to his own hurt. The six
o’clock closing proposal pushes the war hard
against his pocket, and he is opening Ins
defence campaign with a compromise. A
couple of years ago the voters of the State
by an overwhelming majority decided that
liquor was still to be sold under the old
conditions for the next three years. Those
old conditions meant keeping the bars open
until 11 o’clock. Then the war came along
and the no-liquor party got in some fine
work. Because trouble arose in the mili-
tary camps through the absence of the wet
canteen, a loud cry went up to close the
pubs early in the evening, and the Cold Tea
faction saw a chance of beating the refer-
endum which had gone against it. Bung
had a perfect right to say fhat, if the ques-
tion was to be re-opened at all within the
three years, his supporters should have a
right to vote for extended hours. But Bung
did nothing of the kind. While his enemies
are pressing every point against him he
voluntarily offers to give up two hours of
his selling time, and asks his supporters to
vote for nine o’clock. In the light of the
fact that the military authorities can fix
their own hours for hotel closing, irrespec-
tive of the vote of the State, it is probable
that in a straight-out fight between Bung
and Cold Tea the man behind the beer-
pump would carry the day as before; but
the six o’clock issue complicates things. A
very large number of trade unionists and
others have got the idea firmly intp their
minds that all work should stop at or about
six in the interests of the workers; and
these people, adding their votes to those of
the visionaries who declare that beer is the
root of all evil, may turn the tables against
the beermonger. The idea of a universal
rest after sunset doesn’t seem to have much
in it. If the pubs have to shut at that time
of day on a question that has no relation to
liquor, why not close the theatres also?
Why not stop the night trams and trains
and printing presses and crayfish? Bung
may be lighter or darker than he is painted,
but there ought to be some fair play about
his treatment. In December, 1913, the
electors said quite emphatically: “Go on
and make your arrangements for another
three years. We’re satisfied.” The three
years are not up; and neither the labor
question nor Wilhelm of Prussia should be
allowed to walk through an agreement. We
are getting too much of that sort of thing
altogether. If the beer supply is really
interfering with . the war, that is another
matter; but it is a matter for the Federal
military authorities, who have all the power
they want without any referendum.

“Stars and Crowns” : Apropos the
announcement of the Defence Department
that “it is not thought desirable that men
who go away to fight for their country, and
who win commissions or promotions in
actual warfare, should altogether lose their
rank on return to Australia” (B. 20/4/’16).
As The Bulletin says, the men who stay
behind are to keep their ranks; and only if
these warriors who don't go to the war should
drop out will the men who have been away
get a look-in. Meantime the latter can have
“honorary” rank. But the position is even
worse than that. Not only will returned
officers be permitted to retain merely
honorary rank when (and if) they
do return, but in many cases they
will find themselves superseded in seniority
by officers who have remained here,
and who have collected substantive and
brevet ranks during the absence of
the other fellow on service. For example,
here are two, say, majors. One is either
kept at home or pulls strings and stays at
home, generally in a comfortable, very well
paid and perfectly safe job, and he gets
promoted to, say, Colonel. When the war is
over he remains a colonel and keeps his
colonel’s job. The other goes off, does his
bit, and by hard work at the Front gets
promoted to the same rank. On his return
he is merely an honorary colonel, and, if
there isn’t a colonel’s job for both, the fellow
who stays at home gets it, and the man who
has won his promotion on active service
either stands aside or takes his old major’s
job. This perhaps doesn’t matter much if
a man returns to civil imploy ; but it stings
the professional soldier. The order is simply
another illustration of the fact that Pearce
is under the dominatRm of the stay-at-home
soldier, who sees that the juiciest cuts go to
his own plate.
“Hambidge” takes part in a joy ride: —
Through bunting and cheers the recruiting train
drew out of Adelaide bearing the Premier, various
other politicians, business men and returned soldiers.
At Strathalbyn the station was decorated, a large
crowd greeted the visitors enthusiastically and part
of the train’s cargo delivered stirring addresses,
enhanced by a military band. Much cheering and
flag-waving. No recruits.
At Victor Harbor the station was joyfully dressed,
and a larger and more enthusiastic crowd greeted
the recruiters. The Mayor, councillors, torchlight
procession and a returned major (good luck to him!)
managed to raise two recruits.
Nairne —No recruits.
Oallington—No recruits.
The average Billjirr doesn’t like a fuss, so he goes
down to town quietly and enlists there. Goolwa
told the jaunters that 75 men had already enlisted
out of a population of 600, and then packed up its
flags and went home.
At Port Elliot a procession of school-children,
several M’s.P. and a homing soldier (South Africa
and Gallipoli) painted such vivid pictures to the
crowd that it delivered itself of two (2) recruits.
The train is still crawling over the landscape
picking up strays. Meanwhile, at Adelaide, 390
men offered their services during the week, and
most of ’em came from the country. I don’t know
what the picnic is going to cost. Salaries, expenses
and a train of 12 compartments will boost the bill
up.
As a setting for it all, people who think they are
Socialists are expressing their undying determination to
fight against conscription ; Kut has fallen ; Lowestoft
has been bombarded ; out in Flanders men are sticking
to it in the face of overwhelming odds; a British
flagship has been sunk; and {put this in big type )
a trawler has rammed and sunk a German sub-
marine in the North Sea. (Cheers.)
God save the King ! Comanavanother !
In the Present Crisis.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
The world is black. The bitter strife goes
wrong,
Yet at the barrier edged with death we
stand.
Where others tremble, we, erect and strong,
Thrill to the lilt of that full marching song
That is the native music of our land.
We cannot shudder in this evil hour.
The world with our fresh blood is warm and
wet;
Above our graves the wild spring blossoms
flower;
But death and pain combined have not the
power
To fill our souls with flinching and regret.
Our purpose holds through all the dark of
days
Edged with no light of silver cloud or star.
While hope grows pale and victory delays
And headlong courage its stern tribute pays,
Our level eyes, untroubled, look afar.
Somewhere we fail, as strong men failed of
old.
Somewhere we stand, unfailing, sorely prest.
The days to come are wide enough to hold
The triumph-music when the guns are cold
And the war-trampled fields of earth have
rest.
The days are long. The end is not in sight.
But hearts are more than swords at truth’s
last call;
And knee to knee in this most holy fight
We battle till clear faith has clearer sight
Of wrong’s wide ruin when the doom shall
fall.
We, who so long had watched the year’s
increase.
Laughed in the summer, played at feast and
song,
Now grasp the shafts of war that shall not
cease
Till the red harvest yield the newer peace
That dare not break through ages full and
long.
N.S.W.
D.M.W.

Things that Will Occur if They Take
Place.
The most dangerous thing to do just now is
to jump to conclusions about (1) Notes, (2)
battles, (3) anything and everything con-
nected with war. The only thing about
Notes that seems to be at all well estab-
lished is that the exchange of Correspondence
between Wilson and William is going on
till the Last Trump calls the postman off.
The latest yarn is that the German has
replied to Wilson’s “final” memo, with an
announcement of his intention to go on blow-
ing up everything that floats until the Y'anks
establish for neutrals the “right” to trade
with Hamburg, a«u that Wilson is writing
back to inquire whether this is an indication
of desire on William's part for peace. All
this merely suggests that somebody is pulling
lhe world’s leg. Of much more importance than
this letter-writing is the renewal of activity
among the armies. On the Eastern front
German and Russian and Austrian are get-
ting ready for big things, which may occur
immediately, if they don’t happen to be post-
poned. There are also reports of hurryings
to aud fro in Flanders ; and the cables inti-
mate that Australians are now in the front
trenches facing the Germans. But whether
lhe Great Offensive is to be presently deliv-
ered depends upon whether it stands ad-
journed or not. In the interval the little
there appears to be no doubt about is that
the “battle” of Verdun, which was all over a
fortnight ago, is stiff in full blast. Incident-
ally, various anonymous individuals are still
solemnly explaining how useless it would be
for the Hun to breach the line at this point
and push an army through the hole. This
can be regarded as the usual tip-top twaddle.
The German knows his job. So does the
Frenchman. Neither are fighting the most
gigantic battle known in war for the mere
sake of trying to achieve or resist futility.
Defeat of the German’s aim thereabouts
means a serious blow to him. Victory to
him would spell material advantage, mili-
tary and moral. Signs of the German weak-
ening are nowhere any more plentiful than
they were the day before yesterday, and the
Bill introduced into the British House of
Commons providing for general military ser-
vice is the best possible evidence that John
Bull has reached the conclusion that he
lias been looking for the aforesaid signs too
long, and has humbugged bis public into
doing the same thing. In Australia, how-
ever, the humbug is to proceed still further;
aud the pale young man who has all his life
been taught to curse the P.L.L and the
L'.L.C. is thanking Goo for interposing those
beautiful bodies between him and Conscrip-
tion.

When a deputation went to Black. N.S.W.
Minister for Ciiidley, Fish and other diffi-
cult problems, to ask him to throw his moral
eye over the picture-show business, one of
the speakers said that film production in
the United States was. after the steel in-
dustry, the biggest thing in that country.
The reverend assembly was very keen on
hunting the Devil out «f the flicker, but it
never seemed to strike it that the biggest
showing of his hoof was in the importing
part of the proposition. The U.S.A. is of
all countries the one which makes the biggest
parade of moral virtue; but it has a keen
scent for nastiness dished up under prudish
platitude. Australia isn’t like that. We
don’t howl about our conspicuous virtue to
any extent, but there is a wholesome clean-
ness in the thinking done in this Common-
wealth ; and our art and literature up-to-
date are remarkably free from the smirk that
bides the grimy suggestion. If these par-
sons really want to clean the picture-shows,
why don’t they advocate the protection of the
local film-making craft and so at one stroke
kill the foreign devil that deludes the young
and give a job to some decent Australians?

N. S. Wales railway figures are yelling at
the top of their voice to look out for the
smash. During the last eight years the
revenue has grown, partly through higher
charges, bufr mostly through new lines, by
61 per cent. That is, for every £lOO earned
in 1907 £l6l was earned in 1915. But for
every £lOO spent in 1907 no less than £212
had to be spent in 1915. The revenue has
to pay expenses and interest. In 1907 exes,
required £53 of every £lOO collected ; last year
exes, mopped up £69 15s. The £47 which re-
mained in 1907 after exes, were paid was
rather more than enough for the interest;
but only £3O ss. is left now, and as that
won’t pay interest the taxpayer has to do it.
If be can dodge it, then the money is bor-
rowed and the bill is passed on to the baby.
The fact that exes, have risen £ll2 while
the revenue lias risen only £6l suggests that
there is something dangerously wrong; and
Hoyle, the Minister who nominally controls
the Department, had better screw up bis
courage to say what it is before an infuri-
ated people start to smash things.

“Peg o’ My Heart” is making Sydney
Palace a real home for playgoers, and
Michael, her plain dog, seems to be suffering
as badly as ever from stage-fright.
“Mother Goose,” at Her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney. is getting along famously in her Thou-
sand and One Nights entertainment. The
humorist who animates Priscilla, the golden
egg-layer, is glad that the long summer is
done and the chill nights of autumn here at
last.
The jury empanelled nightly at Sydney
Criterion still persists in acquitting Frank
Harvey of the murder of an American mil-
lionaire against all the evidence. It is quite
time that his Honor Mr. Justice Souper
admonished them in the familiar fashion of
our own Bench.
A barrel of blither burst at Sydney Tivoli

After prolonged reflection the writer of
this paragraph came to the conclusion that
there must be a knack in turning three con-
secutive somersaults and landing on the feet
of a friend who happens to be absent-mind-
edly standing on his head. Therefore he left
his gorgeous seat in Wirths’ palatial Sydney
hippodrome the other evening and went
round to interview the Japs on the subject;
but just at that moment the whole family
leaped over the top of itself and disappeared
up its sleeve, so the yarn didn’t come off,
and the seals gave loud applause for a piece
of dead fish. Meanwhile the performance
is being repeated every night; and if anyone
wants a matinee to go on with, it is avail-
able at the ticket office.

“The Squaw Man” continues to travel
on its three virile cowboy acts at Melbourne
King’s. For another week at least the old
rooster chief will confide his dead daughter
to the care of her lawful widower, and he
will carry her off stage as the curtain drops
without pausing to ask how she died. By
the way. talking about manner of dying,
The Bulletin last week remarked that no
one troubled to examine the alleged corpse
of bad-man Hawkins when he fell on the
bar-room floor. In this show, however,
somebody does turn him over and pronounce
him to be shot plumb through the heart.
This the sheriff accepts as first and final
evidence of Mr. Hawkins’s demise, and the
body remains where it fell, neglected and
despised. An exciting citizen whilst he lasts
is Mr. Hawkins. “A short life and a loud
one” is his motto.

Most of the people appearing at Mel-
bourne Tivoli to-day will be gone the day
after to-morrow, so the banalities of the
worst offenders may pass unspecified. V
painful monotony of ragtime and weird
’Murkan accent marks the present pro-
gramme. The tricks and devices of an
alleged musical clown are sad memories,
and even the turn of Arnold and Ladd
makes one wish them back in comic opera.
Four of the items will continue until further
notice: Kverard, a smooth, cultured, light
tenor; Margaret Jewell, a timid-looking
soprano with a strain of Melba sweetness
and a suspicion of Melba tuition in her
voice; Ley wood and Norell, whose style is
a polite apology for their stuff; and Hay
and Crawford, duet dancers, of si in*, appear-
ance and very little importance. All these
and others will be in the bill next Saturday,
when Horace Goldin takes a hand. Goldin
is the most extensive illusionist in ,the
business, and his company of assistants
includes a tiger. He starts his series of
apparitions and evanishments at the matinee.
“The Ideals” are dropping out of Mel-
bourne Auditorium for a time to make room
for War Films of the French Front. These
are pictures taken for the French Govern-
ment, and are in some instances the nearest
approach to views of actual warfare that
the cinema fiend has obtained.
At Adelaide the Williamson Opera Co.'s
players rose well to tin* quick changes.
Florence Young, capable and incisive, was
in and out of the cast as wanted, but won-
derful Maggie Moore worked hard all
through. Phil Smith and Leslie Holland
were always themselves, and the public asks
nothing more. As a successor to Fred Leslie
Roberts was a failure in the crook's part in
“Miss Gibbs,” but came to his own in “Ma
Mie Rosette,” where also Derek Hudson
sang finely as the king. Of the new girls,
Ethel Cadman is dainty and effective, but
it was the jolly methods of Minnie Love that
gathered the fragrant vegetables at the
crowded farewell. Business was big all
through, and for the co. to be sent West
just as Adelaide Cup week began seems a
striking error of judgment somewhere. On
the other hand, the Cannot-Greene-Pearce
Follies at the Tivoli dropped in just at that
auspicious moment. At the King’s, Phillips
and. Merritt have revived the “stiffy” series
of Australian playlets, and Fred James
makes capital of his own mistakes as a
juggler. The rest is pictures.

IN PINK AND GOLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ragtime grates on my cultured ear,
Syncopation offends my soul.
Gloom takes hold of me when I hear
“Rag With Me” or “The Jelly Roll” ;
But the play has its pleasures yet,
Lures alike for the young and old :
What attracts you in “So Long, Let.”?
Mine the ballet in Pink and Gold !
Marie Eaton may romp and rag.
Saved by Frith from a damp-proof sea ;
Plunket may posture, Fisher gag,
Workman frivol with Dorrie B.;
Connie Ediss may still recall
Women and whine of days of old,
But one feature eclipses all —
Mine the ballet in Pink and Gold !
Looks alluring and lovely legs,
Eloquent legs that flash and twirl;
Bare-backed beauty that boldly begs
Homage due to each golden girl.
Youth feels giddy and Age grows gay,
Spoofed is Sadness and Sorrow’s sold ;
In the meadows of love make hay—
Mine the ballet in Pink and Gold !
VICTORIA.
