AT POVERTY POINT.
The Vic. Vocalists’ Society, formed a
few weeks ago for the purpose of reminding
the public that professional singers cannot
live by warbling gratuitously for War Funds,
makes a reasonable appeal to all solo con-
cert performers who can afford a ss. annual
subscription. As a matter of fact, there
are some who can’t. Confidential inquiries
have disclosed “genuine cases of destitution
amongst gifted performers.” who had no
other source of income than the fees which
“THE SQUAW HAN." AT MELBOURNE
KING’S.
E. M. Keon as Baco
(Injun interpreter)
Frank Bradley as
Sir John Applegate.
of late have been unobtainable. So the
Society’s members are doing only a fair thing
by their neediest brethren in agreeing to
refuse to sing at any function whatever for
less than a guinea ; and the social clubs,
etc., that were addicted to the bad habit of
cadging professional services have been
requested by circular to adopt the new and
reasonable principle. Municipal councils
have been asked to run concerts for the sake
of the singers they will employ, and Mel-
bourne City Council has • responded by in-
creasing its monthly output of concerts from
one to two. Also the executive is arranging
for a big concert in aid of its fund for the
relief of necessitous cases ; and Dolores has
promised to sing for the good cause.
“Harrison O.”: Portion of Judge Mur-
ray’s latest annual report on Papua deals
with the indigenous drama. “Jomburu” is
a typical farce which has been well received
by theatregoers in the Ivumusi and Mambare
Divisions, and has had a successful run of
one consecutive night. The play opens with
a woman, after a hard day’s work in the
garden, lying asleep on the broad of her
back. Enter op. &. young buck from a
neighboring village, who has been on an
unsuccessful pig-hunt. After gazing for a
while at the sleeping beauty “he takes a
draw at his bamboo pipe, closing one eye
with a quizzical expression, turns on his
heels, giving the audience to understand that
temptation has no lures for him.” He comes
back “with the air of a fixed purpose,” and
“sticking his spear in the ground near the
lady’s feet, immediately proceeds to wake her
up in a most immoral manner,” whereupon
.Alary plants her feet in the centre of the
hero’s chest and sends him crashing on to his
spear on the broad of his back. So it may
fairly be claimed that the play points a
moral.
“Statist”: A press-agent screlm to the
effect that Dorothy Brunton, at the head of
“So Long, Letty,” is the first Australian musi-
cal-comedy star to score 100 nights in one
piece in one Australian city stabs my memory.
Didn’t Carrie Aloore accomplish the feat in
Alelbourne years back in “Florodora,” with
a cast that included AAhillace Brownlow,
Hugh Ward and Grace Palotta?
“Moana”: Prohibiting peanuts in picture
palaces was a small step alongside the stride
recently taken in Hastings (ALL.), where
the flicker showmen decided to prohibit
Alaoris! It was done because of Henare s
confirmed habit of taking crayfish as light
refreshment and dropping the armor about
for other patrons to sit or walk on.
“O. Ockney”: Actor Frank R. Benson
has been knighted, and that makes seven, the
others being Wyndham, Tree, Alexander,
Forbes Robertson, Hare and Bancroft. Ben-
son was never what is known as a “fashion-
able” or West End actor. But he did good
work in running Sliakspeare—a dramatist
who wouldn’t be much heard of in the land
of his birth but for Benson’s efforts. Though
sometimes he plays a season in LondoiKßen-
son is better known and appreciated in the
provinces. Frequently he has had as many
as three companies touring at one time, all
presenting the work of William. And for
26 years he has been responsible for the
annual Sliakspearian festival at Stratford-
on-Avon. No one could call him a great
actor, but some of the best-known mummers
of to-day have been brought out by him.
Oscar Asche and Lily Brayton learnt their
business under his management, and when
Asche first appeared in Australia most of
his crowd were old Bensonians.
The third time wasn’t lucky for Huvli
Douglas, the flicker operator at His
Majesty’s Theatre, Hobart. A few months
ago he was walking down a Melbourne street
when a live wire fell on him. He was
nearly electrocuted, but got off with the loss
of the flesh from one side of his arm. Later
he took a walk near a quarry while blasting
was going on, and got his leg badly broken
by a piece of flying rock. His third call
came the other day when a film caught fire.
He removed it with his bare hands, and in
doing so got burnt very badly all over the
arms and face, and things look very black
for him.
“Hamer”: The closing-down of the show
at the Little Theatre (Sydney), which was
supplying plays of a superior quality to
audiences that were appreciative but not
numerous, is another evidence of our rotten
theatrical taste. Lack of patronage at the
Little has been credited to the theatre being
on a “bad stand,” but even in a central
theatre, Brough, staging the same sort of
goods, had a perilous season ; and more re-
cently the two sons of the Majeronis, staging
the classical plays that had made the name
of their parents, had almost reached the
rocks in Brisbane, when the bright idea of
putting up “The Kelly Gang” occurred to
them. That glorious masterpiece gave them
packed houses night after night, and filled
the gaping hole in the treasury.
“M.P.A.” : Melbourne Temperance Hall is
still the home of a once-a-tveek variety show
which has been running continuously for
over 50 years. In the old days the enter-
tainment was known as “the penny read-
ings,” and the admission was 3d. and Id.
The “readings” included singing and recit-
ing by well-known amateurs and budding
“THE SQUAW
MAN,” AT
MELBOURNE
KING'S
Willie Driscoll as
Hiram Doolittle.
professionals. Mrs. Pett used to preside at
the piano; and when things went wrong
and a hole occurred in the programme the
management would invite any member of
the audience to chip in. From the “penny
readings” the “Temp” show gradually
developed into minstrel and variety. To-day
it preserves the circle of gaily-clad Singing
and dancing ladies, with a centre man and
two end men. After the interval the circle
disbands and the performers all work singly
or as doubles. I sampled the bill the other
Saturday night and found a pair of Aus-
tralian-Chinese serio-chronics and a dusky
lady ballad singer who made a noise through
her teeth. Except for a tall, charming girl,
with a naturally good voice, the talent was
really awful; yet the “Temps” took their
amusement very seriously, joined in the
choruses and greeted the performers as mem-
bers of the family. The China girls sang
and danced badly, but were very well
frocked. They finished the bill and received
a quantity of shrubbery and sweets from
their supporters. Evidently the “Temp” is
the recognised outlet for Melbourne’s China-
town talent, and it has a certain following
that is prepared to accept whatever it gets
as first-class vaudeville goods.
Hall Caine, who has a stirring little war
sketch, “The Iron Hand,” running at Lon-
don Coliseum, has been denouncing the
theory that people want to forget the war
when they go out for an evening’s enter-
tainment. He considers it “dishonoring and
insulting” to suggest that people are always
trying to escape from the war. They know
it is their duty to think of the war day and
night, and the duty of the theatre is to keep
it constantly in the public consciousness.
In London the other day a benefit was
being organised for destitute Mrs. Barras-
ford, widow of the head of the first big-
music-hall syndicate. Tom Barrasford
started by running a negro-minstrel troupe.
Discovering the possibilities of the “two
shows a night” system, he exploited it and
rapidly acquired the control of 21 variety
palaces, directing operations from gorgeous
offices in Charing Cross-road. When Irving
left the Lyceum, Barrasford took possession
and converted it into an up-to-date variety
palace. It was this cow that kicked over
the bucket, and landed his affairs at last in
the Bankruptcy Court.
“Lantern” : I saw a nice girl on the beach
yesterday. She had her eyes screwed up
like an American picture actress, and was
chewing gum with that soulful look the old
ehewer always gets. Down by the rocks a
straw-haired lad was giving a lifelike repre-
sentation of a dislocated scarecrow. Charlie
Chaplin was his trouble. It didn’t surprise
me to read that Sydney was taking the virus
into its system through 113 shows —city and
suburban. The weekly attendance is
426,000 and at Od. a gazer the yearly bill is
well over half a million.
John McCormack has just sent a wreath
for the coffin of Robert McGuire, of the edi-
torial staff’ of London Daily Telegraph.
McCormack was singing in the choir of an
Irish village church when McGuire chanced
to hear him and recognised the exceptional
qualities of his voice. The stranger inter-
viewed him after Mass and the result
was that John was placed 'on the
road to fame and fortune. McGuire
was himself a good Irish singer, and,
while accompanying a recent concert party
in the war zone, filled the place of a
prominent London vocalist suddenly taken
ill. He served the Daily Telegraph as
Parliamentary reporter, special correspondent
in many places where trouble was brewing
or fighting was in progress, and occasionally
as musical critic.
“O. Blin”: Ex-actress Mary Anderson,
now faintly known as Madame de Navarro,
recently showed herself to the vulgar movies.
But it was strictly for a charitable purpose,
so the 'old lady was able to survive the
agony. As she sobbed to a reporter: “In
these times old artistic ideals must go down,
as the soldiers and sailors go-down, without
a murmur.”
"Floater” : Granville Bantock, the prolific
musical brother of C. R. Bantock who ex-
ploits his circumference for a J. C. William-
son salary, has written a symphony called
“The Hebrides,” which was lately "tried in
Edinburgh with noticeable success. Some
time ago he got the critics talking by setting
bubbly old Omar to music. Granville Ban-
tock, by the way, visited Australia 20 years
ago as musical conductor of the famous
“A Gaiety Girl,” and a brother was one of
the leading comedians thereof. C.R., im-
ported to play the mandarin part in “San
Toy,” was the third Bantock to come here.
“Gustavus Gravelpit” : When, having six-
pence and an hour to spare, I grope my way
into a seat at a continuous picture-show that
boasts a live orchestra, I cannot help specu-
lating as to how the movie musicians enjoy
life. The question spoils my entertainment.
Imagine sitting in front of a screen the live-
long day watching Brobdingnagian photo-
players making faces at each other and
attempting to musically accompany them!
What a life! I’ll never more complain that
the incidental melodies are ill-chosen. I shall
only marvel that there’s a man among all the
conductors sane enough to tell Stop Yerrr
Ticklin’ from the Hallelujah Chorus.
“Oking” : Re “Harrison O.’s” objection
(B. 27/4/T6) that Yankee performers hurl
their points at our benighted audiences. A
fair share of the blame must fall on the
theatrical importers. Quietly-played and
effective scenes have been altered and
broadened “to suit Australia,” by mana-
gerial mandate, almost at the “death knock”
of a production. The result is that gentle
souls like “Harrison O.” have to tie their
hats on.
After all, Irene Browne didn’t play the
name part in “My Lady Frayle ” in London
on its first night. Laryngitis laid the
auburn hair on a pillow, and a hasty under-
study trailed and made eyes and was pet-
tish in her place.
(Continued on pa ye 26.)
R. B. Rees, M.L.C., has resigned from the
Vie. Closer Settlement Commission, and
the press gang must be included among the
many who will refuse to be prostrated with
grief. Rees’s habit of asking unnecessary
questions got on the nerves of his colleagues,
who begged him to sub-edit his inquisitive-
ness. That huffed him, and relations between
himself and the other members finally
became so strained that he resigned. Now
that the fashion has been set, worried press-
men in the habit of reporting other com-
mittees with which Rees is connected have
hopes; for the little Welshman has always
been a prize bore, even for an M.L.C.
Left the port of Hobart the other day
John Dalgleish, master shipbuilder, who
hammered up some good vessels in the times
when the Hobart yards turned out ocean-
going wooden sailers. He had lived 76
years.
Ilicks-Beach, the English politician just
dead, had Beachport (S.A.) named after
him. The late Chief Justice Way, who was
Lieut.-Governor at the moment, made a
point of having the origin of the name made
public, lest it should be supposed to be in
honor of the Beach family, his own near
cafe-keeping relatives.
Incensed by the attempt of the industrial
disputes committee of Melbourne Trades
Hall Council to drive a collective bargain,
Alec Peacock has risen to a tine simile:
A submarine has discharged a torpedo at me after
I have given my promise to the employees, and 1
cannot call a conference while Damocles’ sword is
above my head.
The deputation grasped its aching brow and
fled to begin its yeasty meditations anew.
Until the condemned Sinn Feiners were
led out the other morning there had not
been an execution for treason in the V.K.
for nearly 100 years. The last to provide a
job for the executioner were the Cato-street
conspirators, who in 1820 proposed to
abruptly end a Cabinet dinner by making
dead meat of tbe diners. John Frost, the
Chartist rioter, William Smith O'Brien, and
others were sentenced afterwards, but
escaped death. The last trial for treason
was that of Arthur Lynch, M.P., the amaz-
ingly versatile Victorian who fought as a
colonel on the Boer side in the S. African
war.
It looks as if the Sunset Drought is going
to get through all right in the Speck. Of
the three members of the House of Dodery
who went to the electors this year two were
pronounced 10 o’clockers and both were re-
placed by candidates who had pledged them-
selves to support the will of the people as
expressed at the referendum. The third
member elected was also a six o’clocker, so
that early closing should now have a deal-
majority in both Houses.
“Jean S.”: I was hovering in the neigh-
borhood of John Murray one day when a
pressman broke into his siesta with the news
that Blank, a well-known identity, was
dead. As John was a friend of the departed
we tapped him for a few biographical par-
ticulars. He heard the request behind the
usual veil of smoke ; then straightening him-
self into an attitude of faint attention, he
drawled: “I haven’t any biographical par-
ticulars about Blank, but I can tell you a
good yarn about him.” That was a
tine illustration of Jack’s character. To
those who knew him well he was so emi-
nently the sort of engaging personality who
always had a good yarn about some-
body.
“0.K.”: When is our easy-going Empire
to be enlightened? Look back 18 months
at all the accursed nonsense published in
the English papers, and cabled to Australia,
concerning the shortage of copper in Ger-
many ! At the yarns about the removal of
heavy church bells and bronze statues from
Belgium and France for the sake of the
sorely-needed metal! Look back only a
couple of months at the reports of food riots
in Berlin due to the alleged stringency of
the blockade of German ports! Who but
the German Government, through its Dutch
agents, was responsible for the circulation of
those restraints upon the conscription move-
ment in Britain? The censorship, which
permitted persistent suggestions of the
enemy’s desperate plight, whilst suppressing
important testimony to his strength, has
been working for the anti-conscriptionists
all the time. The “food riots” stories were
disposed of recently by a man who wrote
from Berlin to a friend in England :
Do not believe all that you read in the German
press. Things are better than is stated in England.
There are no disturbances except butter riots. The
people are very angry because of the good food given
to prisoners at Ruhleben, and threatened to attack
the camp.
The fact that good, fattening fare is avail
able to any hated Englander who will pay
for it is proof that all but the poorest Ger-
mans are getting along comfortably. And the
poor are only suffering for their poverty,
which is a pretty general complaint in all
countries. If a couple of million underpaid
workers in America, for instance, don’t riot
for butter it is because they are accustomed
to do without it. Evidently the Germans
were not accustomed to do without it, and
they rioted because they knew someone was
getting plenty.
THE USE OF MUSIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Five hundred bards in Fingal's hall
Made music when the night was drunk ;
Five hundred pipers bold and tall
Blew, to absolve our souls ot' funk.
The foeman’s threats we could not hear
With those wild noises in our ear.
That was some years ago, but still
The principle holds sound and good.
How much of Mary’s frenzied skill
The old piano has withstood !
Yet how we bless the noise she flings
To drown our fear when Thompson sings!

STOP STOMACH DRUGGING.
NEUTRALISE THE DANGEROUS ACID
WITH A LITTLE MAGNESIA.
Stomach drugging is dangerous. Drugs
deaden the nerves and render them insensible
to pain, but pain serves a good purpose—it
is nature's method of indicating that some-
thing is interfering with the smooth work-
ing of the human organism. When the
fault is corrected, the pain will cease. Pain
after eating—heartburn, flatulence, etc., etc.,
usually indicates not that the stomach is
diseased but that it is troubled by excessive
acidity. The acid irritates and inflames the
delicate lining of the stomach and so causes
pain. Obviously it is of prime importance
that the cause of this pain should be
removed, and to accomplish this you should
obtain some bisurated magnesia from your
chemist and take half a teaspoonful in a
little water immediately after meals. This
will instantly neutralise the harmful acid in
your stomach and prevent all possibility of
food fermentation. Drugs do not overcome
this acid—they simply deaden the symptoms
and give a false sense of security. That is
why those who rely on drugs' gradually
become worse and worse, until the stomach
itself becomes actually diseased.— Advt.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Australia has a Little Germany up at
Holdsworthy (N.S.W.), and there has been
a providential inquest to show by the sample
what the whole is like. In the concentra-
tion camp there are, of course, rich and
poor—the population is quite representative
—and one of the chief industries seems to
be blackmail. “There are plenty of rich men
in camp who have had beatings, but were
ashamed to say anything about it,” said one
•witness. Also, according to other wit-
nesses, there were plenty of other rich
men who had bought off their beatings,
The tariff seems to have been as low as six-
pence. Apparently a Lutheran pastor had
omitted to call on the plate before he went
in, so, having no sixpence, he was thrashed
too. Still, you can’t judge a community alto-
gether by what the scoundrels do. The
really enlightening incident in this case
was connected with the manner in which
this Little Germany dealt with the people the
majority didn’t care for: they reduced them
more or less to pulp with any old weapon
that was handy, and then dragged them by
the legs and pitched them out over the fence
in a bleeding, quivering heap. (P.S. —Mr.
Pearce still favors the voluntary system of
coping with the danger of German domina-
tion.)
“Jobber”: Snobocracy has made itself
strongly felt in the army in Egypt. Recently
I went to Alexandria, 48 hours’ leave, and
dropped into the Carlton Hotel to book. The
porter was very sorry, but they could take
officers only ! “To with them,” I said.
“I’m as good as any officer in the English
Army and a damned sight better than a
lot.”' The bloke agreed ; he seemed to think
it wise to do so. I found another pub which,
not being quite so good, we mere blighters
may patronise. Then the question of dinner
in town arose. Naturally I wanted to give
her the best on offer. I made some in-
quiries. “Oh, yes, the Majestic is the best
place in town, but it is out of bounds for
other than commissioned officers !” Yah !
Isn’t it enough to make a decent Australian
“go crook”? I had dinner on the train
coming back to Cairo, and two Tommy offi-
eahs stared at me as though I were a curio.
Damn them, I can probably afford to pay
for my dinner quite as well as they, and
maybe eat it as decently!
“Full Astern”: A queer accident hap-
pened on Sydney Harbor lately. Two ferry
boats were approaching each other as “cross-
ing ships” within the meaning of the har-
bor rules. It was the duty of vessel A to
go to port, and vessel B shaped her course
accordingly. Suddenly A gave one sharp
whistle, meaning “I am going to starboard.”
B’s mystified skipper hastily put his helm
over, only to find that A was, after all,
going to port. B’s manoeuvre had the effect
of making a collision inevitable, and all
that the masters could do was to go full
astern to soften the blow. Fortunately the
impact was not severe and no one was hurt.
Upon B demanding an explanation it
appeared that A grasped the whistle cord to
give the regulation two blasts before putting
his helm over. Either because he pulled too
hard or because the cord was due for a pen-
sion it parted after he had blown one blast
and left him helpless. That’s how the lives
of ferry passengers on the harbor may hang
by a thread.
The men who now and then build the
railway between Broken Hill and Condobo-
lin can claim the championship for polite
speaking in an emergency. They object to
a ganger, and at a strike-meeting passed a
resolution, “That we give him a chance to
view the scenery of Sydney Harbor.” The
idea seems worth cultivating. Thus a girl
need never reject a suitor. She will say :
"Clarence, my sisterly-affection is of the
finest quality; and you can consider yourself,
promoted to try some.” Or the boss:
“Young man, the architecture of this build-
ing is much admired; you shall have a
chance to view it from the street.” Or the
Judge: “Prisoner, it is generally admitted
that, for scenery and climate in the one

The elaborate humbug surrounding
Hughes’s departure from Australia was
puerile nonsense; and he
realised it when he got to
Fiji. It had been given out
to the Australian papers
that the Prime Minister
was travelling by Orient
liner via Suez, and there
was actually a send-off at
the Sydney railway-station;
then in a clever way he
was whipped back and put
on board the outgoing
Niagara. On arrival at
the debilitated wharf at
Suva Hughes found that
his route was so well
known that the party was
met by the whole town
headed by the Naililili
native brass band. William
was next entertained in a
pub, beautifully decorated
for the occasion by a
bumper gathering of Aus-
tralians. who presented
him with a walking-stick—
its handle a gold-mounted
whale’s tooth—and Mrs.
Hughes with a pendant
studded with island pearls.
Further, Hughes discov-
ered that, while the
authorities were playing
their absurd melodrama in
Australia, the Fiji papers,
knowing no secret and vir-
tuously suspecting none,
boomed the arrangements
beforehand and talked
about them afterwards at the top of their
voice.
N. S. Wales P.L.L. Conference found time
to hurl some bricks at the State system of
boarding out destitute children. " Thirty
years ago Ma followed the plan of cooping
up “charity children” in barracks and asy-
lums, a disastrous system which Henry
Parkes changed in 1885. Since then the
infant flotsam thrown on the State’s hands is
boarde_d with private families at a cost of
from ss. to 12s. a week. The success of
such a system depends on its administration,
At the Labor Conference it was asserted that
the administration has broken down, and
after the many years of internecine strife that
has disfigured the N.S.AV. Charities depart-
ment, it is quite possible that it has. The.
Conference decided that the time was ripe
to abolish the whole plan and establish in

A HASH OF MOONLIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She breaks all vessels with an equal hand,
But chiefly scores with those that cost a lot.
The simple china cannot understand
Her whirling style ; but, when the days are hot,
The gas-stove, smashed three several different ways,
Hits back for vengeance with a fierce, blue blaze.
Then does she steam as steams a pumpkin boiled
And puff as railway engines on the track ;
And, stamping through the midst of dinners spoiled,
She bears a household’s curses on her back ;
Yet on her Sunday out so fair she seems,
Her faultless face her weekday crimes redeems.
The girl I met at Bondi that still night
Was all a seaside maiden ought to be.
Her voice was soft, her touch was fairy light,
She wandered far along the sands with me ;
We talked of many things and closer drew,
And as I touched her lips the taste seemed new.
The moon was not yet up. The stars are frauds.
And yet her hair was soft as thistledown.
The low wind sang; the ocean meadow’s broads
Tingled with hooded joy as soft and brown
As when the lampless night on Ossa fell,
And one white god had one old tale to tell.
I told my tale, of course, in happy haste.
I loved. Could this girl doubt it who had felt
The pressure cf my arm about her waist
And heard my voice to softest cadence melt
Breathing her name? She listened and she sighed
And kissed me chastely like a new-made bride.
Oh, Arcady! What tender whispers pressed
The shining of her little, rounded ear!
How soft the fingers that my jaw caressed !
How tremulous her accents and how dear!
Nights have I known and morns of honeyed bliss,
But never in the years a night like this.
The hour grew late and still we stayed and stayed.
The couples starting level all were fled,
Save two so silent that perchance they prayed,
Sand-reverent, each with bowed, averted head.
Our own devotions were of such a kind
That kisses made us dumb and sighings blind.
The laggard moon uprose in silver light,
Trailing white satin lustres o’er the sea ;
I saw that happy face all rosy bright
And dimpled with a subtle witchery—
And then my senses reeled, for in that look
A flashlight of the gods revealed—-my cook !
And now when through the chaos of my home
I hear the clash of dishes as they fall,
I think of one soft night beside the foam
When those pink fingers made my soul their thrall;
And she whose soul makes hurry to destroy
Found mine the lightest thing with which to toy.
Girls are so scarce; and someone must prepare
The tasteless pudding and the daily hash.
Love comes and goes upon the heedless air—
Ye gods of happy moonlight, what a smash!
But Mary says, because her heart is kind,
■“You couldn’t help it, Bertha. Never mind!”

“I can’t help it —they all laugh at me.”
Just who Sis Hopkins is not many Aus-
tralians to-day could possibly have the slight-
est knowledge. There was a time when the
name of Hughes meant no more than Smith
or Brown, but to-day there is but one Hughes,
just as we are told to note that four weeks
hence there will be just one Sis Hopkins and
every letter a capital.
Sis Hopkins says : “Making folks laugh is
my business. What’s yours?” and she "does
it on the Movie Film. Australian Feature
Films can tell you much more about the new
comedienne.
“Carfax” —in the throes of indigestion :
A Sydney co. devoted to the preparation of public
food supplies was fined £lO the other day for
having its premises in a filthy condition. During
previous weeks several similar cases were before the
courts, and the defendants were asked for various
trifling amounts. A rotten system this. Such
individuals should be prohibited under severe penalty
from ever again carrying on this sort of business.
Then perhaps it would be possible for Australia to
have at least one place where the food industry was
run on decent lines. As it is, Sydney is a gastro-
nomic Sodom and Gomorrah. The place is deco-
rated with a multitude of revolting shops in which
perspiring womep struggle for lumps of raw meat;
with fried-fish emporiums exuding hot, full-bodied
stinks; with restaurants foetid, gloomy and shudder-
some. Sydney people don’t eat decently of simple,
carefully-prepared food. They wolf grub. They are
being steadily brutalised by a greasy and greedy
Private Enterprise which wipes the spoons on its
coat and keeps the chops warm in its pocket. Have
we no patriot with aesthetic tastes sufficiently strong
to start a holy crusade to get all this smellfulness
and grimy squalor cleaned up? Don’t, for Heaven’s
sake, tell me that the Government would make an
even greater mess of things. That is not humanly
possible. Of coarse, it would start by losing money
and forget to take the steak off the grill at the
critical moment; but (by our great patron, Cohen
the Kind!) it couldn’t make a more revolting botch
of the job of feeding the public than Private Enter-
prise perpetrates in 9765 places three times a day
and far into the night. You can take it from me
that unless Australians can get plain food under
decent conditions our Great National Resources will
have been pawned in vain.
* * * *
N. S. Wales Equity Court is struggling
with a matter of £4OOO left behind by a
Sydney citizen of Chinese birth. Testator
bequeathed the whole of his property to the
“Canton Hospital,” and the efforts of his
aggrieved family abetted by a painstaking
Bar failed to upset the will. The Public
Trustee then threw the estate into Equity
because there are dozens of hospitals in
remote Canton, and he couldn’t tell wliaffor.
Five barristers instructed by five solicitors
assisted the bewildered Judge, but failed
to identify the winning institution. So the
matter is to stand over generally, waiting
for the hospital to call and prove its title to
the cash. Meanwhile that foreign devil,
Costs, is wondering if it can manage to get
away with the lot to save Canton John the
trouble.
* * * *
The quaint railway system of Port Adel-
aide has been revolutionised at last by the
opening of a new station. The old line
ran along the middle of the main street and
over a bridge which sometimes had to be
swung open for ships to pass. The new
station is the first “elevated” one in S. Aus.,
and has called for a lot of banking up. It
is mostly of concrete. So is the new bridge
over the Port River, by which all trains for
the Outer Harbor and the neighboring sea-
side towns will get through without touching
the centre of Port Adelaide at all. The
new arrangement has cost something over
£125,000, but is worth it in cutting out
a few of the level crossings, which get more
deadly each year as traffic increases. Yet
on the flat, chessboard Adelaide plain they
cannot be avoided without enormous expense.

For the ear of Billjim in particular, the
cry comes from Corp. H. Martin, Ist
Southern Hospital, Edgbaston, Birmingham,
England:—■
Can you help me to news of my son, Private
Ralph Martin, No. 615, 18th Battalion, A.1.F., who
was reported missing on August 27, 1915? Previous
to enlisting he was employed at Richmond (N.S.W.).
Since his reported disappearance I have received his
portrait, taken with a companion at the Sphinx,
Egypt, wrapped up in black-edged notepaper, and
posted in Sydney last October. No name came with
it or anything besides. Whoever sent this probably
knows my boy’s end, and we hunger to get in touch
with him or with anyone else who knows.

The old tradition that no good billet in
the Anglican Church in Australia can be
tilled except by importation got another
small knock the other day, when
Canon Forster, of Bathurst, was
appointed head of the training
college at Armidale, in the same
Ma State. He succeeds A. H,
Garnsey, another Australian, who
goes to be head of the Anglican
college at Sydney Uni. Forster
graduated at Melbourne, and was
trained for sky navigation under
Reginald Stephen—also an Aus-
tralian —who now bishes in
Hobart. Bishop Green, of Bal-
larat, ordained him, and after
serving his time as curate he went
schoolmastering, being also for a
time chaplain at Melbourne
Grammar School. For six years
he was second in command at a
training college in Ballarat, and
lately he has looked after the
collection-plate at Bathurst Cathe-
dral. The Armidale college is a
comparatively small affair, run by
the country dioceses in the Ma
State as a sort of orotest against
the exclusively “Low Church”
twist given to the training at the
big Moore College in Sydney. But
while mere Australians like For-
ster may be good enough for the
small country colleges to train
bush parsons, nothing but the im-
ported article has yet been
thought fit to take charge of' one of the big
training colleges in a State capital. The
appointment of Australian-born A. H. Garn-
sey as head of St. Paul’s at Sydney ’.Var-
sity, welcomed in The Bulletin some
weeks back, is not really an exception, as
that institution is not a training college,
and its head is chosen by a council elected
by the graduates of the college, in which
official Anglicanism has no voice.
“Mujik” ; Any explanation of why women
contract the habit of making a last will
and testament at an earlier age than men?
A mere remark that women are more par-
simonious than men isn’t an explanation—
it’s merely an opinion, and a silly one, too.
The facts are that woman tackles the job
young and that her brother maintains his
casualness until he gets up into the nineties.
According to the available figures in N. S.
Wales more men die intestate than with a
will up to the age of 40; then the position
is reversed. Which suggests that either
the improvident expire young or
not many men have much to will
until their hair is turning grey.
Women mostly get busy with their
wills when they are 25, but la'st
year a couple had postponed the
matter until after they were 94,
though this may have been because
they had only £5O between them.
One lady of 102 left £25; also a
will. Men with a lot of money
do not appear to die young, as a
regular thing. Perhaps chasing
more keeps them in good health.
Possibly the qualities of body and
mind * essential to the pursuit of
cash are those also which conduce
to longevity. For instance, the
10b3 men who quitted between the
ages of 40 and 60 had accumulated
£1,533,045, an average of £lll5.
But the 657 who died between 70
and 80 were torn away from
£2,755,744, an average of £4194.
This suggests among other things
(1) the effect of interest; (2) the
consequences of bloat; (3) that the
older men didn’t go to the races;
(4) that after 70 a man is pretty
hard in the tooth. Queerly enough
the very old men—the chaps who
run from 90 to 100—only have
about half as much as the 70 to
80 crowd. Do these super-stayers get out
on a bend at the last, or give the gilt away
to young wives or what? What it all pans
out to, though, is that the man who doesn’t
make a will dies before he has £4OO (or after
he has spent it) and the man who does make
a will has on the average about £3500. Of
men who died in the year under notice the
thick end was held by two out of three—
and that is something you won’t find hap-
pening in many other countries. There are
some places where for every one who pegs
out with a million a thousand quit with Id.
“S.T.S.”; What a wild idea of humor
some English journalists have! And
what a weird idea of what is harm-
ful the censorship must have! There
are two supposedly-comic stories going
the rounds, and being extensively copied by
Australian papers, with the murder of sur-
rendering Germans by British soldiers as
their sole point. In one ease a Cockney
asked how many men there were left in a
captured trench. “Five,” they said, “think-
ing they would be allowed to surrender.”
Then he threw in a bomb, remarking wittily :
“See if that will go round.” The other story
seems simply devilish. The “polony-biter”
is actually on his knees before a High-
lander, with his hands up, saying: “Kam-
erad! Me a Christian.” “Ye’re a liar,”
says I, sticking ma bayonet through him.
“Ye’re an angel!” Possibly that kind of
thing does go on in hot corners, but to boast
of it in print as a rich joke is a different
matter. For think of the effect of these
yarns when reprinted—as they certainly will
be —in Berlin and the neutral countries.”
“We had to do some stern things,” the Hun
can say, “but at anyrate we never thought
them funny.”

The divorce court is still a handy place
to meet the really casual Australian, and
one of him turned up the other day in Mel-
bourne. After 12 months of marriage he
wandered away and would probably have
stayed away but that, being fined a tenner
for chastising his spare wife, he called on the
real missus and asked her for the loan of
the cash! He was quite pained when she
refused.

“Y.Z.”: The best rifleshot I know has
just been turned down as a soldier because
of an impediment in his speech. The excuse
is that a stutterer would be useless on
sentry go. But a good talker is not turned
down because he is a hopeless hand with a
rifle. Evidently the politicians still believe
we can shoot Germany with our mouths.
HILL CLIMBING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The winter fashions are for the slim woman ; and
hill-climbing is recommended as an excellent correc-
tive for limbs that have become too well-rounded.
Here, mid billows of ninon and lace,
I smile as I gaze in the long pier-glass ;
Swaying for joy of my slim-limbed grace,
And my eyes grow grave as the swift thoughts
pass.
Up from the town the high street ran,
Then turned to the left where the grassy ways
Wound, reaching up till the sky began
And the wild wind blew from the sea and tlie bays.
Oh, all the winds of the earth met there,
And dew-splashed flowers blinked bright through
the mist,
My cheeks were red and my tumbled hair
Held the tang of the sea as we clung and kissed.
Yet ever I urged you higher still
On the path that corkscrewed up to the moon
(Sometimes “you” meant Harry and sometimes Bill),
Nor would I rest, though you prayed that boon.
The dear hill path! Now no fashion-plate
Holds terror for me. But had I sat
To dreamily spoon, had I dropped in weight,
And lost that stone of superfluous fat?

BILLY MAKES A FRIENDLY CALL.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
"Melb”: Wo have just lost perhaps the
most brilliant of our younger architects by
the death of I*. A. Kennedy, pupil of A"ah-
land, of Bendigo, and afterwards with Pitt,
the theatre specialist, of Melbourne. Ken-
nedy designed St. Tincent's Hospital, the
East Melbourne Catholic Ladies’ College
( built from the stones of ivy-clad Chalmers’
Church, where rhe birds used to chatter),
completed the superb St. Mary’s Star of the
Sea. West Melbourne, and was associated
with the construction of St. Michael's. North
Melbourne. Besides the ecclesiastical work,
he did much else on a large scale. His
young sou is a naval cadet at Jervis Bay.
"0.K.”: By a quaint coincidence the news
that William Hughes was dining with the
King at Windsor Castle came to hand just
after Malvolio, the most ceremonious-minded
person in literature, had been strutting his
little hour at the Sydney Adelphi, with four
minor flunkeys in attendance. The noble
Countess Olivia summons her steward to
her presence, and he comes along in state.
Allan Wilkie's idea of a Malvolio who
orders a quartet of waiting-men to stand
behind him when he talks, is hardly justified
by the Shakspearian text In fact, it
seemed a good deal over the odds until the
cable told us that "at dinner four Royal
pages waited on Mr. Hughes." What a
chapter in the life of William Hughes!
Four pages—and Royal ones at that! Yet
the humorless cable stated that no intoxi-
cants were offered to the honored guest-
only cider cup. orangeade and lemonade. As
though the whole proceedings were not cal-
culated to go to his head!

of stirring I'll tale yon somewhere in

SIMPLE FAITH: "Ah! I’m so sorry; but
whenever we get a heat ware I get a

yearn for the. — er —ordinary tramear.”
O'Connor, M.L.A. of South Aus., has been
brought before the Adelaide police court by a
humorous Electoral Department on the
charge of having failed to get on the Com-
monwealth voters' roll. Fined a shilling.
Through John Murray’s sudden death A.
R. Outtrim became father of the Tic.
Assembly. Outtrim, who is now 71, was
first elected for Maryborough 31 years ago;
but he was dropped for a while.
The eldest son of parson S. G. Fielding, of
Paddington (N.S.W.), has just got a com-
mission in the Oxfordshire Light
Infantry. Young Fielding is an old
boy of King's School, Parramatta,
whence he went on a scholarship to
Oxford a while be-
fore the war. As
soon as the Devil
broke out of Pots-
da m Fielding
dropped his books to
join King Edward's
Horse as a trooper,
and before long won
sergeant's stripes in
France. A Hun bomb
nearly passed him
out, but he has been
patched up in an
English hospital, and
is again at the
Front. Meanwhile,
Oxford Uni., reckon-
ing that his time in
the trenches will
teach him more
than years of lec-
tures. has given him
his B.A. degree with-
out further exams.
Parson Fielding him-
self has had a bit of
experience outside
the ordinary clerical
groove, for he served
some years at sea
before the mast in
windjammers, and
holds a mate's ticket
as well as his sky-
pilot's license. One
of his books of sea-
faring stories, “The Southern Light,” has a
place in a good many Australian school
libraries.
Women candidates were a long way from
the top in the Ma State Labor ballot for the
"Senate Three.” Rae (14.8S8), Watson
(12,7431, and Bowling (6743) got the
selection: Mrs. Dwyer ran first among the
women, and she was only 7th, with 2734
votes. After her straggled Miss Matthews
(14431, 16th; Miss Beddie (1144), 22nd;
and Mrs. Seery (779), 29th, and last of the
long tail. It seems like a strong tip to the
feminine section to turn to the culinary
department.
More May birthdays: “Cattle King” Syd-
ney Kidman, 59 on 9th; Prof. A. J. Perkins,
S. Aus. Director of Agriculture. 45 on 11th;
Pope Cooper (Queensland Chief Justice),
6S on 12th: Auditor-Gen. F. A. Coghlan
(X.S.W.), 57 on 13th: Chief Justice John
Madden (V.), 72 on 16th; Justice Chubb
(Q.), 71 on 17th; Melba on 19th; ex-S.
Aus. Senator Charleston, 6S on 27th;
Archdeacon Gunther, 77 on 28th; Geo. Gor-
don McCrae (Melb.), father of Hugh and
Dorothy, 83 on 29th.
The Federal Parliament reassembled on
May 9, which was the 15th anniversary of
the opening of the first Commonwealth Par-
liament by the present King. Of the orig-
inal legislators there are only 29 still
serving: 32 members of that first Parliament
are dead, which leaves just 50 survivors
who are now out of Federal politics. Gould
and Millen (X.S.W.), Stewart (Q.), Keat-
ing (Tas.) and Pearce and de Largie
(W.A.) have been members of the Senate
without a break. Sixteen have served con-
tinuously in the Reps.: Chapman, Cook,
Hughes. Bruce Smith. Spence, Thomas and
Watkins (X.S.W.) : Bamford, McDonald
and Page (Qr) : Tudor (Tic.) ; Glynn and
Poynton (S. Aus.) ; O’Malley (Tas.) ; and
Forrest and Fowler (W.A.).
.Tack Storey, who was “leader” of the
X. S. Wales Labor Party during last week’s
Day of Repentance, is a Sydney boilermaker
with a red. clean-shaven face and spectacles,
who will be heard of later on. He first
dropped into the Assembly in 1901 as repre-
sentative of Balmain, and after dropping out
during the See-Waddell-Carruthers regime
returned in 1907 to make remarks to G.
Wade, and has been there ever since. He
hits been chairman of the Public Works
Committee, and at times a pretty candid
critic of Holman and co.. especially of some
of the co.. and particularly of Arthur.
Griffith. When Holman backed his cart to
the P.L.L. and the P.L.L. had backed its
equipage to Holman and Hector Lamond
had remarked that the age of miracles had
not passed, John Storey said he was “much
relieved.” He was, too.
The South Aus. Liberal Union, after ex-
perimenting with numerous secretaries, has
decided to appoint W. B. Page. He was an
enthusiastic up-country worker at the Burra,
and on the last crisis in the Adelaide office
be jumped into the breach with such energy
that he has been chosen to stav there.
WHEN BAR MEETS BEAK.
CHAIRMAN: “Well, Mr. Blank, after listenin'
to you for three hours the Bench regrets
to say it is not one whit the wiser."
COUNSEL (politely): "I beg to assure your
Worships that I did not expect that
for one moment. I had merely thought
that possibly your Worships might be
better informed."
One Northern Territory family has put
tip a good record. Four Cains are at the
Front, and a fifth brother expects to get
there shortly. All were born in the X.T.,
and grew up in Darwin, one of the places
which the white man finds most uncomfort-
able to live in—if he’s of military age.
Tibooburra (840 weary miles from Syd-
ney ) must hold the track record for enlist-
ments. A recruiting officer and doctor re-
cently plugged through 210 miles of sand
from Broken Hill to examine recruits, of
whom 25 came over the horizon to be tested.
The shortest journey performed by any one
of them was 30 miles, and three dusty die-
hards had done 220 miles. Unfortunately
they all fell at the first medical hurdle. One
trier hadn't seen even a bush shanty for
over two years. Another, who is in the
first flight as a buckjump rider, was rejected
for having a weak heart, buckjumping,
apparently, being a parlor game suitable
-for nervous old maids and retired bank
directors. Only 14 of the 25 genuine bush-
fellows will wave good-bye from a transport
presently.
That broth of a boy. Dr. .T. A. Beattie,
who retires from the directorship of the
Liverpool (X.S.W.) Asylum after 30 years’
service with the Government, says it was
Henry Parkes who put him into a lunatic
asylum: his first job was at the .Mental
Hospital, (rladesville. But the doctor’s jobs
have been varied. First he was an innni-
gration doctor; then he got the appointment
at Gladesville, and afterwards went to
1 arramatta; some years later John See sent
him to London to represent the Government
at the International Congress on Tubercu-
losis; and ar different times he visited Japan
and China, and prosecuted inquiries in Egvpt
Berne- Heidelberg, Paris and Dublin.
At Liverpool, where thousands go to-die of
lung trouble, he was chief entertainer as
well as medical officer. Being a good elocu-
tionist, the drama had a first place in his
entertainments. If a “Povertv Pointer”
struck Liverpool Asylum he found a -ood
friend in Dr. Beattie. The late J. B. Steele
wa ? jH-je for years suffering from cancer
and died well cared for. John Ilowe was
a so an inmate for a time, and manv another
old pro. has drifted into the same haven.
J. B. Reymond, ex-M.L.A. (N.SW i y, ,
his first eighty-second birthdav at iforhe'
the other day. He was born in France ami
became a member of the reaching staff nc tl,
French University in 1853, but the l ore
the goldfields drew him to Australia Snh
sequently he pioneered wheat-growing i n th '
Vest, sowing his first crop near Forbes •it
a time when general opinion persisted that
the Western lands could not be made to d<
anything except cultivate sheep. In a aa;
tion to his experiments with wheat he laid
down vines for wine-production and built
flour- and sawmills. As a pioneer lie filled
the bill, and his failure to get prominence in
Parliament merely shows that a useful per
son is not necessarily a politician.
The frankest appeal to the electors Tiif
Bulletix has met for a long while wa<
made by Dr. (of Laws) Bottrill in the Tas*
Leg. Council fight the other dav bJj
Bottrill, LL.D. ‘ - a,d
At previous elections I have received so few x 0 i
votes that I have twice lost mv £25 deposit >i- lv
I rely upon you on Tuesday, Mav 2, to ’prevent mi-
losing another deposit? •
Undaunted by failure, I again launch my boat
This third time of asking, pray give me" your vote.
The question now is whether Bottrill com-
mitted a breach of the Electoral Act. The
law says no candidate may offer “valuable
consideration” to any elector. A banker
may not offer sovereigns. A man who
grows apples may not -scatter pippins. The
point that ought to be decided is whether a
poet may cast pearls before the swine.
Notwithstanding the reconciliation between
the P.L.L. Conference and the Holman Gov-
ernment, G. R. W. McDonald. Labor mem-
ber for Bingara (X.S.W.), probably chose
the best moment for severing his connection
with the party. An extremely enterpris-
ing little person, still in the early thirties,
he has been, and still is, many things. Com
mencing as a pupil teacher he gravitated
into the Crown Law Department as clerk.
While assistant C.P.S. at Tamworth during
the Peel River Estate subdivision, he saw
possibilities in the land-agency business, and
soon nailed up a shiny shingle. Before long
be was also M.L.A., stock and station agent,
importer, indentor, director and company-
promoter. Ever since the scandals of a
decade back Labor has looked upon land
ag< nts with a suspicious eye, and introduced
a small plank or splinter which prohibited
its Parliamentarians acting as paid agents
in Crown land deals. It might have become
ant-eaten like the other planks about non-
borrowing and other things, had it not beeu
for the more recent Boorabil matter. This
caused the piece of timber to be faced up
again until it was held to be too slippery
for McDonald to cross on his way for P.L.L.
endorsement. As a Government supporter
McDouald had few chances in the House,
but he was generally suspected of being able
to put the Liberal view as well as the next
man.

AFRAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Afraid to go!" the young man said.
He heaved a sigh, and shook his head.
"I read of thousands lying low —
To tell gor'struth, I’m ’fraid to go!’
They pleaded with him everywhere—
Grey streaks v.ere showing in hi' hair.
He only answeied: “No, no, no!
Don't worry me, I dare not go."
I saw him yesterday, and he
Was clad in khaki cap-a-pie.
"Yes, yes, I'm off." he paused to say—
“ Fact is, I am afraid to stay ?”

The Winter
is with Us!
HOWEVER bright the Sun-
shine at mid-day, there is
all the piercing keenness
of the settled Winter in the
early morning, or the nowadays
early evening air.
In any case of day or night, man
needs an Overcoat to ward off
sudden storms of Rain or
Wintry Wind —both his comfort
and convenience are at stake.
And in an “Austral” Overcoat —
under all or any circumstance —
is assured not only the certain
satisfaction of Man’s every need,
but the realisation also of his
every expectation as to perfect
fit and style.
“Austral” Overcoats
A FINE showing of popular
single- and double-breasted
styles in many new and smart
light-weight shower-proof tweeds,
and heavy, soft and fleece-finished
overcoatings. Many of the newer
short-skirted models have Raglan
shoulders, and are loose-fitting, as
fashion demands. Other styles are
more conventional, but the variety
will satisfy any preference a cus-
tomer may have in this respect.
35[-, 40/-, 45/-, 50/-,
55/-, 63/-, 70/-, 84/-,
to 95/-

AMONGST City Men especially a
light-weight raincoat is greatly
favored, and our showing in-
cludes smartly-cut single-breasted
styles, buttoning to neck or with
ordinary step collar, in Garbardine,
Garbicore and Cravenette, in all the
fashionable colorings. Values at all
prices are exceptional.
45/-, 50/-, 55/-, 63/-,
70/-, 75/-, 84/-, to 105/-
“Austral” Macintosh
and Oilskin Coats
FOR men who are constantly out
in the wet, and who prefer a
Macintosh or Oil-coat, we show
a wide and suitable range of styles
for every kind of wet-weather wear.
Walking and Riding Lengths in light,
medium and heavy weights are avail-
able, and every coat is warranted and
guaranteed. The prices are as low
as the standard of quality will
permit.

The gross profits for the past half-year
were greater even than in the Sept.-March
half of 1913-4. which shows up tallest in
the net-profit column; but the exes, were
higher:—
Mar., 1914. Mar.. 1916.
Gross profits ..£198,711 £199,890
Expenses .. 103,856 114,289
£94,855 £85,601
In the earlier half-year £5OOO was written
off premises and £2OOO was handed to the
officers’ provident fund: in the past half-
year the only appropriation was £5OOO to
reduce the premises account. The expenses
now. however, include a fixed charge of
5 p.c. on salaries for the benefit of the
provident fund. Taxes have nearly doubled,
and it is not likely that the limit has been
reached yet. But some of the extra exes,
may be passed on, because the banks are
still in the happy position of being a fairly
united brotherhood, though the arrival of
the Commonwealth Bank has to some extent
disturbed the circle.
But if the banks have formed a happy
circle it cannot be said that they have been
bleeding the country. This bank, for in-
stance. has £1.498.220 of capital and
£G22.102 of reserves. Thus it claims to
have for every £lOO of capital £142 of
funds. Yet the public values the £lO prefs.
at about £lO 15s. and the £5 ordinaries at
only £5 ss. Put iu another way a £5 share
in a bank which says that it has £7 2s. worth
of assets for each £5 share is saleable at
only £5 ss. This seems to mean one of two
things: either the bank's funds (or part
of them) are regarded as unsafe, presumably
because they are guaranteeing doubtful
securities; or the funds are not earning as
much as money is expected to earn at
present. As to the first point, there are. of
course, risks. The bank has £9.343.903 out in
advances —that is, all its own money and
over seven millions of other people’s. In
theory all of it is covered by security; but
in Australia, which inflates all sorts of pro-
perty values by wild borrowing booms,
experience has unfortunately shown that
security which to-day looks ample may to-
morrow turn out to be quite inadequate.
Except for the curse of these booms and the
possibility of heavy bank losses through
slumps, investors wouldn't have to look past
bank shares: a stock backed by £7 2s. of
live assets that could be bought fi r £5 ss.
would be good enough for anybody.
The National has been pushing ahead
rapidly for a good many years past, and the
progress is maintained :
Liabilities.
Mar.. 1915. Mar.. 1916.
Notes .. .. £ 36.223 £34,689 Dec. £1,534
Bills, etc. . . 924.405 1,123,960 Inc. 199,555

Liabilities of all kinds total £12.397.937.
and before paying the dividend shareholders’
funds (capital and reserves) were
£2.177.759. So the bank has about £5 14s.
of the public’s for every £1 of its own:
and that is well within the bounds of
reason. Of more or less liquid assets it
holds £4.808.390; and as that is almost 40
p.c the National is well placed to meet any
sudden trouble, though it hasn’t at the
moment any very large margin on which
to draw for the further accommodation of
customers.
The chairman of the National, by the
way. offered this good piece of Bulletin
policy and exhortation to the shareholders:
It is to be hoped none of us will be misled by the
present apparent prosperity, caused by the distribu-
tion of borrowed money. Producers and distributors
are doing well, bur it behoves each of them to be
very careful, for no one can say what state of things
will exist after the war, there being amongst leading
financiers opposite opinions, mostly depressing, but
some quite optimistic. The wise and patriotic man
will probably be he who, consistently with liberality
to deserving funds, saves what he can and invests a
fair portion of his savings in the War Loans when
money is required by the Government. Both as a
people and as individuals we should recognise the
paramount necessity of restricting as far as possible
our imports from foreign countries, thus keeping all
our money in our own.

Remarkable changes have taken place in
the Board-room of this bank. Old Henry
Scott was the first to go; Arthur Water-
house succeeded him. Then Jenkin Coles
went. George Brookman took his place only
a year or two ago. Now, rapidly, one after
the other, their three colleagues, A. G.
Downer. James Harvey and Mueeke. all
connected with the bank for very many
years, have passed off. The bank of to-day
isn't therefore quite what the bank of
yesterday was. It may be better; it may
be worse. We don't know. All we know is
that it is different. In filling the vacant
chairs S. Aus. has delivered a rather inter-
esting kick. In particular Adelaide has got
it into its head that it has been ruled too
much by old men—both in the public service
and out of it. In some respects it is cer-
tainly so. As the result of the rebellion,
the Bank of Adelaide directors couldn’t
muster among them much more than half the
years they could not so far back.
Comparison of the figures of this bank
has always given uncertain results, because
of its connection with the Government.
Now, apparently, the Government has gone
over to the Commonwealth Bank; and
partly for that reason the figures compare
pretty badly with those of two years ago.
Then the total balance-sheet figures were
£6.228,041: now they are only £4.726.755.
The next table shows how the various groups
have altered :
Liabilities.

Those figures will show why profits have
shrunk of late. And they are really less
favorable to the bank thau the last column
would suggest. because the ordinary*
advances are now down to £2.441,057, the
balance being the amount loaned to the
Government to pay the farmers a first half-
crown a bushel for their wheat. This paper
doesn’t suggest that the present is a suitable
time for splashing around advances: but a
steady reduction from £3.128.104 to
£2.589.386 and on to £2.441.057 doesn’t
indicate a movement towards larger profits.
The bank is of course strong beyond all
question. Including advances to the Gov-
ernment with cash and liquid -assets, it
holds in those forms no less than 60 p.c.
of all the public’s money in its hands; and
the public’s money amounts to only about
£3 12s. for every £1 of its own. Big profits
in such circumstances are quite out of the
question unless the bank is fed on tit-bits.
The bank has the foundations for a much
larger business; and profits will not be
commensurate with the shareholders’ funds
until that larger general banking business
is got. At present, combining capital and
reserves, the shareholders are getting little
more than 3f p.c. on their funds; and plainly
that isn't good enough.

In the previous year the war was blamed
for direct losses of £45.771. In the past
year, though trade has been disturbed in
various directions, the wind has been tem-
pered to the shorn lamb. But that’s a way
the wind has in its dealings with B.P. The
co. is one of the finest money-spinners in
this hemisphere; and a large part of the
art of making money, like that of the art
of making war. is to turn accidents to good
account. In this instance, no doubt, though the
c-o. probably couldn't get away its copra, and
though the Island trade may have been
affected in other ways, its shipping and
agency business with Asia must have coined
money for it The Jap boats (which B.P.
manage this end) have been pouring in
stuff, and the trade with Java and Singapore
in the co.'s own boats has been booming.
Added to that there has been considerable
new business with ex-German New Guinea—a
good deal of it Government business at
prices which don't matter. Anyway, they
don’t seem to matter. It will be quite a
different thing when presently we have to
pay the lOU on which we are so far raising
the money.
As the result, direct and indirect, of the
war. the eo.’s figures have changed a good
deal during the year:—

Trade debts have increased by about
£40.000: on the other side both stocks and
book debts are much heavier. This is
doubtless partly due to the extension of
business (during the year, for one thing,
the co. bought out Robbie, Kaid and Co.,
Ltd., of Fiji) ; and perhaps, owing to the
lack of ocean shipping, the co. is finding it
necessary to finance Island customers. But
the co. won’t be doing it at a loss. They
don't cut quotations by sixteenths in the
Islands. Think of the difference that must
gradually arise between the mental habits of
the wheat-farmer who laboriously once a year
drags out a 10 or 12 bushel crop and the
planter in tropical islands who sees growth
in a week greater than the other can find
iu a year. B.P. has established its kingdom
where, when anything at all is to be got, it
is as easy to make it half a crown as in most
places it is to extract saxpence. It takes
good management, no doubt; for supervision
of these very scattered branches and
agencies is difficult. So far the co. has had
all that; and there is growing up a crop of
inspectors who should be able to carry on
for many years even when the co.’s present
big mainspring is gone.

This business has been in the melting pot
during the 12 months. Three years ago.it
was admitted that the money spent in Fiji
consisted of undisclosed profits made outside
Australia: but the assets were not then
written up —merely a small quarter-million
of visible reserves were capitalised. Since
then, there appears to have been a valuation
of the assets outside Australia, and
£3.250.000 is written on to the total assets.
In other words, to put it quite plainly, the
accounts issued during past years have
misrepresented the position to the extent of
£3.250,000 in the aggregate. This amount,
hitherto hidden, is now brought to light and
represented by £3,250,000 of shares in a new
co., the Colonial Sugar Refining Co. (Fiji
and New Zealand), Ltd. The shareholders in
the old co- while retaining their shares in
that parent co., get an equal number of
shares in the Fiji Co., the latter carrying a
fixed cumulative pref. div. of 6 p.c.
The old . co. pays 61 p.c.; so the two
together are equal to the old 121 P-e- For
the present, therefore, the shareholders have
gained nothing by the reconstruction. But
they hope to benefit several ways.
It isn't healthy these times for a
monopoly to pay anything more * than
10 p.c.; and this co.’s method of dodging the
difficulty by issuing frequent bonus shares
was drawing unfavorable attention to itself.
The new plan is bold and comprehensive. It
seems to be complicated, so there is less row
about it than if the co. merely issued another
half-million's worth of shares. Yet now the old
co. will be able to divide another £113,000
a year and still pay only 10 p.c. And really
if the co. can make the public believe that it
is a concern which scorns to grab at more
than a fair thing, there is hardly any limit
to the profit which it may make. It- has the
sugar trade of Australia in its hands, and is
heavily interested in cultivation and crush-
ing. as well as being practically the only
refiner: and even when it is coining money
it is able to virtuously show that Australia
is actually paying three or four or more
pounds a ton less than England. That Eng-
land doesn't grow sugar and Australia does
is. of course, not mentioned. This recent
shuffling of the cards is therefore going to
strengthen the co. at the point at which
strengthening is likely to bear the best fruit.
The new co., mentioned above, was formed
by the creation of £3.250.000 of pref. shares
issued to the old shareholders and £250.000
of ordinaries for which the parent co. paid
cash ; this cash provided trading capital for
the new concern. Its first 12 months have
given this result: —
C.S.R. Co. (Fiji and X.Z.) Ltd.:—
Net Profits. Dividend. Reserves.
Mar.. 1916., £328,830 6 p.c. = £ 202.500 £126.330
That makes for the two a net profit of £500,0N1
for the year, compared with £520.069 for the
preceding 12 months and £463,159 for the
year ended March. *l3. This indicates a far
too rapid rise in profit-making out of a public
necessity: and the figures are ample proof
that in the frequent strife of wits between
the Governments and the C.S.R. the latter
has been an easy winner—thus far. '■
If one occasionally feels disgusted at the
abominable greed of the co. one can’t heln
admiring the consummate, skill with which
it angles for its fish. Though the entire assets
in Maoriland and Fiji were cut off without
affecting the old co.’s balance-sheet figures
note how already the M.L. and Fiji nn.fit'
scamper ahead. C.S.R.. £201,251; M ] aTlf i
Fiji. £328,830. The M.L. and Fiji Co is
registered in Fiji; and there is no Labor
party there to show tiger’s teeth if a co. pavs
20 or 50 p.c. As mentioned above, the old
co. —the Australian co—has £250,000 in
ordinary shares in the Fiji co.; and these
shares are entitled to all the profits (1) after
0 p.c. has been paid to the prefs.; and (2)
after the reserves have been built to £200.000
Had the required reserves been previously
provided, the Fiji co. could thus last year
have paid the Australian co. 55 p.c.; and of
course in the future we are going to hear
the chairman explain that things were dread-
ful in Australia, but fortunately their inter-
ests in Fiji enabled them to pay 2 p.c. more
than last year. And when he further mentions
that the Fiji co. paid them 50 p.c., the Aus-
tralian will believe that it was all due to the
Fiji co.; and the C.S.R. may escape, as it has
escaped hitherto. It is an awful old humbug,
but it is such a clever old humbug,
and does its humbugging with an air of such
tremendous respectability, that one can’t
help admiring it.

This co. has a paid capital of £72.857. with
£27U5 in subscribing shares and £16,824 in
reserves after paying the current dividend;
total of its own funds. £Ol.BBO. It works
on this and £08.138 of deposits and other
public money. The liquid assets amount to
£14,513. Deduct this from the liabilities,
and £83.625 remains, against which there
are advances £171.063. and other non-liquid
assets to bring the total to £178.063. The
creditors are therefore protected by over £2
worth of assets (book value) for every £1
or debt. What these assets are really worth
is another matter: and accounts don't tell
you that. But there shouldn't be much
dead-wood in the present £171.063. Some
years ago there was a good deal of writing
down: then, partly because of the extra-
ordinary rise in timber, dwellings around
Brisbane increased so very much in value
that this co. ought to have been able to
get rid of its foreclosures. Further, the
interest credited to I*. and L. during last
year was just under 74 p.c. on the average
amount of the advances; and that looks as
though there weren't many dead accounts.
But. though the principal looks right, onecan’t
expect any large thing in the way of profits.
In the first place, we have had all the pro-
perty boom we are going to have for some
time: and in the second place the State is
seeing that in one way and another these
building societies" best customers the home-
makers. are offered facilities undreamt of
by Dad when he was first married.

“You can’t measure service With a foot-rule”—
and you can’t measure the worth of “Chromol”
Leather Belting by lineal measurement. It’s the
service it gives, its durability, pliability and adapta-
bility to all sorts of conditions that tell in its favor.
“Chromol” Belting is mineral tanned, has a longer
fibre, and is more elastic than bark-tanned belting.
Being heat- and damp-proof it does not deteriorate
rapidly, and it will stand the severest atmospheric
or industrial conditions. Used exclusively on some
of the stiffest drives in the mining plants of the
Commonwealth. J. C. Ludowici and Son, Ltd.,
“Pioneer’ 1 Leather Works, 117 York Street, Sydney

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.— Advt.
From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers. — Advt. [2

Extract from The Citizen, London, 15th Septem-
ber 1915: “Colonial Mutual Life Office.—lt is a
distinct pleasure to receive and review the annual
report and actuarial investigation of this excellently-
managed, world-wide mutual life office. Apart from
the purely technical aspect of its operations, there is
the admirable work which it is doing in knitting
together the English-speaking peoples in various
parts of the great British Empire. The Society is
mutual in the purest sense of the word, and the
manifest advantages of life assurance upon the
mutual principle are admirably portrayed in its
records. The Society has no shareholders, and all its
profits are divided amongst its members.” — Advt. [1

Vislok is a single, self-contained lock nut, consist-
ing of two sections hydraulically forced together.
Vislok has a solid, continuous thread throughout, and
when locked gives a regular and positive hold on the
thread of the bolt. Vislok is so constructed that the
energy of the working load, and vibratory force
exerted upon it tend to increase the locking action.
Vislok can be used as an ordinary nut, and is easily
applied in confined spaces. Vislok will not injure
the bolt-thread, and is quickly locked or unlocked
with an ordinary spanner. At the present time
Vislok is proving its usefulness on the Government
Railways and at the Cockatoo and the Naval Dock
Yards. Agents: B. J. Ball, Limited, Sydney and
Brisbane; Elder, Smith and Co., Melbourne and
Adelaide; Elder, Shenton and Co., Fremantle and
Perth; Colin J. Shields, Launceston. — Advt.

Really, of course, the capital is much more
than £27.150, because £72.133 has been paid
as premium capital. In one respect that lat-
ter item has been a bad thing: it has so
bloated the reserves that the directors haven’t
troubled much to strengthen them otherwise.
It is really disgraceful that a co. which has
had such a tremendously good time should
have put by only about £32,000 of its
profits to meet contingencies; and there is
not another line of business in which the
contingencies seem to be more serious at
present. The one thing that can be pleaded
in extenuation is that the co. has written
down its plant severely; which means that
it has paid for a good deal of plant-exten-
sion out of earnings.
Federal Pastoral Shearing Co., Ltd.
(Sydney), which took over the Federal
Sheep Shearing Co., Ltd., contract shearers
in the Eastern States and S. Aus. The
deficit of £9635 comprises goodwill, £9500;
and preliminary expenses, £l3s:

It looked 12 months back as though the co.
had got round the corner and was going to
make a success of a very doubtful proposi-
tion. Then drought looked in, and share-
holders will have to wait to find out whether
there really is any money in the business.
This paper hasn’t great hopes that there
ever will be much. If the profits show up
well one year the co. will be come at for
higher pay ; and it will be easier to get the
pay put up on the strength of a good year
than hauled down because of a bad one.
There is the further heavy expense of admin-
istration and the difficulty of supervision.
Maybe the utmost economy is now practised ;
but the outgoings seem terrific. “Head-office
and branch-office salaries, administration,
charges, telegrams, postage, interest and
general office exes.” run to £11,207, and
that doesn’t include advertising, stationery,
legal exes., wages of travellers, and a host
of other things. These exes., apart from
depreciation of plant, run to 4s. 6d. per 100
sheep shorn ; and that is a lot of money to
pay for a management which produces a
loss. The capital is £22,596; and liabili-
ties total £8135. Assets consist of plant
(£8867), stocks (£8227) and cash and book
debts (£4930).
