The N. S. Wales South Coast suffers from
sleeping sickness, and whenever things get
rather worse than usual the inhabitants turn
over with a groan and put it down to the
Almighty. A shipping co. which serves a
district like that hasn’t any bright thing in
hand. Still it manages to keep going in an
unemotional way, and can mostly put by a
bit of surplus profit. But whether it really
makes the profit to put by depends on whether
it writes enough off against depreciation ; and
on that point no information is given. The
financial position is comfortable : the co. owes
£6166, and its assets apart from ship
property, freehold and plant run to £18,601.
Seven years ago liabilities were £6380 and
the corresponding assets £BO7O. That shows
an improvement of £10,736. But nominally,
at any rate, some of that must be set off
against wear and tear. How much we
can’t say. But it looks as though the
easy-going old co. was keeping things. well
in hand.
* * * *
J. R. Godfrey, formerly mines inspector at
Broken Hill, has given the C.S.A.. Cobar, a
thorough overhaul, and estimates that about
150,000 tons of 7 p.c. secondarily enriched
ore are available for smelting. Taking cop-
per at £6O per ton, which isn’t much more
than half its present boom price, that means
over £600,000 of metal. The enriched ore
hasn’t oeen fully explored, either. Below
the altered ore there are large quantities of
sulphide, carrying little copper, but with
good zinc values. It is not a smelting ore,
but some of it will serve as basic flux. How-
ever, that is wasteful, wherefore Godfrey sug-
gests that experiments be made to determine
whether the flotation or some other known
process will do the trick and save the zinc
and .the lead. “If,” says Godfrey, “Great
Cobar, Ltd., will buy this ore without pro-
hibitive penalties for the zinc and lead, -large
quantities are available, probably amounting
to about 200,000 tons between the Nos. 3
and 4 levels.” Lower down the ore seems
to lose some of its zinc and lead and, not
being so much on the acid side, becomes
more valuable for fluxing. Fifty thousand
tons of the best of it could be added to the
150,000 tons of secondary ore to make a
very fair smelting mixture worth 54 p.c.
The little co. which some time ago took
over the way-back Mt. Hope (N.S.W.) is
sending down enough copper to keep the pot
boiling. Audley Smith has the business in
hand : so far attention is chiefly directed to
the tailings dumps. Now and then, between
strikes, the Broken Hill-Condobolin railway
is being built; and that will relieve the co.
of most of the present burden of road
cartage.
* * * *
Concerning a strenuous life :—-
Fred Neeld, who passed out at Wvalong (N.S.W.)
a few days ago, crowded a lot into his years. Coming
to Australia in 1849 he started mining in Victoria,
and was at Ballarat during the Eureka disturbance.
Then he went to Castlemaine and Bendigo, and
thought nothing of walking 120 miles from the dig-
gings to Melbourne and back. Crossing the Murray he
“selected” in N. S. Wales; but he couldn’t lose his
old gold-hunting habits, and thus became entitled to
a £lOOO Government award for discovering the
payable Wyalong field. Although the amount was
passed for payment it was never collected, perhaps
because the miner didn’t particularly need the
money. It was on March 13, 1894, that Neeld re-
ported his Wyalong find. He did his early prospect-
ing and washing in a fryingpan, and used a splitting
wedge to break down the stone. In the twinkling
of an eye there were 10,000 people on the field. The
old prospector was 86 when night fell.
* * * *
Trouble is threatening in Westralia over
the appointment of part-time workmen in-
spectors of mines. The unions connected
with the Miners’ Federation do not intend
to submit nominations for the jobs until the
Minister gets rid of his present disinclina-
tion to appoint full-time men. The dispute
doesn’t affect the Ivalgoorlie field, where two
full-time inspectors are to be elected by
ballot; but the unions threaten trouble if
any of the 30 who have been nominated are
found to be non-unionists.
Gt. Fingall (W.A.) has effected all the
underground improvements it set out to do,
and 20 head of its 50 stamps are again in
operation. The remainder are to be set
going when sufficient inducement offers.
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) has decided to
look for a new mine in the country that
made it. At an early date prospectors are
to be sent out to hunt for gold and other
minerals, and if they can’t be found in suffi-
cient abundance in the vicinity of Leonora
the search parties will be sent further afield.
An interest will be given the finders of any
valuable discovery.
Nullagine Gold and Diamonds Co. (W.A.)
has most of its plant on or en route to its
dredging holding, and hopes to make a start
some time in August. Profits from gold
dredging are tipped to average between
£l5OO and £2OOO a month. Any diamonds
found will add extra brilliancy to official
anticipations.
* * * *
The April yield of Edna Central (W.A.)
was 2904 tons for 100740 z., worth £4OSB.
Of this total 1304 tons consisted of cement
dirt worth 12s. Bd., and 1600 tons came
from the new reef and averaged 395. The
average of the whole crushing was 28s. 14d.
This is poorer than the March effort, ..for
then 3023 tons gave 31360 z., worth £4465.
the 1119 tons from the new make averaging
545. per ton, and the cement a trifle under
13s. Costs ran out at 18s. Id. and 17s. 2d.
for March and April respectively. If the
run of the mine keeps up and capital costs
keep down, the co., as things go, can afford
the shilling div. it has declared. There is
still a big gap between the assay values,
not only of the new make, but of the
cement; and until that is filled the men in
the Central feel about as safe as if they
were suspended on a parachute.
By going on to the Edna Central Board,
William Orr establishes a mining connec-
tion with every State in the Commonwealth.
An early bird at Broken Hill he raked in
a fortune there, and then topped it off by
going into Mt. Lyell with Schlapp and
Bowes Kelly in the Crotty days. He pio-
neered at Zeehan, and later on sent John
Godkin on the hunt for mines in North
Queensland, with the result that the Hamp-
den was secured. William Orr didn’t cut ice
in Westralia till he got into the Westonia
camp. Dick Williams, of the Central, is an
old servant, and no doubt his word attracted
Orr to the field. No one knows better when
to take a profit than he. And if the market
could only have the call of his intentions
it would be very much obliged.
* * * *
Just when State-Geologist Herman put
out a warning to Victorians not to expect
too much from the Mitta Mitta tin-bearing
area, Adelaide thumped the big drum over
the Springtime claim. C. H. Angas and
C. H. Nitschke represent the cash and the
brains of the show. The modest estimate
is that a 2 p.c. grade ore can be banked on.
At current prices for oxide this would give
a gross profit from the handling of 1000
tons weekly of £3OOO. Think of it!
£150,000 to be picked up annually in near-
by Mitta, and not a motor tooting out of
Collins-street to share in the plunder! Why
so?
* * * *
Corella, Cloncurry (Q.), joyfully reports
that the deepest level in the Rosebud has
run into friable sulphide that doesn’t require
picking before it is sent to the smelter. The
width_is from sft. to 6ft., and values range
from 5 p.c. to 11 p.c. copper. During April
1000 tons ore returned 102 tons matte, con-
taining 56 tons copper and 30oz. gold. A
Charters Towers man, W. 11. Williams, is
managing the show, which has been the
main source of the Corella’s ore supply. In
all 4539 tons have been smelted from the
Corella this year for 252 tons copper; but
some of that was from the Secret and a
trifle was custom ore. As smelting was
stopped because of a shortage of sulphide,
the development at the 300 ft. level is very
opportune.
* * * *
MacDonald, of Mammoth Copper Co., is
chief shareholder in a molybdenite claim on
the Tate (N.Q.). It is popularly known as
“MacDonald’s big show.” The shoot is devel-
oned from a shaft sunk on the underlay, and
the two levels that have been opened indi-
cate very valuable reserves. A battery that
has been carted from Torpy’s Crooked
Creek mine is in course of erection. A
rough calculation makes the molybdenite
value £20,000; but the mineral is worth over
£4OO per ton (for concentrates assaying
90 p.c.), so £20,000 would mean rather a
small tonnage. The concentrator will soon
decide whether this new “big” show is going
to swell the war-tax.
Herberton (Q.) still produces far more
lode tin than the rest of Queensland: its
114 tons of concentrates in March were
worth £12,500. But the Good Friday con-
tributed £6900 (60 tons of concentrates)
from 401 tons of crude ore, and Herberton
doesn’t see a crushing like that every day.
The Vulcan crushed 900 tons for 16 tons,
which was poor.
In March the Oaks (N.Q.) crushed 1100
tons for 1250 z. It costs only 6s. 2d. to
mine and mill the low-grade crushing stuff
from the open cut and tunnel workings, so
a small profit is made. In Queensland only
Gympie can compare with these figures.
Several attempts have been made elsewhere
in the North to make low-grade gold-stone
pay dividends. Mt. Leyshon, near .Charters
Towers, is the latest failure.
Some wag who must be running a bear
account has been digging out typical anal-
yses of North Lyell ore to compare with the
Lyell Blocks bornite. The North bornite
averages about 6 p.c. copper, 63 p.c. silica,
7 p.c. iron, 74 p.c. sulphur, 94 p.c. alumina
and 2 p.c. residues (barium). No lead is pre-
sent, and the gold is 0.0050 z. and the silver
I. per ton. Typical analyses of the
Blocks ore are not obtainable, but for 24ft.
just driven the borings show copper from
7.3 to 9.12 p.c., silver running up to 23dwt.,
lead 0.4 p.c. and gold to 20gr. per ton. This
ought to be very profitable, though it is an
axiom at Queenstown that it doesn’t pay
outsiders to send in stuff going less than
7 p.c. copper. Probably Robert Sticht isn’t
over-anxious to encourage small fry to ask
him to treat low-grade ore. Knowing birds.,
ranged on the “short” side of the market,
are grumpy just now because the sampling
isn’t done their way, and because they didn’t
get a first tip of a change in the face. None
of them, by the way, have ever been liberal
in that direction over their own mines.
* * * *
There seems to be a livelier time brewing
in the oil business. Recently a combine of
big shipping interests, including the
Cunard, White Star, Atlantic Transport,
Dominion and Leyland lines, concluded an
arrangement with the Mexican Petroleum
Co. whereby the latter has undertaken to
supply all the oil the shipping concerns may
require during the next 25 years. In order
to hump the fuel to Noo Yark and Liver-
pool contracts were immediately placed for
no less than 30 oil-tank steamers.
* * * *
John McWhae’s Philippine Dredges, Ltd.,
has been picking up steadily ever since
J. G. Aikman and secretary Showers got
the Gillies crowd to ease up their claim to
a slab of the co.’s ground. They also were
able to size up the groggy state of No. 1
plant and consign its working parts to No.
2 pontoon, whence, as resurrected, it will
emerge as a unit late this year. Such vir-
tuous semi-scrapping has its reward, for
last month’s yield with only three dredges
was 23470 z., against 19640 z. for March,
when four dredges were in commission. The
ground dredged ranged from sd. to Is. No
wonder John McWhae’s fiat feet beat a
pleased tattoo in Collins House, for with
£4OOO to £5OOO profits a month to be swilled
into the treasury and litigation out of the
way, worries over the debenture issue can be
made light of. The co.’s four plants last
year did duty as follows :

sole them for the long wait for the accounts.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 8, 1916.
My Dear Arini.—
There are a number of nice old gentlemen
wandering about Sydney looking as though
they have lost something. And so they
have: the Athenaeum Club has gone. The
membership got too small for the high rent
which the absentee Rosebery collected, and
the chop and the steak conspired with the
cook and the waiter to put up costs till
there was little comfort in the old place
which “Toby” Barton used to frequent and
of which “Hop” was a pillar to the end.
This week the auctioneer's hammer is being
heard through the rooms. The faithful will
merely hold on to enough glasses to furnish
a couple of rooms- —with the help of an easy
chair or two. They will move with this
salvage into Bull's Chambers till the spring-
time comes again.
Once upon a time women wore hoots to
keep their ankles warm and because cash-
mere stockings were not wildly attractive.
With the advent of the cheap silk stocking
shoes became the vogue. High-heeled and
silk-hosed we walked the block and feared
no man. Then came the short full skirt. It
was attractive, but it ended so early and the
shoes commenced so late that only a boot-top
permitted fashion and “respectability” to
walk together. But the other evening, at a
spot where fashion and beauty foregather to
drink the before-dinner cocktail, the legs of
one girl were not only respectable but re-
splendent. Her boot-tops were in some light
shade of suede, edged round with dark fur
and finished off with choux of tulle!
An audience composed almost entirely of
the Old Testament derived much enjoyment
at the Repertory Theatre on Saturday even-
ing from a performance in Yiddish of “The
Jewish King Lear,” a four-act drama by
Jacob Gordon, in aid of the starving Jews
in the Russian war zone. For myself I
confessed I felt like I think Shylock would
at a Methodist Conference. The drama
depicts a Russian Jew who acts as King
Lear did in dividing up his kingdom among
his two elder daughters, and, like that ancient
monarch, he has a third daughter who, de-
spite his ill-treatment of her, really loves
him. This Cordelia is named Tibala, and
is apparently protected by her tutor from
the old man's rage. As I don't understand
Yiddish, the plot, further than that, re-
mains somewhat vague. But the difficulty
of understanding a play in Yiddish is not
so great as with other foreign tongues, be-
cause Strunin’s talented players speak so
eloquently with their hands.
Although in his most tragic moments
King Jacob Strunin Lear was compelled to
flourish a brilliant bandana handkerchief and
wave his flowing beard in the wind, he con-
trives to present a picture of nobility
and genuine pathos. The two husbands
of the wicked daughters. Messieurs Morris
and Cohen, furnish brilliant character studies,
and even manage to survive their humorous
hats. One triumphantly achieves the re-
markable feat of being conscientiously drunk
in Yiddish. In Mr. Broid the company
has a humorist beside whom Charles Work-
man would look as pathetic as a film-
imitator of Charlie Chaplin. He expresses
more with his hands than Hugh Ward used
to express with his feet Mr. Berkovitz,
the juvenile lead, has youth and dramatic-
vigor. Miss Lewis, one of the wicked
daughters, is -also to be complimented upon
her illuminating gestures, which needed no
translation into English. Mrs. Strunin,
the quiet Cordelia, looked the youthful part
and preserved her girlish demeanor through
the first two acts. Probably her chance
for drama arrived while I was away learn-
ing Yiddish.
On Saturday afternoons the Clerical
Women War Workers bang the covers on
their typewriters, switch off their adding
machines, jab an extra hatpin through their
lids, and are ready for the motor-lorry jour-
ney to French’s Forest, where they dig and
delve and make afternoon tea, just as we
suppose Eve did in the Garden of Eden.
One band of helpers, under the direction of
Miss Horticultural Brace, is laying out a
few acres after the style of the poultry-runs
and vegetable patches of Los Angeles. But
the girls who run the commissariat sling
billies of steaming tea on a long pole, and
distribute the same with a smile to the
different workers, and I guess that’s pretty
Australian.
The Octavius Beales —Mr., Mrs., and two
younger daughters—are going to the Unoted
States early this month. The very valuable
and public-spirited daughter Ruth remains
here. She also had the chance of the trip,
which will be a prolonged one, but reckons
she has work to do here —the work of induc-
ing the Australian woman to wear her own
tweeds and eat her own chutney and make
the mesh of the Protection fence as fine as a
milk-strainer.
Mrs. Walter Elkington. a much-travelled,
brilliant talker, capital listener, doer of kind
deeds and a no-account frocker, who used to
live in Macleay-street, is now a widow. She
had been in Switzerland since just before
the war, but was with her husband in Genoa
when he died the other day. It her
son Geoffrey that pretty little “Bunty” of
the string-pulling married. And it was her
niece (Colonel Otway’s daughter) who was
Melba’s first daughter-in-law. Just-departed
hubby, once an officer in the English army,
was of the well-known family of silver-
smiths of his name. He had stations in
Maoriland and fads everywhere. There was
quite a real though short-lived fear that he
would leave all his woolly property to Chidley.
The widow Elkington was a noted beauty
in her day, and a number of the Best Fleeces
in M.L. overlooked her unconventional views
because she and the quarrelsome Countess
of Warwick had been presented to the dear
Queen (Victoria) at one and the same
Court.

Cabled that Mrs. W. J. Holloway has
died at the home of her daughter, Mrs. J. R.
Wood (Essie Jenyns). in Surrey. England,
aged 75. She was the widow also of Dr.
Jenyns. of Brisbane, but married “Plain
Bill” Holloway many, many years ago.
Tea and the scone run a pantomime or
comic opera quite close as a money-winner.
How much do you think the various batta-
lion workers who had a tea-room at the
Agricultural Show took for the week? Six
hundred pounds! To be accurate, they took
more; but six hundred is the profit the bat-
talions will get.
Dr. Taylor-Young, who left here many
months ago to help in Egypt, is now
attached to the Third London General Hos-
pital at Wandsworth. “This is very dif-
ferent work,” he writes a friend, “to the
Sydney routine, but I am sincerely thankful
to have the opportunity of doing a bit.”
When Dr. T.-Y. was in Sydney he sat on
the topmost branch of the gynecological tree,
and fellow sawbones used to have a bit of
fun with him over the name of a house he
inhabited in Edgecliff-road. It was called
Kilbride.

“Your affectionate but thirsty brother
Billjim” dates this from “A Bad Smell”: —
Dear Vandorian (and I hope you’re young and
don’t mind), —Let me give thanks unto the Aus-
tralian women who have started a teashop for
soldiers in Heliopolis. Good cakes, boiled eggs,
sandwiches and tea, coffee and cocoa from half to
two piastres (sd. to Is. Bd.), and served by good,
clean, wholesome Australian girl —after the evil-
smelling Greeks and their loathsome food it comes
like a breath of Australian air in this city of ancient
odors. And how those girls work' There seem to
be only about a dozen of them, yet one day they
served 2700 of us. I don’t know them all, though
1 was trying to when I was pushed off to somewhere
else. However, 1 did recognise Mrs. Chisholm, who
started the show, and looks very much overworked
(and will you please give her three cheers) ; Mrs.
Stanger-Leathes and her daughter; Mrs. Norman
Robertson, whose lightning calculations on her
fingers are one of the attractions; Miss Constance
Carson and Miss Beresford. But they’re not all like
That. There are still some “ornaments” who pervade
Shepheard’s angling for staff fish. And pretty fish
some of ’em are, as one or two of the dears have
found out a’ready.
Mrs. Holman’s address to the Citizens’
Association on Thursday was about the most
interesting to which that body had listened
since its auto-creation. The Premier’s rib
is a great advocate for United States
methods —industrial, sanitary, labor-saving,
and the like. When she told of going to a
cupboard in the dark and a light appearing
automatically on the opening of the door
her bearers gasped “How lovely!” Did the
Premierix lie abed and letters of light sud-
denly appear on her bedroom wall, it was
not with the usual fatal significance attach-
ing to fiery writing on such an absurd pad.
No, it was not “The Loan has fallen due,”
or .“Thou bast been weighed and found
fatter,” but merely an intimation that there
were letters or telegrams for her in the
office.
Women’s industrial and intellectual
achievements in U.S.A., as told by Mrs. Hol-
man, . proved a revelation to her listeners.
She spoke of their citizenship as being a real
religion. And they think and talk and
work for the benefit of the Child. All this
enlightenment is certainly needed by people
who have let their views be tinctured, if
not formed, by stories in American maga-
zines and by Rupert Hughes’s novels. Bad-
ness, money-madness, brainlessness, selfish-
ness, faithlessness, all worked into the com-
plex pattern by America’s most brilliant
novel writer! The Child has about as much
place in these stories as a dodo chicken. One
wonders, after reading six months’ output of
American mags., how the race is carried on.
Talking of conveniences in home-planning,
Mrs. Holman’s remarks reminded me of a
humorous experience of a friend of mine.
She and her husband got to Wiesbaden dur-
ing tbeir foreign travels. Arriving at night
they went to their hotel, and this is how my
friend tells the remainder: “I woke early.
I always do —drat it! And I looked envi-
ously at my sleeping mate. Then I
got up softly, cooed an order ‘Tea for two’
through the telephone and proceeded to un-
pack and hang up our united kit. All the
time full of admiration for the architect of
such a room! One side had double walls
into which were built the finest wardrobes
that ever gave a woman pop-eyes. One door
after another I opened suspending masculine
or feminine clothes —I must have filled four
and, still in my nightie and holding an even-
ing gown, I opened a fifth door, a mirrored
one and— there was a man standing within!
My scream must have lacked kultttr, for
Hans looked injured as he stepped past me
into the room, carrying a tray with tea for
two. A second look, of course, showed me
that Hans had come in the outer (wall)
door and closed it behind him. thus giving
himself every appearance of hiding in the
wardrobe.”
Mrs. Louis Waxman, wife of the “stormy
Petrol” who lifted the lid off something in-
flammable connected with the British Im-
perial Oil Co., is still at the Australia,
invalided. She came over for the Easter
races, saw them, and met with an accident,
falling down some steps in Her Majesty’s
Theatre whilst making her way in the semi-
darkness to her seat. Mrs. Waxman
injured one arm and an ankle rather
severely.
Everybody who
ever reads a Syd-
ney paper knows
Miss Rose Scott:
she has been full of
good works for
years and years,
and a fairly well-
lined purse has
been open to the
needy, especially
poets and other
afflicted people. But
not everybody who
asks receives. One
strapping fellow,
the worse for Miss
Scott’s pet horror
of drunkenness —at
least, that is one
of her pet horrors
—called a while
ago and demanded money. The lady told him
he looked very well able to support himself.
He argued the point; then began to bully.
Finally he scraped up a handful of light
gravel and threw it at her. There were no
men-folk about the house, so Miss Scott
called a hefty housemaid, and the man left
full of abuse and throwing more gravel as
he went. All the time, a few yards along
Jersey-road, a carter had been sitting on
the kerbstone. Miss Scott, seeing her duty
clear, spoke to him. “You heard that man
abusing me; you must have seen him throw
gravel at me —and yet you didn’t move to
the assistance of a lady! Why didn’t you ?”
“Well, mum,” he said, “he was your hus-
band, I suppose, and I make it a rule never
to interfere atween husbands and their
missuses.”

John McCallum. M.P. for Wairau. hastened
back to the Maoriland session, after doing
the rounds in Egypt and other warlike parts.

Mrs. W. C. Wall has had word that her
doctor son, who was at the landing at Suvla
Bay and afterwards spent some months in
15th Stationary Hospital at Lemnos" k
coming home by the Medina. Another ’son
who was invalided from Gallipoli in "the
latter part of last year, is making a good
recovery at Wentworth Falls.
George Meudell, of Melbourne, takes his
wife to Vancouver by the Makura. which
leaves Sydney on Thursday. For four
years the volatile Australian has been
pioneering the oil-well industry in Australia
and he is visiting the United States to talk
to Uncle Sam about it.
St. Andrew’s Cathedral housed the wed-
ding of Captain E. E. Longfield Lloyd and
Miss Elsie Wilkinson, daughter of Lancelot
A. Wilkinson, of Bondi. Captain Lloyd,
who was twice mentioned in despatches
won the Military Cross at Gallipoli.
Word comes from the Cold Country that
Dr. W. .Tenner, late of Leichhardt, has
passed the exam, for the F.R.C.S. of F.din-
burgh. Just before the Great Upset
the doctor and his family left on
a holiday, but on arrival in the Big
Smoke found that there was no room
for idle doctors in war time. So he plugged
into a hospital job, and in between sleep
and work found time to secure the coveted
Fellowship of Edinbro’s R.C.S.
As the people found their way to the
Conservatorium on Monday night to hear
Holman, senior, lecture on elocution they
were met at the door by a frantically anxious
man who asked, “Can you play the National
Anthem?” Nobody could, so his Ex. and
two daughters came in without being “God
saved.”
Mrs. Ellis Rowan passed quietly through
Sydney on her way to Tapua lately. She’ll
spend some months painting the gorgeous
plumage of the Wonder Jungle.
Eva Lambert, daughter of the late Captain
and Mrs. Walter Lambert, and adopted
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. de Coureey Russell,
of Arlington. Edgecliffe, has promised to
keep the moths out of Captain A. T. H. Xis-
bet’s uniform. The captain belongs to the
Ninth Field Ambulance, and is the eldest
son of Dr. Nisbet. of Townsville.
Brendan, only son of John Lane Mullins,
cables his arrival in England, where he has
gone looking for a commission.
“Rosna’s” note from London:—
The one Australian hospital for officers in this
village is at No. 1, Moreton Gardens, S.W., the
former home of Jenny Lind, and now the property
of Mrs. Popplewell, who used to be Rosa Bird, of
Sydney. She has given her house and runs the show
herself—under the War Office’s eye, of course—with
the aid of a paid matron and two sisters and a staff
of voluntary nurses. They are nearly all Australian
damsels; and Mrs. Popplewell’s own daughter and
(until her marriage) Thelma Reid and various other
butterflies have for many months peeled the potatoes
A Red Crosser tells me plaintively the'
workers are badly needed at the Town Hall
to keep idle machines company. What offers':
Our young friend Ellis Ashmead-Bartlett
has been correctin’ persons in Maoriland
who have been callin' him a mere journalist
or common war correspondent. It seems
he is a Military Expert and that he was
first of all a soldier, having been an oflieah
in the Boer war. It was only by accident
that he became a descriptive writer. Another
rumor that he has been energetically chasin
about Volcanoland with a club is the one to
the effect that he is a son of the Ashmead-
Bartlett who married the Baroness Bnrdett
Coutts. He is explainin’ firmly that he is
a nephew of William Lehman Ashmead Bur-
rlett Cootts and that he is the son of Sir
Ellis Ashmead-Bartlett. Bart. I cannot tell
you, my dear, how mnch my mind is
relieved.

There is a little island in the centre of the garden

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melboi'rne, Mat 8, 1916.
Mr Dear Yahhie, —
Governor Stanley aud his lady, as if to
atone for their haste oa the opening day of
the exhibition of antiques—this inustn t be
confounded with the Leg. Council, which is
still in recess —strolled along again on Fri-
day. Mrs. Russell Clarke tshe was the Tas-
manian Mackenzie girl, you know) gave
them some excellent tea. Admiral C reswell
joined the group later, and said he'd have
three lumps, please. General Bridges s widow
was present in deep mourning. Command-
ant Williams also adorned the party in a
really beautiful purple tie.
Mrs. J. B. Pearson and Mrs. Sharp came
down from their Berwick castles and took
charge of the Government House lolly-shop
for one day last week. They built all the
sweets themselves and made their respective
kitchens so sticky that both houses are now
hanging out the pathetic placard, "cook
wanted." the established practitioners having
declared that they'd "stand no sech non-
seuse with the saucepans." A week's notice
followed in each case. The result of the
nonsense was £l4 taken in a few hours.
Some frank country girls, who came along
to help rathe the big boxes, declare that the
Government House brand of dame is "just
too mean" and hangs on to her sixpences
until the coins nearly break with the strain.
One haughty hippopotamus, loaded with
hundreds of pounds' worth of fur and dia-
monds. and with four pounds of gold in
her tusks, waddled past with her nose
v. rinkled. "I never eat sweets !" she hooted.
The snubbed little country mouse who didn't
know that hippos feed principally on aquatic
plants, said quietly: "Well, now! Doesn t
she know a child in the world who would
eat some for her?”
Government House got rid of its antiques
on Saturday by a long-distance auction sale.
Arthur Tuekett took' the hammer in hand
at 2.30. and at dusk, the bidding still being
suirited. placards appeared announcing that
the show would continue until eight o'clock.
Tuekett was then standing like the sole sur-
vivor of a bombardment among an awful
medley of furniture of bygone days, facing
another mountainous heap of stuff awaiting
disposal. The relies were slapped off at
any old price. Miss Clapp put up her
poled spectacles and g! ated over a massive
mahogany bedstead of ancient pattern, which
was knocked down to her bid of a tenner.
Mrs. Rmmerton collected an ivory -handled
parasol, several thousand years old. for l'is-.
and daughter Mrs. Norman Brookes go: an
ostrich-feather fan. with a real osprey and
an amber handle, for a song ( ‘Home. Sweet
Home." I think was the item!. Some of
the Baillieux st od aloof, watching and wait-
ing. and nodded away some of their barking
accounts. Strong men struggled and fought
over a hideous panel of some rare textile, and
the bidding went up by leaps and pounds.
S. me lucky woman caught a scarlet-and-g dd
Oriental c wn of great magnificence for .ess
than bargain-counter price. Dr. CTeyeni
hovered lovingly over some r.ne pieces of china.
Nobody would go more than £4O for the mas-
sive Georgian silver teasel, so it was removed,
greatly to the relief of the man who had to
shoulder it round the crowd for exhibition.
Miss Currie was one of the brisk bidders.
The sweets and dower stabs did s me flna.
i- aaltory business in the porch and entrance
hall: bnt passers-by escaped the f. wer maid-
ens with a murmured “Not to-d-c.. thank
you." “Just as if we were John Chinaman
at the door!” hissed one flower fairy wrath-
fully.
Somebody remarked that the Government
House dis lay i antiques was really a •
as a whole, bnt a tritt green in parts.
' . nuine antiques frowned on th merely .d.
which in turn scowled at the frankly ociera.
But not one visitor In a thousand knew
which was which, so there was no earthly
chance of a riot about It.

Rupert Boyd, who belongs to the ft Beck-
etts and Gurners (old-time Australian
families), is Lieutenant Boyd once more.
He held the rank in the piping times of
peace: but when the guns began to plaj he
enlisted as a private, and subsequently got
au active-service commission —which is quite
a different thing from the parade-ground,
evening-party lieutenancy.
Esther, the ruddy-haired daughter of the
Fairbairns. is applying all that she learned
at St. Vincent's to the patients in Dr.
Douglas Shields's London hospital. In a
recent letter she underlined the news that
she is no longer a Red Cross housemaid but
a nurse. Then she proceeded to describe
the string of motors which wait each after-
noon to carry the noble English housemaids
to their ancestral homes.
• Bob’’ Cain, after assisting to reshape the
Egyptian end of our Red Cross work, drifted
on to England and a commission in the
Grenadier Guards. This tremendous exploit
caused no surprise to his Australian friends,
for Robert had an air. a mannah. and
pretty taste in socks and ties which
was thought to eminently qualify him
for life in a crack regiment. I under-
stand from my war literature that
the crackest regiments nowadays sit in
the mud and scrape themselves with hoop-
iron : but the sock-and-tie test may still be
used, for all I know.
The girl-nurses of the Massage Corps at
the Base Hospital (they wear ducky uni-
forms both indoors and out) lately got an
infinitesimal rise in their pay. Overjoyed,
they arranged a huge theatre party to "ho
Long. Letty." to follow a dinner-party at the
Francaise. The doctors (God bless them !)
delighted the bonnets by attending.
The Scot Sharps gave a merry house-
party at their Beaeonsfield country place in
honor of cousin Walter Berry, who is leav-
ing for the danger-zone almost at once. Some
friends assisted the family to keep Care
beyond the doors and eat the fatted rurkey.
and the parting guest of honor broke the
news that he had been made a major.
These dear, unmercenary society pets do
appreciate the war-glory of their one-time
dancing partners. “Oh. my dear." squeaked
one. “did you see that Billy Woolbale has
been made a captain 1* Tern pommds a iced.
you know!*' So Biiiy was put on the list of
eligible*. and will get a silk mutSer or a
set of trench slippers by return post.
Dr. Fred Bird and Dr. Harold Jackson,
the latter the lengthy brother of Mrs. ARGUS
Cunningham, came back during the week.
Dr. Bird was not attended by the quartette
of nurses he took away at his own expense.
Sterns that little squad was broken up long
ago.
Philip Schuler has been converted from a
war correspondent into a mere private. He
is off to the Front at an early date with the
A.M.C.. and is taking several specially-bound
editions of his war book to impress the
enemy.
General Landale (an Indian Mutiny
veteran * passed out in his English
h me on the same day as his soldier son.
Captain J. Landale. was killed in action in
Mesopotamia. He was a brother to the
late Alec and Robert Landale. two of
Riverina's wealthiest squatters.

The Wattle Day Leaguers organised a Bose and
Button Day on Friday, and added £1650 to the
motor-ambulance fund.
S. C. Kaines Smith, who has been given a com-
mission in the British army as interpreter, is a
brother of Mrs. Evan Kyffin Thomas and S. Aus-
tralian br birth. He has made his name as a writer
and linguist, his “Greek Art and National Life
being a very learned volume on which many front
teeth have been broken.
The church thief has turned up again in this
righteous community, and one of him relieved the
Roman Catholic Chuch at Hectorville of £6O worth
of vestments the other evening. But the detectives
are sure to catch him when he wears them.
Popular tipping is that Acting-General Traffic-
Manager McGuire will be the new Railway Commis-
sioner—for a time, at least- It is also suggested
that Professor Osborn may take Dr. Holtze s place
at the Botanic Gardens, while keeping on his pre-
sent job at the University. He is still in the 30 s,
but has already done very noticeable wor« as an
economic botanist.
Miss Gillies-Stewart and E. Stanley Rob-
inson increased the list of war marriages
bv one last week. She made an effective
bride, and her white doth suit would have
passed any fashion inspector. Sydney is to
be their home.
Jack Goodall and Vera Crowle signed a
marriage certificate at St. Peter s. Eastern
Hill, last Saturday, and then left for Syd-
ney. Jack recently inherited a good share
of grandfather Goodall’s wealth. So the
bills won't worry the pair.
The local Petts are busy with the £117,000
left by grandmother Victorine Pett —the very
musical lady with the remnant of a glorious
voice who lived in Toorak.
The handsome St. Kilda girl who was
Kitty Boyes until a couple of months back,
when she said that she would to Captain
Russell-Jac-kson. has suffered in the cause
of patriotism. Wearing the national cos-
tume of Haggisland she was to have ridden
in the national pageant organised by the
wife of artist MeCubbin for Anzac Day. but
at a dress rehearsal the trooper's steed on
which she was mounted became restive and
the lady was thrown. Kitty broke a bone
in the leg and is at present confined to
crutches.
Mrs. Carlyle Smyth- is back in Melbourne
and breathing easily after a stopwatch rush
to Westralia and bac k with her bus'and and
Frederic Villiers. I understand she or ke
all records for the distance.

The academic air of the Lyceum Club was
almost electrified last week. Mrs. Jam- -

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Coolibali”: “McNorth” says his buslimen
don’t bother about studying topographical features,
points of the compass, etc., when striking a course
through the wilderness. They just “rely on plain,
instinctive sense of direction.” Well, I know horses
have this sense, and so have dogs, but I have never
been able to discern possession of any such faculty in
the human animal, black, white or piebald. I have
seen a nigger just as badly bushed as any jackeroo
could be. If “McNorth” -thinks there is anything in
his theory let him take any duly-certified bushman,
blindfold him, and tell him to strike a course. I’m
game to bet pints for the crowd that his “plain in-
stinctive,” etc., won’t enable the bushie to even find
the pub. No; Billjim gets his direction from the
sun or the stars, and makes a close study of
watercourses, dividing-ranges, belts of- distinctive
timber, etc. True north may be found when the sun
is cloud-hidden by observing the white-ant nests on
the trees. These are always on the north side of the
trunk; at least, this is so south of Capricorn, but it
seems probable that in the Far North, where the sun
in the course of its orbit travels all round the trees,
the white ants may do likewise and build indis-
criminately.
* * * *
“D. Tease”: Re “Beeritus’s” ancient
battler and his wheelbarrow (B. IC/3/’l6). In the
early sluicing days of Victoria a prospector named
Joe Curran, more often known as Wheelbarrow Joe,
took his kit from one rush to another on the one-
wheeled contraption. Sometimes he pushed it; at
others he pulled, harnessing himself between the
handles. He was shifting camp on one occasion from
Kilmore to Reedy Creek, and slept a night on
the road. There w r as heavy rain that night, and
when morning came all tracks were obliterated, and
Curran had forgotten whether he had been pushing
or pulling his landau the night before. He decided
at last that he had been pushing it, and went the
way the wheel was facing. He was highly delighted
when, after midday, he arrived back at Kilmore.
“Try Fluke”: About the quaintest thing
I have seen in the way of a graveyard ornament in
my M.L. wanderings is a battered old tin bath-tub,
which lies under the shade of a cabbage-tree beside
a tombstone set up by a Government of the ’7o’s
to the memory of the Maori chief Epiha te Tuhi in
the old native churchyard at Mangere, on Manukau
Harbor. The bath-tub had been used by the vener-
able brown party just before he died, and so, being
tapu, it was no longer a useful piece of furniture,
and was placed handy for his ghost to shelter,under
on wet nights.
* * * *
“Billy T.”: Scobie came to Tinfield, and
put up for the time being at the Dingo Inn, which
was next but two to Mrs. Semple’s small emporium
—greengroceries and fancygoods, fresh milk and
eggs daily. Scobie took up a fencing contract with
Quilliam the same evening; next morning he pro-
posed to Mrs. Webday; on the following afternoon
he married her. Two nights thereafter he was
back in the bar, lamenting his sins. “I’ve let myself
in for a pretty thing,” said he dolefully. “S’elp me
fat, the house is chockful of kids— her kids.”
“But the youngsters were there when you married
her,” chirruped the barmaid. Scobie shook his head.
“I bin took down,” he said. “I thought she kep’ a
school!”

“My oath, we’ve got ’em beat!”
“Tiaki”: That ferra “W.D.” (R.
24/2/’ 16) him all te wrong when he say te maori-
hen or weka no good for kai. Te Maori eat him
and te pakeha over at Nelson used to cook him too.
Te reason te Ngatika hungunu no take te weka is
because Hau Ananaia changed two pononga into
kiwi and weka. Te Ngatika-ete. no like eatum
transmogrified brother. Te Maori eat shark. It
taste like boot soaked in sheep dip. If that werry
good to Henare why would he not swallow te tender
little weka? “W.D.” got te rat.

“Wanderer”: “Barcoo” (B. 13/4/'IG)
asks if anyone knows of the meat-eating proclivities
of the horse. A nag belonging to a hotelkeeper on
the Downs (Q.) was turned out during the last
drought into a slaughter-yard and paddock. The
tallowman was at a loss for some time to understand
why his pots were always more or less empty, until
he caught the quadruped enjoying the broth. He
was in splendid condition and had a fine coat. (I
mean the horse.)
* * * *
“F.D.”: One of tlie quaintest brews or
squashes on record is that manufactured by the abos.
on the Tully River and adjacent dis-
tricts in N. Queensland. In the scrubs
of those regions there is a peculiar
sort of green ant whose nest is about
the size of a football and is found
installed in the leaves at all heights
from the ground. It is a sort of con-
glomeration, with the ants in thou-
sands , and their eggs, belongings and
bad breath in the centre. The blacks
wrap the nest in leaves or a cloth and
squeeze it over a billycan or such
receptacle. It is surprising the quan-
tity of liquid that can be secured by
judicious pounding and pressing.
The overproof tack, is then diluted
with water and handed round amongst
the bucks. In the days when the
Chow did a roaring trade in opium
charcoal and other strange compounds,
there was a great demand for the
ant-juice, and little mobs of nigs
could be met at all times in the scrub
hunting for the nests, or sitting
round the billycan while the squeezing
process was going on. After a de-
bauch of the Chow’s decoctions it
seems as if the green-ant juice acted
as a sort of pick-me-up, and no beer
shark after a perisher ever handled a
foaming tankard of colonial wallop
with greater anticipation of bliss than
our wayward and benighted brother when eyeing the
ant booze trickling into the billycan.
* * * *
‘Pick ’: Perhaps there is something in
“Pharmo’s” idea (B. 13/4/’l6) re inoculation with
snake venom, and maybe it was, after all, gradual
inoculation and not an antidote that made Morrissey,
Fox and other snake fanciers apparently immune
from the poison. Anyhow, I know, because I have
been through both games, that bee-farmers and bush
bees-nesters after a season or two of constant sting-
ing are no longer affected, though to beginners
among the honeypods the stings cause much pain,
swelling and profanity. The misfortune is that
Government bodies and the medical fraternity turned
down Morrissey and Fox, and nobody authoritatively
proved whether their antidotes were shams or the
real thing.
“Piscatory”: Re “Chaney's” inquiry (B.
23/3/'l6) for a lure for codfish. During a sojourn
on the Darling I found rabbit-liver to attract them
from Menindie to Wilcannia. True, I haven't been
there for years, and cod may have changed his fancy
since I tickled it. Or, maybe, rabbits took a tumble
and changed their livers.
“Barcoo”: “Quandong” (B. 16/3/16)
seems to doubt my statement that the electric motor-
driven sheep-shearing machine does the quickest and
most effective work. He complains of his electric
machines heating and fusing on any cool morning,
and asks what gear our machines were swung on to.
Let me whisper to him that, providing the electric
power is adjusted and applied intelligently behind
any brand of “cannon” on the market, it will do the
job in hand more speedily, more effectively, and with
less friction to all parts (including the “gun” itself)
than any of the old-fashioned methods, while the man
behind the implement wears a joyous smile as his
record mounts upwards. The machines I mentioned
are still running, and not hot either. Two or three
of the same breed are receiving no complaints in
C.Q., and 50 of them will be in one shed shortly.
My cheque this year is already beginning to acquire
the dimensions of a ’possum-rug. I can put my
thumb on the causes of “Quandong's” growls—
crook “experts” and snide electricians.
“Stanlioe”: Re the Jackaburra-snake-kill-
ing argument. I was prospecting near the Weir
(Herberton, N.Q.) when I happened across what at
first sight seemed to be a bundle of feathers sur-
rounded by numerous hoops. It turned out to be our
friend Jack in the death-grip of a brown tiger snake
oft. lOin. in length. Both parties departed this
life ; the snake per medium of a small hole drilled
in the back of his head. I could not find a mark
on Johnny, and the only conclusion I could arrive at
was that Joe Blake had strangled him.
* * * *
“Corio”: What is the record number of
fencing-posts split out of one tree in Australia? (I
say split, not sawn.) Three years ago a red gum
near Forbes (N.S.W.) yielded 130 posts; while
another, also near Forbes, delivered up to the splitter
143. Is that anything like top figures?
“M.W.”: It was at a country sports meet-
ing that I saw the most hospitable man in the world
reclining, dead drunk, against a fence. There was
beer-froth on his lips, and his faithful but thirsty
dog was standing by and licking off the sparkling
hubbies. As I watched, the owner put up a heavy
hand and feebly patted the dog's head, muttering
sleepily: “You're welcbme! You’re welcome!” The
sociability of a perfect gentleman!

NEWS FROM TARRAWINGEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One day there sped this message
(God send its like again!),
“Good news from Tarrawingee—■
One hundred points of rain!”
And straight it set me seeing
Once more the leaden showers,
The crowfoot and the trefoil,
The everlasting flowers.
I have heard a Melba singing,
I have heard the chanting seas,
I have hearkened to the harping
Of the sea-wind in the trees.
But, when Drought is lord and master,
And fair Hope has drawn aloof,
There is ne’er a music equal
To the rain upon the roof.
Oh, ’tis well I know that music,
And how sweet it is and rare;
For ’twas drought at Tarrawingee
In the year that I was there.
Bitter Drought, and stony hardship
For the man upon the land—
Herbage dead and dust and done for,
And dry creeks on every hand.
For the crow ’twas pleasant quarters,
And ’twas he that battened well;
But for birds of nobler feather
It was Hell, or nigh to Hell.
Aye, ’twas Hell for man and woman,
Though right well they bore their part;
For ’tis theirs the will of iron,
And ’tis theirs the willing heart.
And there’s few 7 could bear their burthen
And be strong, like them, and true,
M ith the sheep and horses dying,
And the big bills falling due.
On the road to Tarrawingee,
In the year whereof I speak,
When the Drought was lord and master,
Gleamed and glittered Campbell’s Creek.
Creek in name! No w 7 ater greeted
Sight or taste—no gleam or sup ;
lor the lands had soaked its treasure,
And the sun had drunk it up.
But ’twas mine to see it flooded
Bank to bank, a torrent brown,
In that year at Tarrawingee,
When the saving rains came down.
It w 7 as this way. No bird whistled
In the hushed, hot, stirless air;
And the trees and saltbush reddened
In the djing sunset glare.
And the white quartz redly glistened
Like crushed china in the ruts;
And the night fell, still and noiseless,
On the homestead and the huts.
It was this way. Sleep had fallen
On the homestead and its cares,
And the night had turned towards morning
When the change came unawares.
First ’twas just a wind that whispered
In the inulga trees, and died ;
Then ’twas mighty thunder rumbling
through the gasping countryside.
Then ’twas rain.... one drop.... another,
And then—O joy of joys !
A,fury of wild music,
A madness of sweet noise!
Then ’twas talk of men awakened—
Did they dream, or had it come?
Had their prayers at last been answered ?
Were the heavens no longer dumb?
Then the noise of many waters
Loud and laughing, low and weak,
And the tramp of restless horses,
And the noise of Campbell’s Creek!
Ah, that noise upon the darkness!
Ah, that message kind and fond!—
Ah, the eager waters speeding
To the Border and beyond!
Ah, the fear of wreck and ruin,
And the hope that stood aloof!
Ah, the music in the night-hours,
And the rain upon the roof!
Lord send I’ll hear to-morrow,
And grow glad of heart again,
Such news from Tarrawingee—
“One hundred points of rain!”

RODERIC QUINN.

“Agricola”: Mention (B. 30/3/T6) of
“snake bread” induces me to ask why the bush kid
labels anything mysterious or repulsive in plant life
as pertaining particularly to the snake. There is a
white, frothy substance exuded at certain seasons
from the bark of the wattle and other small trees,
and caused, I believe, by the irritation of some grub
under the bark. The kids know what it is; it is
“snake spit.” Any flower of a certain unattractive
shade of purple is a “snake-flower,” although none
of the varieties I know flourish in snaky spots. A
thorny creeping plant with striped, green leaves
bears a berry about the size of a bottle-marble. This
is the “snake apple.” A vine here (southern N.S.AV.)
produces sickly-yellow berries known as “snake
berries.” Two varieties of spindle-shanked mush-
rooms, slimy and dull brown, are “snake mush-
rooms.” An evil-smelling, distorted fungus growing
about old stumps is “snake punk,” and a round
fungus, nauseous to behold, of the toadstool breed, is
“snake’s egg.” Truly, the serpent finds it hard to
live down the curse handed him in Genesis.
“Hay”: The fall of downy flakes men-
tioned by- “Alurrabit” (B. 6/4/T6) is common near
Lake Frome, in the north-eastern part of South Aus.
Also, north-westerly from that point not far from the
AfcDonnell Ranges. The silky substance is produced
by a dark, hairy- caterpillar common in those parts,
and known to the blacks as “parappi parooka”
(meaning “walkabout grub”). AVhen these caterpil-
lars are on the move they form themselves into
trains, head to tail, in lengths varying from a yard
and a half to four or five yards, and it is very- inter-
esting to watch a string of them on the wallaby. Brush
one caterpillar out of the train and the whole caravan
stops immediately and waits for the missing link to
right himself. They infest the boonti bush, a
broomy- kind of scrub, and it is when coiled up in a
heap at the foot of the bush which they- feed upon
that they spin the silky material. The wind lifts the
light stuff and spreads it about in large flakes.
Another caterpillar much resembling the ordinary- silk-
worm feeds on the same bush in large numbers and
hangs thousands of silk cocoons from the twigs. The
contents of these cocoons are pale yellow, and look
much the same as mulberry-leaf silk.
■’Mulga” : If “Munguburra” (B. 23/3/TG)
is passing through the Darling Downs at any- time,
let him look into the brigalow and belah scrubs
that abound there, and he will see that there is a
non-venomous as well as a venomous green tree-
snake. AYhere I am the non-venomous specimen
wears white underclothing; the other, red. AYhen
you’re scrub-falling you’ll meet them in scores,
coiled up in the wilga bushes; but they are rarely-
more than 3ft. in length. As to whether the laugh-
ing Jack kills venomous snakes I submit this evi-
dence. AVhile scrub-falling it is no unusual thing to
have a dozen of the birds perched on trees around
waiting for the crash of timber, and as soon as this
occurs the Jack family- are on to the lizards and
centipedes that come to light; but never a snake
have I seen them touch, and snakes are often the
most plentiful of the collection.

Johnson (or cane) grass poisonous? No less than
three instances of dairymen losing cows from feed-

Qualities exist. Sorghums of all kinds in a green
stage are apt to poison stock, but with cane-grass
’ s Q u 'te a common occurrence to feed it down at
stages of growth without any bad results; then,
suddenly, apropos of nothing in particular, a whole
herd is affected. Last month a farmer on the
Hawkesbury put 20 head of cows on a six-acre patch
a a ' Iln thing unusual happened. Two days
l a l el his next-dooi neighbor lan his cows on the
same patch and four skied their hoofs within an

“Sandgroper”: That yarn of “P.L.’s”
about native bees sucking up the perspiration from
the hides of cattle would be all right if the beasts
sweated through their hides. Any cattleman will
inform him that they- sweat only from the pores in
their muzzle.
“Tiaki” : Piri Waitoa was the paramount
chief and brown prosecutor of Taihororia. Billy
Bevans was a pakeha-Maori who loafed on Henare
when not otherwise engaged, and incidentally loved
Kura, the illegal wife of Paturama. A fixture was
immediately- arranged by Piri for adjudicating on a
petition for divorce. English was spoken on Billy’s
account. “You ruv tish ferra Paturama?” asked
the C.J. “No,” said the dusky respondent. “Y'ou
ruv tish ferra Firry- Pewan?” “Yesh,” said the
brown bride. “I giw-it te diworsh. Pilly, he win te
prurry case,” said his Honor, and the decree was
made absolute in three minutes. As a matter of fact,
it was absolute before the Court sat, as Billy had
taken the precaution of briefing Piri with a bottle
of whisky and a quid.

“Markham” explains his change of
address:—■
I was quite all right with the Netherby farpily
for three days, but on the third night things occurred
that convinced me I would have to go elsewhere
for that perfect quiet which the doctor ordered.
There were on the Netherby estate three daughters
and a fond mamma, all agriculturists, all of a cer-
tain uncomeliness, very- good creatures, and most
anxious to please. They- even gagged the poultry to
relieve me of the stress of farmyard chatter. I was
well fed, well bedded, and every prospect pleased.
Only- man was vile, as the sequel proves. It was
past midnight, I had found sleep by the pleasing
process of jumping nine million sheep over a hurdle,
and peace brooded o’er the scene With even mor-e
than her customary broodincss, when the front of
the house fell in. That was a first impression. Later
I discovered that some portions of the front were
still intact. On collecting my senses I came to the
conclusion that an exceptionally powerful dromedary
was trying to paw his way in through the parlor
window. Later I abandoned this in favor of the
belief that a regiment of Germans were busy about
the place with an old Jerusalem battering-ram. 1
heard bad language in a Hunnish guttural, and
female voices in protest; so I went down in pyjamas
and a ’possum rug, and carried an iron bed stretcher
in case the Teutonic invader should have some
special antipathy to red-headed men. From a
strategic position I gathered that a large, angry man
was cutting his way into the house with an axe,
while a shrill woman argued with him from an
upstair window. The aggressor was chopping at the
dining-room wall on the left front, so I betook
my self to the parlor on the right front, and investi-
gated by- way- of the window. I had betrayed my
usual sagacity. A large man was cutting his way
into the house with an axe, and Mrs. Netherby at
the upstair window was expostulating and dropping
ware. The good woman missed him with the basin,
but fetched him with the jug, and Mr. Netherby—
for I have since ascertained it was he—was all out
for the count, during which time two of his large
daughters tried to tow him indoors by the leg. They
got him -half in, when he recovered and fought his
way out again.. Air. Netherby resumed operations
with the axe. Mrs. Netherby- dropped a small dress-
ing-table on his head, and the Netherbv girls pleaded
with him to go in by the door. Papa refused to go
in by the door. He said it was his own house, and
would go in where he damn well pleased. It
pleased him to go in through the dining-room wall,
and he’d go in bv the dining-room wall. He’d teach
them to go shifting the front door about. He’d
let them see how necessary it was to leave the front
door where a man could find it coming home ex-
hausted from his work. Netherby made a hole
through the wall, but the third Miss Netherby stood
on guard inside, and jabbed at him through the
embrasure with a clothes prop, while the second Miss
Netherby provided milk pails for mother to throw
at father. Father secured the prop, lamed the
younger daughter, and continued the task of creating
new exits and entrances to his hannv
milk-pails, buckets and hand-basins !

“Bad Australian”: “Verdad” (r
16/12/’l5) is quite right about the llama, which is
as useful to the dweller in the Higher Andes as th
reindeer is to the Lap. The remnant of the Inca
empire is generally born on a bed of llama skins
covered with a rough hand-woven poncho or blanket
of llama wool; he is cradled and 'carried about bv
his ma in a kind of dilly-bag of the same material -
he finds much of his early nourishment in ll atn a
rnilk, and later he waxes fat on a somewhat odori-
ferous cheese derived from the same source. Young
or healthy llamas are seldom killed, except on
some special occasion, when the whole community
gorges itself and goes on the wildest kind of
crude alcohol jag for several days. The llama
is almost exclusively a pack animal, but it is occa-
sionally used for riding, and it knows its load to
an ounce. Give it an extra kilo, and down it goes
until the pack is adjusted according to the beast's
ideas of what is a fair thing. The llama’s Indian
boss is clothed in a pair of shorts made of a rough
hand-woven material like Harris tweed, a gaudv-
colored poncho, a kind of skull cap and sandais,
the whole outfit harvested from the llama. The
pack-saddle is a bundle of skins of the same useful
animal lashed on by ropes made of its wool, which
is of long, coarse and exceptionally strong staple.
The boss uses the animal’s bones for making
“musical” instruments and correcting the missus
and kids, and when he cashes in he is tucked up in
his shallow grave in an old “poncho” which was
once the natural covering of his universal provider.
When he is forced to pass the night on a round
camp the want of a “corral” doesn’t worry the
Indian. He just rounds his troop up as closely as
possible and passes a hitch of his woollen hawser
around the necks of the animals on the outer circle
and connects up at the starting point. Anchored in
this way the troop makes no attempt to break away,
and the caravan is ready to up-stick and off at any
moment. “Verdad” is not quite correct when hi
says that the beast does not do well below 10,000

feet. I have seen him flourishing like the green
bay tree as low down as 1500 ft. above sea-level I
harbor a deep suspicion that any interested person
may find him performing the same act in Sydney or
Melbourne Zoo. The Indian who infests the Great
Andean Plateau is exactly like a Chow —high cheek
bones, slant eye and pigtail; but he wears two tails
instead of one. It seems a curious coincidence that
the people of the great plateau of Thibet and those
of the Higher Andes resemble each other so closely
and that the most venerated circumstance in both
these elevated lands is the llama.

Business, Bobbery, etc,
“Sarto”: lias Tiie Bulletin a second-
hand wheelbarrow for sale? I want one.
Eight days ago the owner got the invoice
and I got the rail ticket for some goods
despatched from Sydney. To-day the things
turned up. Of course they were not urgent,
being merely drugs for a local (Albury)
chemist. I got another ticket for cement
from Portland, and reckoned the stuff ought
to be along in a couple of days at the out-
side. A fortnight passed and no cement
came. Wired and wrote, and still no
cement. The consignees, who were making
pipes for the sewerage system at Albury,
were lucky enough to beg, borrow or steal
some of the stuff locally, otherwise they'd
have been hung up. Evidently trucks are
kicked off and forgotten until the SAL,
crawling about in the small hours, falls
over one and (if he doesn’t forget) makes
a note of it. Then next morning or two
weeks or' three years afterwards he sends
them on their way. But if I can get a
cheap barrow I intend going for my next
consignment of goods myself.
* * * *
“A.G.8.”: Does anyone know anything
useful concerning smut, especially in barley?
S. Aus. barley is badly affected this year,
more so in the primer samples, and the
“Prior” or Chevalier type is suffering more
than the “Cape.” I have seen crops one
mass of smut in spite of being grown from
seed pickled in bluestone, formalin and all
sorts of patent medicines. One half of a
crop I saw last year was strongly pickled
in brine, and the other half was planted 10
days later without pickle. The unpickled
half was clean, and the other mostly smut.
At the same time, the reverse was noticed
just as frequently.
+ * * *
“Oof Bird”: Perth and the goldfields are
again howling for beef, and the same old
tale about the difficulty of obtaining cattle
from the Nor’-West is told to account for
the shortage. Supplies are at present
brought down by Singapore boats, with occa-
sional shipments in coastal steamers; but
more are wanted, and in view of the large
quantity of beef taken by the transports
and the consumption in military camps the
Federal Government has been asked to lend
the State a ship or two to meet the demand.
All this time the show vessel of the
State fleet, the motor-driven Kangaroo,
whose predecessor, the West Australian,
was bought to smash the beef ring and bring
down the price of meat, is wandering around
the world with odds and ends, and a promise
of a hold full of kerosene for Fremantle on
the return journey. Why isn’t she engaged
in the cattle trade? Is somebody pulling
strings, or is it merely Scaddan’s utter
futility?
“Carrier”: Brown paper—almost the
only sort we make in Australia —took
another leap upwards in price last week.
Presently the people who sell things that
are already sufficiently well wrapped won’t
be so anxious to disguise the customer’s
purchase by swatlfing it in a few extra
folds of brown. As often as not the shop-
man wraps from force of habit, and the
customer waits for the same reason. Most
other times the wrapping is done by
the insinuating salesman who wants
to show oft' his beautiful counter manners
and make dead sure he can't sell you some-
thing else. #
‘•Dyer”: Woman’s a curious cuss. A
sweet thing came to me the other day with
six pairs of hand-knitted socks of a vivid
scarlet and wanted them dyed pearl grey
for the soldiers. She explained that Gen-
eral Orders had declined to let our boys
wear any footwear except khaki or grey.
She had unravelled a last year’s woollen
petticoat, and would I, as a patriot and a
man, and incidentally a dyer, make ’em pearl
grey for her, for nix? I compromised for
khaki, the best shade I could get, made a
fair job of them, and delivered them to her
door. Next day she came along to the shop
and raised Cain —the hoof wear had shrunk
to nothing and weren’t pearl grey, and she
demanded £1 compensation to “go to the
Fund” ! Tom Potstick, my rival in the next
street, I found later, had also been patriotic;
but it cost him only 10s.

A female rushed into the shop for a lamp
chimney and remarked that she belonged to
an anti-Gerhog League was down on
buying enemy products. When the store-
keeper showed her a chimney she waved it
aside: “That’s no good to me if it was made
in Germany.” “Excuse me, madam,” said
the pirate of commerce, “you are mistaken.
It is plainly branded ‘ Made in Saxony.’ ”
“Oh, I beg your pardon,” said the patriotic
female, and produced the cash. “That gag
works every time,” murmured’ his nibs as
he rang up lOd. and started sorting out
some pencils made in Bavaria.
The case of Seurr, a land agent, v. Stout,
Chief Justice of Maoriland, a claim for
commission on the sale of property, recently
decided against Scurr, has been given a
fresh interest by the publication of the
correspondence. New premises were re-
quired for the Public Trustee at Dunedin,
and Scurr, while considering the matter in
his office, was struck with an idea that a
building opposite would be the thing the
Public Trust was looking for. It further
occurred to him that the C.J. owned the
premises, and as time was scarce he wired
to Stout at Wellington, asking the lowest
price, subject to commission. The reply
was: “Willing negotiate. Writing.” The
letter which followed gave no set price, but
indicated an amount somewhere in the
vicinity of £5OOO. Scurr then wrote back,
setting" out the whole position, stating that
he had placed the matter before the Deputy
Public Trustee, and asking for a definite
price; whereupon Stout repudiated Scurr, as
having no client and no authority to act, and
intimated that as the Public Trustee was in
Wellington, he (the C.J.) could if necessary
negotiate with him there. Incidentally he
offered to compensate Scurr for his trouble.
Scurr held that if he were not entitled to
Chamber of Commerce rates of commission
he was entitled to nothing, and, that commis-
sion not forthcoming, he sued —and got
nothing. Under the Land Agent’s _ Act
(M.L.) a land agent must have written
authority to act for the vendor, this to con-
tain an agreement regarding commission.
Therefore the Court held that Scurr had no
standing.
* * * *
“Devilfish”: A now extinct Tasmanian
industry was the “making” of natural ice.
Before freezing machinery came in there
used to be a couple of ice-houses dug out
on Mount Wellington. After a heavy fall
snow w T as shovelled into them, and after
being well beaten and trodden down was
left to freeze. Then the pits were roofed
over with rushes, and when ice was wanted
blocks were hacked out, wrapped in blankets
to keep them from melting, and carried
down to Hobart slung one on each side of
a horse.
* * * *
“D.F.”: There is a better dodge for keep-
ing apples than cold storage. Dig your
apple-house in on the side of a hill so that
one side-wall and most of the two end-walls
arc- formed of the natural earth, and then
roof with some non-conducting material.
‘ Put the apples inside, and they will keep
for months and their flavor will remain
perfect—which some people think is not
the case with cold-storage fruit. R. 11.
Beally, of Kangaroo Talley (Tas.), has an
apple-house of this sort, and he keeps one
season’s apples in good condition till the
next season’s crop is ready.
* * * *
“Moana” : Apropos the shortage of paper
“B.” (B. 30/3/T6) asks why the absurd
and expensive system of sticking postage
stamps on telegrams can’t be dispensed with.
It has been —in Maoriland. All the larger
telegraph offices now have electrically-oper-
ated cash-registers. When you hand in your
message with Bd. to pay for it the clerk
drops the cash into the till and sticks the
telegram form into a slot in the machine.
Then he presses the key marked “8d.,” and
the machine automatically stamps on the
form the time of receipt, the number of the
message and the amount paid. So the cost
of the stamps is saved, the requirements for
audit purposes are complied with, the tim-
ing and numbering is done quickly and
accurately, and the cash received is recorded
and totalled by the machine. The only thing
it doesn’t do is brush the customer’s
clothes and say “Next!”
* * * *
“D.F.”: A new use has been found for
the outer coveting of the paperbark tea-tree
which flourishes in Bananaland and parts
of N. S. Wales. As a packing for eggs and
such like breakable articles it can’t be out-
classed. It is light and as soft as down, and
there’s no chance of the supply running out
for a week or two.
The authorities of the Stock branch of
the Vic. Agricultural Department say they
are unable to account for a sudden appear-
ance of tick among the dogs of Bairnsdale,
Vie. Possibly they are watch dogs.
Now and again, at the Repertory
Theatre, the stage directions indicate “a
mob.” Giegan McMahon gets what he wants
in a few minutes, without any need for the
expenditure of time and wardrobe in dress-
ing the part. He simply sends a messenger
around to the Socialist Hall, where the
“mob” is ready and waiting, and every man
sufficiently sansculotted to fill the gap and
a bit over. Cheap, too.
“C. Ockney”: The war is apparently not
ruining English music-hall managers. The
Coliseum, run by Oswald Stoll, had so much
surplus capital just before the disturbance
that it gave its ordinary shareholders a bonus
of IUO per cent, in preference shares; and
on top of that it has since paid an annual
U 5 per cent. The Moss Empires (known up
till a few years ago as the Moss-Stoll cir-
cuit) has just showed a credit balance of
£150,104, and the Shareholders’ dividend was
S per cent.
J. M. Barrie’s contribution to the Shak-
speare tercentenary is a film version of
“Macbeth,” When Lady Mac wants Duncan
slain she gives Mac the tip in a note, which
is thrown on the screen : “Dear Macbeth,—
The King has gotten old and silly. Slay
him. Yours sincerely, Lady M.” The film
habit of informing the spectators before each
scene what is going to happen is also hit
off. “Old Man Duncan Retires for the
Night” is a typical announcement. The
King hides under the bed. The scrap between
Macbeth and Macduff starts in the courtyard
and finishes on the battlements. Macbeth
is dropped over and falls 100 feet, but. is not
injured and is able to take part in the
happy ending which Barrie has thoughtfully
provided.
Frediswyde Hunter-Watts (Allan Wilkies
leading lady on and off the stage)
comes from the Intellectuals. Two great-
grandpas lie under the stones in W estmin-
ster Abbey. On the distaff side was John
Hunter, known to the present generation
as one of the fathers of surgery.
On the other side was Dr. John W atts, the
famous parson who wrote the things we
chirruped at Sunday-school. However,
Frediswyde didn't go to the pulpit for her
elocution. She learned it from Aorman
Forbes, brother of the beauteous lorbes-
Robertson. So far she has pleased Sydney
best as Ophelia.

The rain is pattering on the roof and rattling on the
window-pane,
The sea booms grandly on the beach, and I’m in
Maoriland again.
For so rain crashed and surges screamed as grim
black night came driving on
When I was very sweet and staid and righteous,
there in Wellington.
Oh, days bygone
Of sleet and slop!
The Muse daren’t hop
In Wellington!
I like to think that in a while when I’m an angel
in the sky
I shall look down on Wellington and see the spin-
drift driving by.
Then in that honeyed clime I’ll smile to see the
people, staggering, shout
About the streets, bedevilled by the climate that
they boast about.
It is not bland —•
Oh, not at all —
The capital
Of Maoriland!
Meantime in Sydney town the rain comes switter-
swishing on the sills,
And with its shifting haze it drapes the beauty of
the tree-clad hills.
Ah me, ah me, how sad to know in autumn time, as
slips life’s sand,
That soon there’ll be no trees at all to take up
room in Maoriland!
With gleaming axe
They’re cleared away.
Which helps to pay
The income-tax.
O Sydney skies, so blue, so blue! O Sydney girls,
so blooming, so
Demure-provocative! I stand secure as in a sooth-
ing glow.
A little pilgrim glad I walk among a goodsome
Christian band
Most circumspect; and when I’m gay I shed a tear
for Maoriland.
0 mind ill-planned,
O senseless block,
Which dares to mock
At Maoriland!
Still on the roof the pattering rain is sweet. It
taps the windowpane.
The sea is angry on the beach, and I’m in Maori-
land again.
So screamed the gale, so surges smashed, as savagely
grim night came on
Once when I was a tradesman slick in dour and
doughty Wellington.
Meek and foredone,
Mid sleet and slop,
The Muse must shop
In Wellington.
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
THE STAGE-DOORKEEPER'S WAIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met him weeping on the Quay;
He turned a woeful eye on me.
“Ah, sir,” he said, “I recognise
One who, I’m sure, will sympathise.
“No doubt you wonder why I weep.
A stage door, sir, I used to keep.
“It was for years arid years my job,
I loved it”—here he gave a sob.
“Though some might say my work was light
’Twas delicate —I ‘checked’ each night
“The actors and the staff, and lacked
Never both courtesy and tact.
“I could prove that, sir, by the tips
I got. But now the great eclipse
“Has come for me —alas! No more
I'll faithful-hound-like guard the door
“When evening-dress swells want to see
The lady stars re lunch and tea!
“No more I’ll boss the letter-rack;
No more I’ll keep an ‘urgent’ back
“Until the time I deem it fit
And proper to deliver it!
“Cry, like Othello, now I could:
‘Mv occupation’s gone’—for good !
“The old shop doesn’t want me —no!
It’s turned into a picture-show!”
VICTORIA.

withstanding the upset caused by the
recent fire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia” Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the Winter
Garden, which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.
