POLITICAL
POINTS
A petition now in circulation in Fiji opens
with “the humble prayer of the petitioners”
which “sheweth that the condition of the
Colony after 40 years of Crown Colony
Government is not satisfactory.” Following
this plain and plump declaration, it sets out
a list of grievances whose variety would
turn a Labor League Conference green with
envy. For instance: —•
The colony is without railways, lias few roads and
bridges, and very inefficient means of communication.
A vigorous public works policy is an urgent need,
a fact that the petitioners respectfully submit the
Colonial Office has never seemed able to grasp.
Finally the petition in sweet frankness says
that “the potentialities of these isles can
only be properly realised by those commer-
cially and personally interested in, and in
understanding sympathy with, the progres-
sive spirit of the Colonies,” and therefore
prays “that the Colony be annexed to the
Commonwealth of Australia or to the
Dominion of Maoriland—to the one or to
the other as the considerations of Imperial
and Colonial policy in the Pacific may dic-
tate.” One little thing, however, the peti-
tioners neglected. They forgot to ask the
two dogs referred to whether they would like
a new tail.
There’s a permanent and reproductive
work lying in a corner at the Hobart rail-
way station. In 1890 it was decided that
the Macquarie-street crossing needed a pair
of ornamental iron gates, so the Government
borrowed £5OO and bought them. Then it
was found they were not wanted and they
have never been used and are not likely
to be. But, 26 years later, the kid, the poor
kid, is still paying the interest bill —or has
fled to the mainland to eseaue it.
The hand of a malign Fate has rested
heavily on the Irish Nationalist cause ever
since it raised its head; but no incident in
the long, weary campaign has been so tragic
as the deliberate striking down by Irishmen
of »tlie prize they had toiled to win, at a
moment when it was within their grasp.
Leaders of men and of causes the world over
have tasted bitter disappointment; hut not
many of them have had to swallow the kind
of draught just presented to John Redmond.
One day lie saw Ireland on the steps of
nationhood once more. On the next there
was nothing for him to do but plead for
leniency for Irish allies of the German.
Almost simultaneously with the news of
the intended stoppage of the export of
indentured Indian labor comes the passage
of a Bill through the Leg. Qouncil of Fiji
providing for the acceptance of a loan of
£lOO,OOO by the Fiji Government from the
C.S.R. Go. “for the settlement of Indians
on the land.” If the Indians are not avail-
able, what then? Some idea of the solu-
tion of this problem may he got from the
announcement that the C.S.R. recently
leased two fine properties, previously con-
trolled by Britishers, to Chinese syndicates,
“the syndicates to import their own labor.”
Evidently that's what’s going to happen to
Fiji.
In Fiji they have no income tax, no land
tax; the Customs House is the only blood-
sucker, and while it leaves the worker a
little pale it only tickles Fat. Fat, in the
form of the C.S.R. Co., recently showed its
appreciation by registering the cut-off half
of its little business (the Maoriland and
Fiji eud) in Fiji. Patriotism, in the shape
of the Fiji Government, effected the registra-
tion for a few hundred pounds; the same
job in Australia or M.L. would have cost
as many thousands. Altogether Downing-
street’s receut administration of Fiji could
do with a lot of investigation. Meantime
the growing cry for an income tax is caus-
ing some of the Fiji Fats a little anxiety.
To the attack made upon Mm by the
P.L.L. Conference Holman had one answer
that he didn’t use. The resolution hinged_
upon the Government’s failure to proceed
with the abolition of the Leg. Council. Last
year’s conference had sustained Holman in
his attitude, such as it was, towards that
Chamber. So the resolution this year could
only have reference to his conduct during the
last 12 months. Now, abolition of the Coun-
cil requires an amendment of the Constitu-
tion. That calls for attention by the British
Parliament, and, taking all things into con-
sideration, it is probable that the man who
rushed that body just now with a Constitu-
tion Amendment Bill would stand a fairly
good chance of being handed over to a warder.
Anyhow, the public is rapidly coming to the
conclusion that to abolish the State Assembly
is about as necessary as to sweep away the
Council. Eight Houses may be better than
14; but eight are six too many.
THE THEATRE OF WAR.
THE VILLAIN: “Gott strafe! I did not make
much of ,a hit in that act.”
Pearce sa'ys there is a lot of stinking fish
about the demand for conscription. So
there is. And Ministers who say that the
people of Australia are not sufficiently demo-
cratic to approve of a measure which the
most democratic countries of Europe have
adopted are the people who cry it.
The first Labor member to disturb Tas-
mania’s House of Dodery has just been
elected. He is Jim McDonald, president of
the F.M.E.A., who was turned down at the
recent Assembly election, but in the fight for
the Council managed to knock out Major
Morrisby for the Gordon seat by 623 votes
to 383. As a set-off. Peter McCrackan, a
veteran who committed the crime last session
of acting as Honorary Minister for the
Laborites, was thrown out in Launceston by
Mayor Ilart, a wealthy old Tory.
THE RESCUE.
HOLMAN: “Gee whiz! That one nearly got
us. Whoa, pet!”
A large number of ambitious X. S. Wales
Labor delegates no longer want the Upper
House abolished. They only want it embel-
lished.
Veteran war-correspondent Yilliers. now
lecturing in Australia, scores his strongest
point with a simple note of interrogation.
Speaking of a British gas attack as being
harmless instead of deadly like the German
chlorined vapor, he turns his head from Ms
reading-desk and asks “Why?” Whereupon
the audience to a man—and a woman —ap-
plauds the querist. Why?
Retired from the Active Service list, the
S. Aus. Early Closing League. Few public
bodies have had such a short and successful
career. It was born in November, 1914. In
the following March it engineered a big State
majority on the referendum for six-o’clock
closing of all bars —the Adelaide Club
always excepted, by special dispensation of
the Fat Man’s Council. Last March the
closing came into effect. So the League is
retiring from work, leaving its parent, the
Temperance Alliance, to keep its eye on the
world.
The tiger is quite a Conservative pet these
days, and the Hobart Mercury calls loudly
for more of him :
We are too fond of applying Socialistic methods
to matters that might well be left to individual
enterprise and of invoking voluntary individual effort
where collective action by the State or Common-
wealth might properly be taken. What idiocy it is,
for instance, to go round with a cap for charity to
provide a regiment with a proper kitchen or a band !
We are not sure that this criticism does not apply
also in a measure to the repatriation movement.
Iu fact, the only difference now between the
Socialists and the Anti-Socialists is that the
Anti-Soshers want to socialise something
else.
When Hagelthorn was Vic. Minister of
Works last year he made an order that a
dozen or so one-horse councils which had
failed to earn a revenue of £l5OO fx-om an
annual Is. rate were to be swallowed by
stronger neighboring shires. Of course all
the one-horse councillors rose up and cursed
the Minister: but the general public ap-
plauded this display of departmental muscle
by a member of the weak Peacock team.
When the portfolios were re-shuffled to let
one or two of McLeod’s crowd in, Hutchin-
son got the Works job and Hagelthorn was
appointed to Agriculture. Now it looks as
if the strong-man turn is not to be finished,
after all. Hutchinson, the new artist, will
allow the shires to live on unswallowed if
they can raise £l5OO per year any old way.
So rates are being hastily shoved up and
re-valuations made in order to keep the little
dutchies going, and a lot of money that the
roads can’t spare is to be spent on unneces-
sary officials and their superfluous frill.

The Victorian crowd has its
trout los, too. George Mead, the
Wesley College schoolmaster
who has grown old in attempt-
ing to enter public life as an
academic Laborite, has been
“blowing the gaff” in regard to
what happened at the confer-
ence in Melbourne. Mead re-
presented the Prahran branch
of the P.L.C.; and speaking
afterwards to his flock he stated
that there were three distinct
tickets run for the places on
the executive—the employers’
section, the Catholic section and

Parliamentary party, the old
executive and the rank and file
straggled along anyhow. But
. , _ Comrade Mead still wears the
beautiful P.L.C. motto on his watch-chain-
“ Each for all. All for each.”

TAKING AN INVENTORY.
“This little pig went to Egypt;
This little pig stayed at home.”
Seasonable warning by the chairman of the
National Bank of Australasia: —
It is to be hoped none of us will be misled bv the
present apparent prosperity, caused by the distribu-
tion of borrowed money.
Simultaneously the Savings Bank of X. S.
Wales reported that the previous months
deposits had been rhe greatest on record.
And on the same . day Holman, mak-
ing his usual monthly remarks about
the finances, had the gall to say
that things were pretty good at the
Treasury, “in spite of the war.” Of course
it is on account of the war that the Treasury
feels good. The fearful pains will arrive
when the wasteful horror has to be paid for.
THE LAST PATROL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlic last patrol of England's fleets
Moves ever, to and fro,
O’er seas where drifting iceberg meets
The southern summer's glow.
A lonely ship—on ocean’s sweep
Of grey, a hull forlorn —
She has her watch and ward to keep
From Leemvin to the Horn.
An east-bound liner saw her pass,
A shape, beyond the mist.
On seas that were like darkened glass
That gleamed with amethyst:
And in the dusk, off Borda Light,
The keepers watched her go,
And saw the royal ensign white
That all seafarers know.
Who says, in pessimistic moods,
That from the Cape to here,
The ships must seek high latitudes
A safer course to steer.?
Who talks of raiders, sweeping south,
To reive our coastal lands?
Strange lights shone off the river-mouth,
You say, across the sands?
Strange lights! And yet no cannon spoke.
The day came, clear and sweet;
The sunlight, through the morning smoke,
Glowed on the waking street.
Oh ! had you watched the sealine grey
Where bitless surges roll,
You would have seen her steam away,
The lonely Last Patrol.
Our fleets in Northern waters cruise,
And every ocean knows
The thunder of the British screws
Where Commerce comes and goes;
And, fighting out front port, at night
A lookout man will see
All suddenly a winking light
Where no light ought to be.
“A British warship!”—watch the Morse
To British steamer speaks;
Follows a word about the course
And news the cruiser seeks.
And then —the night is black again; .
Spray flogs the funnel guys,
And on the bridge the deep-sea rain
Darkens the steersman's eyes.
The Last Patrol of all the fleets
Is she the ship forlorn
That cruises on the lonely beats
From Torres to the Horn ;
Perchance the colliers know her name
That gave her Westport coal;
We only know that in the Game
She is the Last Patrol.
JIAOUILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

NOTIONS
TO OLD JOHN MURRAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sleep well, John Alurra.v, for the long last time,
Forgetful of the personal attack,
The rough cartoon, the disrespectful rhyme ;
Henceforth we will remember you as “Jack,”
The man you were before you turned your coat —-
Not John teetotaller, but Jack tee-Tote.

There was some good racing at Tatter-
sail's meeting in Sydney on Saturday, and the cold
wind was only moderately tempered to the lambs
who were shorn at the A.. 1.0. fixture in the previous
week. AVhile the Cup was being run it wasn’t tem-
pered at all. The race is invariably a disastrous
one for punters, and this time disaster arrived in an
avalanche, with Karanaud King, 6st. 71b., a rank
outsider, well in front. Fantarie, the imported Eng-
lish mare, who had run a good second over a mile
and a half in the Place Handicap the Saturday
before, was backed down to 3’s. Canzone at 4’s.
Karanaud King, occupying a good position all the
way, landed into the straight alongside the favorite,
and, shaking her off at the distance, won with a lot
to spare by a good three lengths, amid a vast, abys-
mal silence. Whereupon Ikey girded up his loins
and withstood a heavy charge of punters, who wanted
to back the imported Celano at 3’s for the Novice
Handicap—l 7 starters. AVhy they backed him the
good Lord only knows. He is a surly brute, went
out only after making emphatic remonstrance, and
ran like a rotter. Anotto (7st. 41b.) and Tartan’s
Double (7st.) fought out the finish. They are a
very decent pair, and ran the seven furlongs in
“27f.”
The flying Poitrina, carrying 9st. 71b., made 18
other youngsters look like carthorses in the youthful
Stakes. She cleared right away from them and won,
easing up, by four lengths' from Hitachi, having
negotiated the five furlongs in lmin. lsec. This
immature lady stands out as the fastest thing of her
sex in Australia just now. But the filly that takes
her jockey’s breath away leaping through the air
in the autumn often doesn’t do it again when the
spring happens along. Poitrina and AVolaroi are
going to be Australia's great national problems when
they are six months older. •
Three, having run into second and third places at
the A.J.C. meeting, had a chance of justifying his
name by winning the Denman Stakes, and thus com-
pleting the sequence. He carried a saddle-cloth with
“3” on it, Ikey W'as yelling 3 to 1 about him, and
his years are 3 in number ; and there is a great fool-
pubiic which pays attention to these things and calls
them “the office.” If it sees a parson it looks at
the race-card and sees the Christian Brother is
to have a go, and calls that “the office,” too—and
generally loses its cash. But the alleged tip to •
hank on Three w'as all right this time. The animal
sprinted to the front early in the proceedings and
stayed there, and the multitude of persons explaining
all about the significance of “3” were loud in the
land while AlacShortodds in a broken voice announced
his readiness to pay the winner. Three ran the six
furlongs at his ov'n pace and passed the judge in
“12f,” half a second only outside the record for the
course put up by Soultline eight years ago. If Fisher
had given him a kick of encouragement Three’s
name wmuld have gone on the hook.
; The McMahon family, one of whom,
James, owns Karanaud King, which romped away
with Sydney City Tattersall’s Cup on Saturday, didn’t
have five bob on the disappointing brute. They
Were justified in their caution. The fact that
Karanaud King was ordered only 6st. 71b. proved
that even the handicapper didn’t think much of his
prospects. Karanaud King was a runner in Sydney
Cup, and after being well up for a mile dropped
right out, and was one of the last two to reach
home. In that race 100 to 1 was vainly' offered to
the public, although the McMahons had a sneaking
regard for their horse’s chance. Thirty-three to one
w'as easily obtainable on Saturday. This Prudent
King colt was the third outsider in succession to
beat the favorite into second place in rich handicaps
in a fortnight. Eurobin started the debacle by win-
ning the Doncaster from AYoorak, and then Prince
Bardolph rifled the public pocket some more by
downing Green Cap.
What mad freak made the public go
baldheaded for the imported nag Celano in Tatter-
sail’s Novice Handicap last Saturday'? One explana-
tion is that he came down from Newcastle, and any-
thing on four legs from that quarter seems to be
regarded as worth risking a wad upon. Yet
the wretched nag had tried on 13 occasions to
win a race at Newcastle and never once succeeded.
Patrick Aloses w'as so obsessed by' the seriousness
ot the matter that he opened up by offering 6 to 4
againt Celano. Then he warily lengthened out to
7 to 4, and not being knocked off his perch took
courage and called “3 to 1 the field.” Then the
money tumbled in. The mad-headed brute gave the
ALL. jockey Low r e a rough passage round. In the
circumstances, the man on board did well to bring
Celano into fourth place even though he was many
lengths behind the winner.

Duke Alwyne looks like making a great
hid for the Newcastle Cup again this year. Twelve
months back, with 7st., he won the event. This
time the titled animal has 41b. less on top, and on
Saturday demonstrated that everything was O.K.
with his feet by easily securing Boolaroo Alay Handi-
cap, conducting Bst. over a mile and a quarter in
2min. lOsec. AVhereupon punters unloaded some of
their winnings in supporting the Duke for the Cup.
The improvement worked on Blague in a
few w'eeks is remarkable. Just prior to the New-
market Handicap, which the nag won in 1915,
Blague was given a full-dress rehearsal in Aloonee
A’aliey Trial Stakes. Odds were laid on him in a
small field, and he ran like a goat. On Saturday he
won the Goodwood Handicap (S.A.), of 700 sovs., with
ridiculous ease. Horses well behind him were lownif
(third), which occupied a similar position in the
last Newmarket, Achernar, Aliss Bob, Aliss Neckersgat
and Tringa, all of which ma.v he considered class
enough to be given a cut in the next big dash down
FJemington straight this y'ear. Blague is the
property of one of the Svmes, who races under the
name of “S. A. Rawdon.”

“Musketoon” : For years I have been get-
ting tons of excitement cheap—trying to back every
winner at every race meeting I visit. The recipe is
simple. If the day is a bad one my loss is never
more than 55., so there is no danger of my being
put in the bull-ring for hooking the boss’s cash to
square up with. On the first race I dump ss. on the
most likely-looking sort. If it loses —no more bet-
ting for me that day! AVhen it gets home first I
plank the winnings on the next race and keep on
doing it. On Doncaster day at Randwick I backed
five winners and ended up square. I had ss. on
The Spire in the Hurdles, four to one, and, pocketing
55., put the £1 on Reputation in the Autumn Stakes,
and scored £5 10s. Backing two-year-olds is not
a strong point with me, but, all the same, bang
went the £5 10s. on Thana,' which once more landed
the bacon. AlacShortodds thought I had been
drinking when £59 10s. went on Eurobin at 33 to
one, but he laughed on the other side of his ear
when I called round to collect £2023. It seemed
risky putting two thousand odd on Kandos to beat
Cetigne and Patrobas, but I chanced it, and at six
to one collected £14,161. Then it took a lot of per-
suading to coax myself to jamb the whole lot on
the last race, but as a faint heart never broke a fat
bookmaker, I fired the whole £14,161 on Sy'dian in
the Highweight. The wretched animal finished
last, but Ikey' had a narrow squeak all the same of
being asked for £84,966.
“Trot”: How much is riding on Austra-
lian courses affected by' (1) the blindness of authority',
(2) the reckless conduct of horsemen, (3) the fear
jockeys have of the consequences of reporting inter-
ference? The number of times the public hears of a
jockey reporting another for try'ing to empty him out
of the saddle on the journey is mighty small. The
number of times jockeys have been blamed for poor
judgment in riding is pretty considerable. But
neither the public nor the stewards always know the
kind of milk that is in the cocoanut. Jockeys don’t
report one another more often because they have to
ride again, and want to be reasonably sure there are
not a dozen or so others with a “snout” on them.
If there is the ride may not be pleasant, or some-
thing may happen suddenly off the course at night
with odds of 12 to one.

In 1914 there were 301 flat-racing fixtures
in England. Last year the total was pruned hard to
96 ; this year the shears have been put in again and
another 31 roll off.
Amid the clash of a world at war someone
found time to cable from London that the famous
mare La Fleche was dead. Owned by Baron Hirsch,
who wasn’t born in Aberdeen, La Fleche won several
classic turf events in ’9l-’92, but was beaten in the
Derby.
John Bull’s Government will shortly be
racing prads, as well as breeding and owning them.
Colonel Hall AA’alker having presented his stud to the
Government, with a view to breeding nags suitable
for army work, it has become necessary to find
out what's w'hat. So several of the most promising
two-year-olds in the stud have been leased to Lord
Lonsdale, who will train and race them under an
arrangement that the Government takes half the cash
they earn. Will the State go the whole hog and
place a vote on the Estimates for putting a bit on
the prads with MacShortodds ?
When the Moana swung into Wellington
(M.L.) the other day from U.S.A. she had something
faster than the ’Frisco mail on board. This was the
pacing stallion Copa de Oro. The animal was im-
ported by Charlie Hudson, of Dunedin, who expects
to produce a string of flying machines, because the
nag is credited with having paced a mile in lmin.
59sec.! This time was never “passed,” but he has
an authenticated record of 2min. lsec., which puts
him in the front rank. He is the sire of the famous
Rayo de Oro, the champion three-year-old pacing
gelding of Fried Niggerland.
Ten seconds after the starting gong
sounded at Sydney Stadium on Saturday Air.
Edward Alov, of the U.S.A., was getting beans. Air.
Herbert AlcCoy, of Victoria, was assaulting him with
great fervor and continued doing the same thing
until the first interval arrived, whereupon Mr. Aloy
took a seat, had his nose mended with collodion
and listened to some earnest advice about where to
keep his face. He looked as cheap as peanuts. They
had run into clinches in* that first round, and at
infighting the Yank wasn’t worth tuppence. AlcCoy
got all over him with hard, short jolts which nearly
knocked his head off. For 10 rounds the A’ietorian,
finding that at close quarters Aloy was practieallv
helpless, carried the battle there and gave the
stranger a really good hiding. At this stage the
argument looked like ending abruptly, though Moy
didn’t look cheap any' longer. He had shown that
he could box with fine skill, could use bis feet with
speed and judgment, and had tremendous vitality.
Therefore, instead of being woodened, he started to
hold his own fairly well, and ir. the next few rounds
commenced to make McCoy wear a worried look. By'
the end of the sixteenth he was master of the situa-
tion, and for the rest of the journey had the other
fellow alternately hanging on or on the run. At
the end there was nothing for Scott to do but give
McCoy his benediction ; but the winner was mighty
glad the disturbance had finished. Luckily for
McCoy, Aloy had found out too late how to fight him.
The stranger is not decorated with a beauteous
dial bv nature, and after a hostile glove had laid
an eye open, cut his nose across and burst his lips,
Mov was no more handsome than before. But this
youngster has a big heart and extraordinary stamina,
and is a better boxer than McCov, and if the pair
meet again the Australian may not get away with
the bacon as he did on Saturday. Moy was com-
pletely demoralised at first, and was a long time
discovering that McCoy is singularly open to attack
with the right. He seems to have ifo defence against
this and no great liking for a welt on the jaw. But
he would beat Afoy every time they met unless the
other fellow either learned something in the in-
terval about infighting or interposed a smack on the
jaw before it took place.
“Roughhouse” Burns got away with
Matt Murphy’s scalp at Brisbane on Saturday.
Matthew stood a severe battering in the sector where
he keeps his grub, and at the end of the tenth
batter reckoned things had gone far enough. The
iron-visaged Canadian smiled agreement, and went
to get ready for a scrap with Ferdinand Quendreux.
“Masetto” : In the good old days pugs had
a reserve blow in the shape of a butt with the head.
At Andover, in 1834, Sam Evans, “Young Dutch
Sam,” fought Gavnor, “the Bath carpenter.” Sixteen
rounds had occupied two hours, and both men were
sparring for wind. Suddenly Gaynor caught Evans
by the hands, but that astute general, instead of
wrestling, dashed his dome full in Gaynor’s face.
Evans followed up his bull-goat act by dealing out
port and starboard sheol, and next round the timber-
merchant was easily finished. Evans’s elegant style
of butting was declared foul in subsequent ring legis-
lation.
For their 10-round no-deeision assault at
New York recently Willard received £13,150 ( £1270
as his share in the motion pictures) and Moran col-
lected £5875. Total takings amounted to £37,500.

“Cleido”: When “Sam” Cassidy captured
the Stawell (Vic.) Handicap, the blue ribbon of
professional sprinting, bn Easter Monday he regis-
tered his first big win over the Sheffield distance
(130yds.). Starting off 131yds. he rushed over the
remaining distance in 11 3-ssec. If the time is
correct he ran Jyd. inside “even time.” Which is
rapid travelling, and gives the men on the virtual
scratch mark (7yds. and Byds.) less than Buckley’s
chance of getting the cash. Hitherto Sam’s chief
pastime has been arriving home first in 220vdff. and
440yds. doubles, at which kind of flutter he is a
topnotcher.
* * * *
G. B. Shaw, who left England recently to
take whatever is waiting for fine athletes and good
fellows, was a world-famous hurdler in his day. Ama-
teurs and pros, everywhere took their hats off to
Shaw, for he was the first man to leap over 120yds.
of obstacles in less than 16sec. ’Way back in the
’Bo’s a slim young man arrived in Maoriland from
England with a lot of beautiful clothes, a pair of
running shoes and two flying feet, took a job as a
clerk with the N. Z. L. and M. A. Co. at Timaru, and
started to pursue the jam-dish and toast-rack.
Heavens, how he could run over hurdles! He was
easily champion of Maoriland, and, after a few years,
went back to London with a crate of e.p. ware, and
commenced showing the world’s best his heels.
Each year from ’93 to ’96 he won the 120vds. cham-
pionship at Stamford Bridge, his best time being
15 3-ssec., a really wonderful performance then, and
only beaten since by the merest fraction. Perhaps
his mightiest effort was when he won a handicap from
15vds. behind scr. in 17sec. Death beat him at 51.

Motor-cycle records got another tilt lately.
In the 200-mile toot at Mortlake (Vic.) H. Parsons
whizzed over 101.7 miles in 97min., and thereby
w r rote a new line in the book ; while the full journey,
by N. G. Duckett, in 3hr. 40min. 42sec. knocked
out Yankee Baker’s Australian record of 3hr. 41min.
E. Absolom put up a very good motor-
cycle dash the other day in an effort to catch the
Rotomahana with a supplementary mail before she
got out of the Tamar. AA'ith 201 b. of letters slung
over his shoulder -he left Hobart at noon, and bv
4.55 p.m. had covered the 160 miles to Beauty
Point, though the roads were muddy and in bad
shape. He missed the boat, after all, but only by
10 minutes.
According to The Horseless Age. there
are 3,114,000 motor_ vehicles on the planet. Of these
2,400,000 are in U.S.A. Britain comes next with
276.690. France has 98,400, Germany 71,455, Aus-
tralia 20,350. Maori];)nd, with a population of only
one million, had 10,010, or one machine for everv
100 inhabitants. On a Population basis Hicketty-
ville stands second to AVilson’s country, which had
one car for every 45 residents.
“Barcoo”: Met a once well-known snort
in Emerald (Q.) a week or so ago—wWi
man Bill Richmond, whom Plugger Bill Marti
“discovered” at a cycling carnival in" Rrk
bane. Plugger Bill made no mistake when he lobbed
on the Glasgow boy, who had perfected his riding
in the Sunny South. He induced Richmond into the
professional ranks, and took him to America where
they still talk of the unbeatable “Caldo” in’ handi
caps. He got away with no less than 37 of these
straight out, five of them in succession in New
Orleans City. With Martin attending to the
sprints, and “Caldo” making no errors in the handi
cap, bags of dollars flowed to the treasury. He
looks just as fine a type of man as the day I saw
him sprinting over the winning mark at New
Orleans like a flash from a gun.

* * * *
The Vic. “Head of the River” races were
rowed on the Barwon (Geelong) last week. The
heats were won by Wesley, Geelong and Melbourne
Grammar. The final was spoilt by a collision. The
river is not wide enough for three crews, especially
with schoolboys at the tiller. Wesley, coming out
too wide at a bend, collided with Geelong Gram-
mar, and was disqualified. Then the two Grammars
rowed, and Melbourne was the winner. This is the
first time the Dark Blues have been in front.
Wesley reckoned it was badly treated, and, anyway,
the Barwon will probably never be the scene of the
race again.
“Bad Australian”: Away back in 1914
every aviator in South America had notions about
flying across the Andes, but the death of that fine
athlete and sport, engineer George Newberry, when
his ’plane fell with hiip at Mendoza, coupled with
Macias's miraculous escape when attempting the
flights from Uspallata a few weeks later, seemed to
have put the kybosh on the hopes of the bird-men.
But another risk-merchant has arisen in the land.
His name is Bradley, and he proposes to cross the
ranges in a balloon accompanied by a military friend.
They are now busy in Buenos Aires getting the gas-
bag in condition. There is one circumstance which
Bradley appears to have overlooked, and that is that
the winds which fan the summit of the Andes always
blow from the Pacific, and how he expects to claw
across in the teeth of an Andean zephyr beats me.
What he should do if he means business is to
inflate his balloon somewhere on the Pacific coast,
take in about a month’s provisions and let her
drift. His undertaking as at present outlined looks
about as hopeful as old Vanderdecken’s attempt to
punch around the Cape. Of course, starting from
the coast the air vehicle is liable to be blown out
to sea by some unexpected air current. So if, during
the next month or two, you should observe a strange
bird hovering over The Bulletin office, don’t worry;
it will only be Bradley making his landfall after a
somewhat trying voyage.
* * * *
The game of billiards is of very respect-
able antiquity in Australia. According to the general
muster of the inhabitants taken in 1800, just before
the departure of Governor Hunter, Sydney found
employment for one billiard-marker. He was bracketed
with a musical-instrument maker, each being the
only representative of his kind the settlement could
boast of.

Colonel Cody, the Buffalo Bill of the
penny dreadfuls and Wijd West business, has
quitted showmanship and is running a sum-
mer resort at a ranch in the mountains
of Wyoming (IJ.S.A.). Bill houses his boarders either
at his ranch or in cabins and tents on the place.
His idea is to cater for actors, vaudevillains and
show people who want a rest after a fast season.
Sydney bowlers have worked off two more

sham) by 35 —14, from which the skipper got a

Colquhoun.
“Bass” : What is known as big game fish-
ing lately commenced a new season in N. S. Males.
At certain times the estuaries on the coast Broken
Bay, Port Hacking and Port Stephens—swarm with
opulent, useless fish, including tunny, Spanish
mackerel and such like. The Angling Club, mostly
doctors, lawyers and bankers of sporting tastes, spends
much time and money in toying for these members
of the Finny Legislative Council with rod and reel.
The method adopted is to hire a motor-boat ot tan
speed and to patrol likely ground. Then when you
get a strike the launch driver has the time of his
innocent life to help land the fish. AAhen two men
on the same launch strike together there is a biggo
circus than Wirths’. I went out with one of these
boats t’otber week-end. We had a good day, an
when we started for home we were well out at sea,
nothing in sight and a bit of a jobble. The lonely
aspect of the outfit suddenly came home to me. anc
asked the man in charge a few questions. He ha
on board neither air-tanks, lifebelts, fire-extinguishei,
spare oars nor boat for emergency. So I thought
would liven up the picnic. “Any of y’ou chaps kn w
Larsen?” They didn’t. “Who was he?” I explained

jumped overboard. Someone asked was Ins .

far as we are.” One by one pipes went out am

source of never-ending annoyance—then chronic ill-health.
Judicious exercise is the key of health.

"D.F.” : Ever since Sprent’s triangulation
of Tasmania, more than 50 years ago, Cradle
Mountain with 5069 ft. has been reckoned
nearer Heaven than anything else in the
Speck, till a year or tw r o ago someone
measured up Ben Lomond again and made
it 5150 ft. Now the Cradle has dropped
back to third, for another scientific gent,
has bumped Barn Bluff, a few miles away,
up to 5115 ft. Some people have a theory
that the Frenchman’s Cap is higher than
any. But none of them is within cooee of
the pile of the drifting little island’s unpro-
ductive debt. There is so much of that
curse that even in a good year all the public
works and services don’t earn 75 per cent,
of the interest bill, and 40 per ceut. of all
the revenue goes to keep Mr.. Cohen from
putting the bailiff in.
Sam Johnson foresaw Zeppelin raids
away back in the 18th century. The in-
ventor in “Rasselas” objected to teaching
the world the art of flying, which he had
discovered, and gave his reasons: —
If men were all virtuous, I should with great
alacrity teach them to fly. But what would be the
security of the good if the bad could at pleasure
invade them from the sky? Against an army sailing
through the clouds, neither walls, mountains nor
seas could afford security. A flight of northern
savages might hover in the wind, and light with
irresistible force upon the capital of a fruitful
legion.
Henry Lucy mentions in his final notes as
“Toby, M.P.,” in London Punch, that of
Lord Russell’s Government of 1866 there is
only one survivor—Lord Eversley—and of
Gladstone’s first Ministries of 1868-1874,
only two —Eversley and Lansdowne. Sur-
veying a crowded Commons he could discover
only one survivor of those who first greeted
Gladstone as Prime Minister. So 48 years
has made practically a clean sweep of the old
identities among the Commoners; they are
either dead or in the peerage—both of which
are useless occupations.
How effective is likely to be the scheme
of crushing German trade in Australia by
getting emotional dames to take an oath not
to buy enemy goods has been illustrated
by a firm of chemists in Hobart. They ran
a well-known line of German-made soap,
and a while back put up placards in the
following terms :
Enemy goods.—Let us wash our hands of them.
This soap was bought in 1913, and is now offered at
specially-low prices to clear.
The scheme was so successful that it was
applied later to another line, which was
mopped up with equal celerity.
When Cecil Rhodes founded his scholar-
ships he made room not only for Americans
but, after seeing how keen his friend the
Kaiser was on the study of English, for a few
Germans, too. His hope was that his scheme
would promote a British-German-Ameriean
understanding which would ensure the
peace of the world. Rhodes had been in his
grave only about a dozen years when the
Germans went mad and were soon at death-
grips with the British, with U.S.A. writing
notes to each in turn. Now most
of the German ex-scholars are with
the Hun forces, and doubtless able
to render specially good service owing
to the experience they were paid by
the Rhodes Trust to get at Oxford. Of
the British scholars 167 are known to have
enlisted. Only 18 are still left at Oxford,
and five of these are going for commissions,
four have been turned down by the doctors
and six are studying medicine and have been
advised to stick to it for the present. At
least six scholars are known to have been
killed, including A. Wallace (M.L.) and A.
P. Turnbull (W.A.).
From L. E. Hubbard, Tasmania’s crack
rifle-shot: —■
I am sorry the par. (B. 20/4/T6) about me turns
out to be wrong. The facts are that, though I had
failed to get any medical officer to pass me for the
A.1.F., in spite of all efforts since the very early
days of the war, I was called upon to submit to
medical examination under the war-census scheme.
The examining doctors sent my papers up with a
recommendation for a special case, and on the
strength of this my name was included in the list
of men passed as fit. But the higher medical
authorities have now refused to break a rule and
have therefore rejected me. As far as I can make
out, the chief objection is that, being one-eyed, I
would be a totally-disabled soldier, and qualified for
a pension as such, in the event of my losing my
remaining eye.
Born in Canada 28 years ago, Captain
Dick Gould, the mariner who shoves the 84-
ton ketch Hawk across Bass Straits, has
had experiences which would serve most men
of 60. Two years ago he salved part of the
cargo of the s.s. Norfolk, which was breaking
up on the Ninety-Mile Beach. Another
time his engines broke down, and the ketch
drifted across the straits and bumped the
Flyspeck, but was brought off undamaged.
In 1915, a gale swept away the rudder, the
cook, a seaman and the lifeboat. Only the
cook came back. Afterwards Gould himself
was washed overboard, taking the steering
wheel with him. When he came on board
again he found the engine-room flooded.
Going from Tasmania to Geelong with 4000
cases of apples, 21 stormy days were spent
instead of the usual six, and a third of the
cargo had to be jettisoned. In January the
Hawk grounded in 7ft. of water at Black-
man’s Bay, but was righted after several
days’ hard graft, in the course of
which a boat was overturned and the
crew had to swim ashore. Two months
ago he was upset from another boat,
but his luck was still good. Gould
takes all these things with philosophy, the
only occasion on which he became eloquent
being when a deck-hand who spoke no Eng-
lish started a winch which the skipper was
adjusting, and took off a finger. The deck-
hand learned a word or two that day.

A French Proverb
runs like this: “It is the tone that makes the song." (C’est
le ton qui fait la chanson) and in English we say, "It is
the way it is said or sung that makes the difference.”
If the tone makes the song, it certainly makes the Piano,
for most people will choose a Piano from their appreciation
of what they consider the right tone. In approaching
The BEALE PIANO
the first notes struck create in the hearer a sympathetic chord
of appreciation- a sort of definite acceptance of the instrument
as having passed the first and most important test.
Other tests, such as touch, responsiveness and sympathy, as well as general
beauty of case are matters which easily tall into line once the tone has
been pronounced perfect.
And this leaves one thing for decision. Is this beautiful tone permanent ?
And we guarantee that it is that it will positively improve as the Piano
matures, and be a constant and never-ending pleasure to live with.
We can help you to pay for one by allowing you full value for your old
instrument, and giving you extended time to pay off the balance.
We give a Life Policy, if desired, to every purchaser, which provides that if
the hirer, with intent to purchase, should die before the completion of
payments, the Piano will become the property of the person named in the
Policy without further payment. Call and let us talk the matter over with you.

on sinful land values. That is the chief basis of The

A pathetic person with a Scotch name
was convicted and remanded for sentence in
Sydney the other day. When the Judge asked
the gaol recorder if there were any pre-
vious convictions, the man in the dock saved
all parties trouble by admitting: “I have
been in gaol all my life!” The little jobs
he pleaded guilty to this time were done on
January 3. January 4 and February 31. On
February 12 he had fled from custody, and
one of his “previous” was 15 years for
shooting with intent. The heavy-footed Law
spends a great deal of its time catching and
releasing poor devils who can’t or won’t go
straight; but it doesn’t look really foolish
til. one of its own customers stands up in
court and confesses’that he has nearly worn
out a gaol on his own account without get-
ting any nearer social salvation or becoming
a top-hatted rogue in a large- way of busi-
ness.

Kodak “N.-C.” Film,
always dependable
Safeguard your photographic results by making sure that it is genuine Kodak film with
which you load your Kodak. Look for “X.-C.” on the package and “Kodak” on the
spool end.
Made in Australia
and always thoroughly fresh and dependable.
Reart what “The Bulletin” of 13th April, 1916, says:
“Who has anything to say against the Australian-made photo film? When buying some
in Sydney a few months ago I was told that the Australian film would not travel
through the tropics. But I got some, had good results with them on the voyage to Egypt,
and later during the long, sweltering run to Colombo. There, the supply being nearly
exhausted, I got more —English this time, and ‘specially made for the tropics.’ The
results from the few remaining Australian reels, even after their long trip, were much
better than the results from the special English stuff.”

IN BANANA LAND
The Queensland Railways have just done
a brilliant piece of business. Irately they
called tenders for linseed oil, raw and boiled.
Meggitt’s, who are putting a tremendous lot
of energy into the vegetable-oil industry in
Australia, tendered at a price which they
knew must give the Railway Department an
advantage of about 6d. a gallon over Eng-
lish brands of equal quality. When the
tenders were opened, however, it was found
that an Indian oil was offered at a penny
per gallon less than the Australian ; and the
amazing Department accepted it. So it
seems that there are railway bosses in Aus-
tralia who think they are best off by en-
couraging people to live in India or some
other foreign country and use foreign rail-
ways. While there are such bosses it
plainly isn’t safe to leave the Customs tariff
on linseed oil at the present sixpence a gal-
lon. Incidentally, the Australian workman
is paid 10s. a day 7 for work for which the
Indian coolie gets 10s. a month; and the
Queensland Labor Government is apparently
willing to allow one of its Departments to
give preference to the lOs.-a-month laborer
even when his product is only a
penny a gallon cheaper than that of the Aus-
tralian lOs.-a-day workman. How the
Queensland Labor Government expects the
lOs.-a-day standard to -be maintained when
preference is given to the cheap foreign
colored person The Bulletin doesn’t know.
Neither does the Queensland Government.
Eieut.-Colonol Bailey, a Gallipoli man,
and “one of a number of officers recently
demobilised bv the Defence Department for
purposes of economy,” shot himself at the
Commercial Travellers’ Club in Brisbane the
other morning. 11 is dismissal, it is said,
preyed on his mind. Tiie Bulletin knows
nothing of Bailey’s case; but the treatment
they are getting on their return to Aus-
tralia is preying on the minds of a good
many officers. There are one or two men
in large jobs under the Defence Department
who- are fighting for the employment of re-
turned men ; but they are not able to make
much progress. To put it quite plainly a
very large number of those officers who
haven’t moved outside Australia, but have
entrenched themselves in safe, easy jobs, are
making a dead-set against the returned
officers—men who have been invalided back
from the Front —and the men who have
stayed at home seem to be able to pull most
strings at present. The fact that the Censor-
ship and the instructions under which the
Censors act are in the hands of such officers
doesn’t make it any easier for the public to
get a grip of what is going on.
“Cleveland”: In 1884 Fred Johnstone
(present Mayor of Charters Towers), Dave
Edwards, wheelwright at Townsville, and
Harry Walker took out an improvised target
in a cart at Charters Towers and established
the first rifle-range in the North. John-
stone lias the distinction of having sworn
in more than 700 recruits from the North,
and it was he who hit upon the idea of pre-
senting a silk handkerchief to every Billjim
enlisting at the Towers and to every native
of the village sworn in elsewhere. The
hanky bears the seal of the city of defunct
goldmines, and has been claimed from all
corners of the earth. Johnstone foots the
Dill himself, and has so far paid out over
£9O.
Died in England recently, W. A. Diggens,
who rectored in Rockhampton for many
years and raised most of the cash to build
the Anglican Cathedral there. When Dr.
Dawes was made the first bishop of the new
see in 1890, Diggens, who had expected that
the gaiters would come his way, shook off
the dust of the ungrateful city from his
feet, and went to Maoriland, where he was
a canon of Dunedin Cathedral. Since 1896
he had been expounding Anglicanism to a
population of mainly-Meihody Cousin Jacks
in Cornwall (Eng.).
A PREMATURE QUERY.
MEAN UNCLE (to nephew): "D'ye think baby's like me, Ithaac?"
ITHAAC: "I dunno. He ain't got nothin' to give awag yet."
Tom Ingham, one of the survivors of the
Maria tragedy of 44 years ago, rolled up to
the Historical - Society the other night to
give a first-hand account of how the New
Guinea bubble burst. The leaky cutter left
Sydney with about 75 men aboard to go to
the new Eldorado, where gold was sup-
posed to be lying about in lumps. When
the cutter put to sea it was discovered that
the captain and mate knew nothing about
navigation, and that the Maria was leaky
and could only be kept afloat by all hands
manning the pumps. Several days out the
captain unsuccessfully tried to make the
Solomons; then he went in search of the
New Hebrides, New Caledonia and Moreton
Bay in turn. Finally, he discovered a hid-
den reef near Cardwell, and only a handful
of them, of whom Ingham was one, got
ashore to face hunger and the native sav-
ages. For days they dodgt d boomerangs
and spears and pleasantries of that sort,
till they gave up in despair. At last a ship
hove in sight and they were rescued. But
the bubble had long since burst.
“A.D.”: “Boro Belle’s*’ big shout at
Mackay <B. 13/4/’l6) is small potatoes
for the North. During election time
in ’BB Fred T. Wimble and R. A.
Kingsford, canvassing the Cairns dis-
trict, held meetings on the second section of
the Cairns-Herberton railway, then in course
of construction. After a meeting a refresher
often counted 300, and more than once ex-
ceeded 400. Also when W. Martin went
over the Barron Falls in ’9O the body was
recovered some three days after, hauled up
with ropes from the bottom of the falls,
about 800 ft., and carried by relays of men
to Ivamerunga. The subsequent “shout”
cost £24 10s. = 9SO drinks, and I know the
bill was fairly correct. I counted the crowd;
the contractors (through their riding ganger)
paid.
“Camp Horse” : Full your muzzle out of
the feed-box and listen to this. At a patri-
otic orgy up our way a loyalist whose bank
balance goes three laps to tlie mile donated
one complete pony to be sold for the good
of the national heroes. The animal was
sold and resold to the extent of £5O before
the last bid arrived. As it happened the
man who got it was an employee of the
original owner, and that potentate was so
anguished at seeing his own hired man in
possession that he dropped a hint to the new
owner that he had no receipt for the moke.
The employee thought it over behind the hay-
stack, and eventually decided to stop his
boss’s mouth and stay his boot by handing
the patriotic horse back.
From J. Avery, one of the late artist’s
schoolmates:—
Hilder won a State scholarship in ’94 from the
\ alley School with three mates-—Jack Boast, Percy
Best and Bob Brennan. I can see them now—Hilder
always scribbling, Best a maniac on old music,
Brennan a very powerful athlete always smoking-
cigarettes, and Boast the possessor of a remarkable
soprano vojc% At lunch-hour Best would provide
the music, and Hilder would scribble spine words to
go with it. If any lad ventured to interrupt
Boast while the voice mounted higher and higher
some biting sarcasm from Hilder or one threaten-
ing movement by the powerful Brennan would
silence the scoffer. Brennan died by his own hand
in inland at the age of 20 ; Best was passed out by
cpnsumption ; now poor Hilder is gone of the same
scourge. I wonder if Boast—the last of a remark-
able group of four —is still living?
The Wolf and His Own!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Goth is away on the war-path to-day,
The Hun has his bayonet bare;
The Turk lias his dirk in a merciless work ;
The Bulgar is out of his lair;
The Eagles are screaming and glad in the
blue,
And down by the smoke there's a groan,
And deep in the forest there's howling for
joy—
The Wolf has come to his Own.
Too long has. he slept on the rights of his clan
And thirsted in vain for the red
Hot river that leaps like a joy to his throat
When War-lord and Rapine are wed ;
The lust in his soul has been sated at last,
lie peels the good flesh from the bone.
And blood-drunken yelpings proclaim to the
blast
The Wolf has come to his Own!
Afar on the Vistula master is he,
He prowls on the skirts of the blaze ;
His banquet at Yule was the bodies that lie
Unburied by Danube and Maas;
By Scheldt and by Struma, by Niemen and
Bug.
They’re lifting a Kaisorlieh throne
And scribing above it in letters that burn —-
“The Wolf hath come to his Own.”
The Goth is away on the war-path to-day,
The Hun hath Bis babies to drown :
The Turk hath his dirk in a murderous work,
The Bulgar is burning a town ;
The Vultures you startle, they scarcely can
rise
So sated they he and so blown,
And Ruin tells Death with a bating of breath
“The Wolf hath come to his Own!”

It was owing to her efforts that Toowoomba

Flic Y.W.C.A. opened a new hostel the
other day in which business girls will he

Another batch of wounded and invalided
men came home on Friday night. Amoiw
them was .Major Hill, who won the Mili-
tary ( ross. But he is the unhappiest sol-
dier in the world if anyone refers to it.
The star performance of Anzac Day was
contributed by Toowoomba. In the Downs
city they have a moss-grown piece of iron-
mongery that passed as a cannon in the good
old hlackbirding days. In a burst of patri-
otic fervor someone dragged out the ancient
blunderbuss, oiled its whiskers, and ordered
it into action. The gay old gun responded
joyously. It awoke with a reverberating
roar that smashed windows, wrecked plants
and made, a general mess of Toowoomba’s
prize conservatory.
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Analjst" : ] )engue fever, which has been
shaking our bones up, has crossed the border
and is giving Sydney a turn. It takes a lot
of getting rid of, especially the hacking
cough, but an old hand up North recom-
mends this: Two ripe persimmons skinned
and squashed, two grains, of sulphate of
quinine, two tablespoonfuls of rum, the mess
mixed with milk and taken at bedtime.
Banana land is still busy manufacturing
Anzacs. A seven-year-old youngster named
Boebig got lost in the bush near Wulltim-
billa, out Iloma way, recently. lie was
barefoot, bareheaded, and dressed only in
a shirt and knickers, and the weather was
wet and stormy. After eight days of anxious
search he was found “walking along uncon-
cernedly” in the thick and dingo-infested
scrub. Though dazed with hunger he was
otherwise quite 'well, and had managed to
keep going all that lonely time on wild
berries.
One of the features of Anzac Day at
Townsville was the turn-out of the original
members of the Naval Brigade. There were
about 30 of them, and they sent for their
old C. 0., Lieut. W. A. Ackers. R.N.R., to
knock them into shape. This he did in a
style which showed that he and his men
still have the old habit about them. The
veterans, all of them near 60, made an im-
pressive guard of honor.
The Lands Department estimates that the
pear pest has cost the State 16 millions
sterling so far. In many districts the afflic-
tion is carrying on a campaign of depopula-
tion. Instance, the shire of Taroom, with
an area of five million acres. Its total male
population has fallen off to 425. Through
this rich territory runs for 100 miles the
Dawson, and Joey Bell used to say it was
potentially Hie richest irrigable belt in Aus-
tralia.

would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
WA
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot. water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason, why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that elynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

Martin Hannah, Victorian M.L.A., who
for ages has been advocating a wider use of
Australian woods, is once more acting as
spruiker for a Made-in-Australia sideshow at
Btate Parliament House. This time it is an
outfit of furnishings for the Commonwealth
offices in London, built entirely by Aus-
tralian craftsmen from Australian timbers.
Next year, war or no war, the A.N.A.
intends to have an Australian exhibition in
Melbourne, whereat particular attention will
be paid to a display of home-grown timbers,
representative of every State. The A.N.A.
does this sort of thing rather well, so there
will doubtless be a very imposing array of
useful and beautiful woods and things Aus-
tralians have made with them.
Simon Hanley, of Diggorra (Vic.), is a
survivor of the Eureka Stockade trouble.
He went forth to the revolt with two
brothers armed with a pistol and a pike.
The trio had just arrived on the fields via
Geelong, and as they were out of Ireland
it was not for them to stand out of a fight
whereby poor bhoys hoped to win their
rights. ' One brother who was riding was
shot in the wrist, and, having only one hand
to control his rearing horse, fell and subse-
quently died from his injuries. Simon has
been peaceably farming in the Rochester
district for 45 years, and as a blue-eyed
veteran finds great comfort in the fact that
he didn’t succeed in damaging anyone.
“Chasuble” : The eight bells of St.
James’s Old Cathedral, Melbourne, having
been rehung, were chimed for the first time
-in their new site on Easter Sunday. The
tongues were only knocked against the sides,
because it was thought the steel frame
might not be strong enough to stand the
regular swinging. These bells are 64 years
old, hung first in 1852. 10 years after the
churcli itself was opened. About two years
ago it was shifted, nearly a mile northwards,
to the corner of King and Batman streets,
where there was once a wooden shanty dat-
ing from 1837, and used by various denomi-
nations until the arrival of the St. James’s
which has just taken up its bells and
walked to make room for more warehouses.
Faith may move mountains, but Melbourne
has seen for itself how Business moves
Faith.
Rua, the Maori prophet who got it in
the neck the other day, once raised a fairly
big loan in a simple way. Being desperately
in need of cash to develop the “great natural
resources” of Rua, he struck the happy idea
of starting a bank. The head office was in
his famous temple at Maungapohatu, and
there were receiving offices in all his pahs.
Rua was Chairman and Board of Directors
and General Manager, while the Accountant
w r as a Tirown friend who took the same size
in liquor and knew as much about astronomy
as he did about accountancy. All Rua’s
subjects were ordered to pay their loose cash
into the bank, and such was the hold that
the long-haired maniac had over his chosen
people that most of them obeyed. One
prosperous native, on going along later to
draw some of his deposit to meet current
expenses, met a smiling Rua, who informed
him that, in spite of excessive care on the
part of the management, the whole lot had
escaped in the night and leapt towards the
Mist Mountains, where the Pale Moon
breathes. When the depositors heard about
this they rushed for their money; where-
upon the prophet looked in the till and told
them there was only sfd. that hadn’t
escaped to the bosom of the Moon. Then
the bank exploded and the smell was heard
all over the King Country.
“Clarrack”: “Verdad” (B. 1/27/T6)
reminds me that South America is not the
only place where streets are called simply
by their names. My “digs” here in San
Francisco are at “126 McAllister,” one block
from Market. Also it is most incorrect here
in writing the date to put the day of the
month first; it must be the month first, as
above. After one year’s sojourn I am quite
Friscofied. I never dream of calling an ele-
vator a lift, or an automobile a motor-car. I
inquired of a traffic conductor once concern-
ing a certain tram. He said “Whart?”
three times; then, with a grin, “Oh, I get
yer; yu mean car.” If I’d told him I’d
been here only a fortnight it would have
brought a stare that would have made me
decide on “two weeks” next time.
Sir Sidney Colvin’s retirement from the
job of London adviser to the Melbourne
National Gallery, following the resignation
of Frank Gibson, Charles Ricketts and Sir
Claude Phillips (representative of the
Felton Bequest Committee), was probably
responsible for an article in which London
Studio denounces the alleged lack of system
in making Felton purchases. ' The writer
deplores that Manet and Degas, the two
great masters of the Impressionist movement,
are not represented in the Gallery, and
states that “Foyer de Danses,” by Degas,
was offered to the Felton committee for £6OO,
but, owing to the bad working arrangements
between the trustees and their London repre-
sentatives, went to the Neutral States for
£2500. But this isn’t quite right. The
picture was a small one, and the price asked
of our Gallery was £lOOO. Still, it would
probably have been a bargain. The fact
that we missed Gainsborough’s “Viscount
Hampden” earns animadversion, and we are
urged to get something by Orpen and Augus-
tus John before the prices soar, or we may
suffer as the British National Gallery did
in the case of Whistler. On the other hand,
it is quite possible to suffer from precipi-
tancy. The S. Aus. Gallery knows what
that means, especially in the case of Orpen.
Unco’ guidness still likes its odor of sanc-
tity a trifle gamey. Cairns’ Memorial
Church, at East Melbourne, has lately been
varnished and veneered at a cost of £350.
Yet, although Cairns’ Memorial has ample
ground space for such a building, the young-
sters’ Sunday-school is still located in the
basement, parts of which haven’t seen Aus-
tralian sunlight since the place was built. 34
years ago. Instead of pushing children
down into the cellar once a week for their
souls’ good, a really wholesome and truly
religious management would have given the
place a coat of sanitary wash, and spent the
rest of the cash on some sun for the infants.
A well-sunned infant will radiate more good
in the long run than the consumptive result
of underground soul-shaping.

THE DAY OF THE EXPERT. To-day is the day of the expert—the man who knows every detail of his work
—who is thorough, efficient and up to date. The engineer who desires to reach the top of the tree in his profession must
be conversant with every engineering method which makes for speed, complete craftsmanship, and for economy.
THE ENGINEER WHO DOES NOT KNOW of the Oxy-Acetylene process is not abreast of the times: he is
at big disadvantage, and leaves himself open to the charge of incompetence.
Throughout the world to-day the Oxy-Acetylene process is the latest scientific addition to every engineering-
workshop, which, fitted with this process, has an advantage over the opposition impossible to estimate. It is simple
yet wonderfully effective; its price is trifling, and the plant is portable. Whether the broken parts require to be cut
or welded at the bench or by the roadside is of no consequence.
Any work of Iron or Steel welding or cutting can be carried out with Oxy-Acetylene. It is revolutionising
the Engineering trade. Broken parts that once were useless can now be successfully welded and made new
r again. This, too. in the matter of minutes. Cost is a matter of pence as compared with the expenditure
of pounds in the old method of buying new parts and making new drawings and castings.
All Engineers should at once write us for free catalogues and illustrations showing the remark-
able variety of work that can be done with Oxy-Acetylene. Following are a few of the broken
and worn machinery parts our process w ill make new again:—
Cracked or broken Aluminium Crank or Gear Cases, Differential Boxes, Steering Wheels, Pipes,
Cylinders, Pistons, Cranks, Clutches, Cardan Shafts, Band Brakes, Worn Keyways in Shafts.
Pieces welded to broken Gears and New Teeth Cut, etc.
Broken Machinery of any kind can be welded, including Cast Iron, Wrought Iron, Mild
Steel, Brass, Copper, Gun Metal, etc., which cannot be repaired by any other process.
The latest authoritative work on the subject, “A Practical Manual of
Autogenous Welding,” published by Charles Griffin & Co., London, states:
“Within the last few years welding by oxy-hydrogen and oxy-acetylene blow-
pipes has been applied in thousands of workshops.” Those workshops which are
rapidly adding the process to their equipment include manufacturers, con-
tractors, machine shops, garages, blacksmiths, boilermakers, repair shops,
foundries, steel mills and railroad shops.

Legal Notice.
■pURSUANT to an Order of the Court of Chancery
-k of the County Palatine of Lancaster Manchester
District made in an Action 1916 Letter A. No. 18.
In the matter of the Trusts of an Indenture of Settle-
ment dated the Hist day of January 1887 made by
Henry Joseph Andrews, Ilea Id v. Bowden. Whereby
an enquiry was directed to be made by the Registrar
as to whether Thomas Corlett Andrews a son of Henry
Joseph Andrews is alive or dead and if dead when
he died and whether he left any and what Will and
whether he was ever married and if so when and to
whom and whether there were any children of the
said marriage and who are his legal personal repre-
sentatives. The said Thomas Corlett Andrews or if
he be dead his legal personal representative or the
persons claiming to be his next of kin or to be the
legal personal representatives of such of the said
next of kin as have since died or any of them are by
their Solicitors on or before the eleventh day of July
1916 to come in and prove their claims before the
Registrar of the Manchester District at his Chambers
• situate at 4 Clarence Street, Manchester, England, or
in default thereof they will be peremptorily excluded
from the benefit of the said Order. Friday the 21st
day of July 1916 at 11 o’clock in the forenoon at the
said Chambers is the time and place appointed for
the adjudication on the said claims.
NOTE. —Thomas Corlett Andrews who was a son
of the said Henry Joseph Andrews formerly resided
at Gatley Cheadle in the County of Chester and is
believed'to have sailed for Australia in or about the
month of February 1908. For some time letters
were addressed to him c/o Messrs. Dalgety & Co.
Limited of Adelaide Australia who used to forward
them to various addresses he left with them. In
December 1908 the said Thomas Corlett Andrews was
at Pooncarie New South Wales. The last-known
address of the said Thomas Corlett Andrews was
Cambewarra P.O. New South Wales from which
address he wrote on the 14th June 1909.
Dated this 20th dav of March. 1916.
HUBERT WINSTANLEY,
Registrar.

ESTABLISHED 1860.
The Inky Way.
Harold McCauley, one time of Brisbane
Daily Standard, and more recently of
Melbourne Age, is now with the Light Horse
at Seymour. After a few weeks foot slog-
ging with the infantry he sacrificed two
stripes to get astride a horse again. He
doesn’t go green to the game, as lie had three
years with the 14th Light Horse in Banana-
land.
* * * *
F. W. Boreliam, Baptist parson, book-
writer and Inky Wayfarer, has had a call
and leaves Hobart for Armadale (Vic.).
Boreliam was once a reporter on the London
Daily Chronicle ; then he was drafted into
the Baptist Shepherds’ Union, and fed the
slieep first at Mosgiel (M.L..) and for the
last 10 years at Hobart. Besides preaching
and writing books he produces essays by the
yard, and brightens up the local Mercury
with more essays, billed as leading articles.
* * * *
Richard Harding Davis, the lately-dead
Yank war correspondent, was probably worn
to death by liis clothes. Richard always
looked like a tailor’s latest creation, and
even in war he had his underpants creased
and pink clocks worked into his pistol hol-
sters. Despite his dandyism, however, he
was gritty and resourceful, with a large
capacity for observing the things that
matter. ,
Henry Brett, owner of newspapers and a
benefactor of Auckland (M.L.), confessed
to a record the other day while wondering
what next to give his home town. He
said he had started singing in a choir at
the age of six and hadn’t given his voice a
rest since, a distance of about 73 years. He
is now choirmaster in his josshouse. Brett,
by the way, set the type that broke the news
of the end of the Crimean War.
* * * *
“C. Ockney”: Kennedy Jones, who
recently contested the Wimbledon (Surrey)
seat, and but for a matter of two thousand
obstinate objectors would have won it, is
Northcliffe’s riglit-liand man. He was a
struggling journalist in the English pro-
vinces not so much more than 10 years ago.
Ilis rise (or fall) lias been meteoric, and
he is now well on the road to being a mil-
lionaire. Like Horatio Bottomley (who in
other ways he much resembles) Jones is fond
of the turf, and owns a number of race-
horses. But, unlike Bottomley, lie has not
vet annexed any race of importance, or won
the right to put M.P. after his name.
“Eddyson” : A story of an Australian bard
of whom we were all very fond, despite a
Bohemianism that made him out of place
in a well-ordered household. Once when
living in Melbourne lie sold an accumulation
of rejects and bought a fortnight’s supply of
whiskv. “No man with self-respect should
b* in ’the position of having to run out for
sixpen’orth when the spirit calls,” said he.
Next morning his wife and a black-and-
white artist found the sweet singer down
in the parlor, spread on the hearthrug,
embracing an empty demijohn. It took him
14 hours to recover recollection. Then lie
explained. “I couldn't get the whisky oft
mv mind,” lie said. “Suppose the house
took fire! The thought wouldn’t be shaken
off, so I got up and covered my risk.”
*■ * * *
“T. 0.1.” : The fire at Tenfold’s, in Sydney,
recalis that this is one of the oldest printeries
in Australia —perhaps the oldest. It was
founded by William Moffitt, in King-street
East in 1820, and came into Pitt-street in
the early ’3o’s. When Moffitt died in the
’7o's the business fell into the hands of a
Mr Yeo, who had been manager for many
years. On Yeo’s death the Penfolds came
in John Sands’s business was founded in
the mid-’3o’s, in a house still standing in
George-street, near the corner of Market-
street.
STANZAS BY AN OVERWORKED SUBURBAN
POET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The lawn,” Chlorine announced to-day,
“Just looks a positive disgrace” ;
A shadow of annoyance lay
Across the joy that is her face.
Off to my room I went to gaze
For long into my inkwell there,
While from its depths I sought to raise
A girl with some new' kind of hair.
Of black-haired wenches, big and bold,
And maids with locks of purple sheen,
Of ringlets wrought of living gold
I’ve raved, and e’en of tresses green!
Is there not left some Tot or Grace,
Who from my inky Well of Lies
I yet may lure, to help me chase
The troubled look from Chlorine's eyes?
I call my sinless past to mind,
And curse the girls I never kissed—
Alas! that men could be so blind—
They make indeed a lengthy list!
Some dead romance might help to-day ;
Unfortunately, though, in rhymes
The very few that came my way
I have exploited many times.
Madge, whom I -wooed but never w'on,
Helped me the furniture to buy ;
Dot often saved me from a dun
In impecunious days gone by.
Of Elsie I wrote many reams,
With which the kitchen shelves I filled ;
Lolette, whom I but knew in dreams,
The fowlyard kindly helped to build.
I've written off the ancient debt
Upon the house in which I live;
Jean gave her aid and fair Phrynette—
But now they have no more to give!
The lawn's indeed a shocking sight;
It well may move Chlorine to tears ;
But still I strive in vain to write
A mower and a pair Of shears.
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

The Judgment of Harris.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
train was languid and the way
I was long.
The whining brakes at wayside
stations talked ;
And I regretted that the sun was strong
Or I perchance had tumbled out and
walked.
I yawned each mile —a hundred times or
so —•
And watched the three girls sitting in a
row.
Three commonplace and dowdy maids they
seemed,
Though one was rather pretty in a way.
Under her yellow lashes blue eyes gleamed ;
And as they roved around they seemed to
say:
«[ w ish these other tiresome girls were
gone;
Travelling alone, I’m sure we could get on.”
The second maid was dark and rather pale.
She kept a steady front and never spoke.
Her hat was beehive shape ; a rabbit’s tail,
Or something like it, nodded stroke on
stroke
With every jolt the toiling engine made,
Pulling us southward through the forest
glade.
The third was of a larger, bolder style,
Red-haired, green-eyed and stout of hand
and foot,
With quantities of teeth which in her smile
Glittered with gold enough for thieves to
loot;
And her slow voice was resonant and strong
With brazen rollings like a Chinese gong.
The languor of the afternoon increased ;
My brain seemed empty as a paper bag ;
The engine like a weary, sullen beast
Grunted and tried my angry thoughts to
drag
A little faster through the breathless heat
That over all creation kept its seat.
Who threw the apple in I never knew.
It seemed to come from nowhere in a
trice;
But each girl grabbed it as it came in view
And each declared the other wasn’t nice
In such a truculent and heartfelt way
I shuddered at the words I heard them say.
The row grew worse. I stood upon a seat
Leaving them space to wrangle to and fro.
They scratched, they yelled, they swung
their arms and feet,
Their wildered locks about me seemed to
flow ;
They rent their garments till they seemed to
wear
No blessed thing at all except their hair.
And whether it was rage or nakedness
I really had no power at all to tell.
But each girl when she got outside her dress
Seemed to have changed ; in fact, they all
looked well,
Though I for very shame had turned my
head,
Pretending I was blind or drunk or dead.
The battle ceased. They did not yell or
scream, .
But merely panted, standing in a row.
The sunlight in a hot and dusty beam
Poured in a yellow flood upon the show ;
And in its brightness that ripe apple lay
The red-streaked cause of all the fierce
affray.
My thought was but one incoherent smudge.
I placed that shining apple on my knee ;
And then the big one spoke : “Here, you be
judge!
Which is the nicest looking of us three?”
The others chorused sweetly: “You decide !
Who gets the apple for the best outside?”
I am a modest man ; yet I was plaeed
In such a curious predicament,
The task they gave me simply must be
faced;
So to the job my languid wuts I bent.
And all three smiled as hard as they could
smile
To catch my plain judicial soul with guile.
The tall one spoke: “Election day is near.
I have a lot of say in such affairs ;
Five hundred pounds for talk is sometimes
dear
To those who pay it, but the billet wears
A pleasant wreath of joy around its hat
For those who have a taste for things like
that.”
She laughed serenely, but I shook my head ;
And then the dark girl wildly butted in:
111 give you something more than that,”
she said.
‘l’ll put you on the track to safely win
By your own efforts anything you like —
Post money’ is a useful thing to strike.
Have a good look at me and really see

As clear as kookaburras in the morn;
And then the slow train labored on its way
And nobody had any more to say.
The landscape jolted very slowly past;
The tall, grey ring-barked trees in silence
fled.

And time, sat heavy on my weary head,

Although within my heart I did not care,
In that pale instant I felt all bereft;
And all that evening through the engine’s

CURSE O' MOSES.
Las Palmas.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’XTTT'EST of the morning, a hill; delicate
\ \ as a wisp of cloud, looking so
fragile through the haze that a
soft wind might have blown it away. The
sea, a deep blue, fluttering like a hundred
flags ; and the smoke of the steamer, racing
its own shadow, loses itself in the colors of
the air.
Separated only by a distance of three
miles the cloud hardens into hot stone
swathed over with yellow «sand.
No birds.
A hole in the hills, the fallen mouth of a
dead giant—and the white stones of a ceme-
tery.... Yet not a cemetery, after all; only
houses. Three men, quite close to us, in a
boat like an old box, seem to imitate flying-
fish by waving their oars as much in the
sunlight as in the water. They shake their
hats to us, and keep on shaking them, till
they duck out of sight.
Some of the passengers, grown childish
after long days of nothing to do, set up a
strange chant without any meaning, and
then suddenly all burst out laughing at their
own silliness. The boatmen listen
attentively.
People begin asking all sorts of stupid
questions. The coast continues to go by,
just as a picture unfolds on a cinematograph
film ; colors change, outlines alter. But the
sea is always the same.
We pass a wireless station with four
very high poles, quite solitary looking and
right at the edge of the water.
They say there are men living on the
hills behind there with caves for houses; I
wonder if it is true and am afraid to look
incredulous.
Then gradually, yet just as surely, and
with the same effect as though, watching
through glasses, we had trimmed them to
our vision, Las Palmas appears before us.
A careless scattering of whitewashed
buildings, and, above these, the twin high
towers of a sixteenth-century cathedral.
Blackened with age and weather, it rises on
two strong wings like an eagle against the
flat hills at the back. There is no beach....
only a wall.
Leaving the city negligently behind, we
pass on to the port and find that, except for
two rusty old steamers, we have the harbor
to ourselves. But not for long. About
seventy or eighty boats come struggling from
the shore to meet us. These boats with
their oars all tangled together resemble a
suddenly disturbed nest of water-spiders
tearing towards the ship. Men stand up
close against the thwarts holding baskets of
oranges, bananas, cigars, etc. Some even
are wanting to sell dogs, and show fingers
for the number of shillings required. Then
there are canaries, lots of them, in bamboo
cages ; shawls and packets of indecent photo-
graphs ; tomatoes of a pale geranium color;
long strings of beads.
Under the boats are solid blue reflections,
not with any detail as we see them in Aus-
tralia, but thick unpatterned shadows. A
boy, quite naked except for a loin-cloth,
beckons to us to throw money in the water.
He is rowing his own skiff and is companion-
less. We toss a threepenny bit to within
about six feet of him and watch the silver
turn over and over as it goes under. The
boy drops after the coin any old way, and
presently comes up again gripping it in his
teeth.
Ready for a fresh diversion we turn to
note the arrival on deck of the doctor, a
withered little bun-faced Spaniard, dressed
in a khaki -suit and peaked cap to match,
with gold leaves round the band. He carries
a thin black stick which he shifts from his
right claw to his left when he shakes hands
with the ship’s surgeon. A torrent of
hucksters sweep on board.and carry him out
of sight as we go down the gangway to the
launch.
On the quay, built of dingy white stone,
we find beggars, hotel-porters, policemen and
four-wheeled carriages; we choose a con-
veyance and a guide, and drive to the city.
The road is made of huge cobbles like loaves
of bread, but our three grey horses can go
like the devil. We pass plantations of
bananas and long, monotonous fields divided
into little squares by sunken rubble, pre-
sumably to retain the water wherever it
falls. The footpaths appear to be about 24
inches wide, and the streets nearly as narrow
again as the winding lanes of Sydney.
The way to town lies parallel with the
shore, and, when we look backward, we can
(Continued on next page.)
