filling the skips with coal. In this work there is

to get nasty pains in the small of my back. At first
I thought they might be caused by fatigue, but they
continued and grew worse. They troubled me also at
night when in bed. as if there was something hard
gripping my kidneys. Matters looked pretty serious,
so I commenced trying different remedies, but I did
not meet with any success until I got Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills. They have given me splendid
relief; I can now work with ease and am quite free
of backache.”
Fourteen years later Mr. Merrill says: “Fourteen
years is a long spell, but time has made no difference
in my cure. I am still absolutely free of
backache. That is a good record, and proves that
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are a genuine remedy.

pain in my back. My ankles and arms swelled a

Four years later Mrs. Osborne says: “Twelve ynars

still see between the houses the Beltana and
the boats in myriads about her.
The dust gets into our mouths and a big
double-decked tram worries away to the
harbor; a man, the size and shape of Sancho
I’anza, patters behind us on a mule not
much larger than a do^.
There are no more fields now, only shops,
straight-up-and-down ones, flat-roofed, with-
out verandahs or even awnings. The doors
reach very nearly to the full height of the
ground floors.
Our first visit is to a cigarette and cigar
factory. We dash into an abominable little
lane j rags, beggars and dogs everywhere;
soldiers in what look like striped pyjama
pants; in the background the clean blue
mountain shimmering up into the sky. The
guide g'oes before us and explains the making
and the packing of the cigarettes. We
sneese. steal, buy, gaze secretly at the
women, and hurry out again.
Higgledy-piggledy into the carriage, or
cart, or whatever you like to call it, and we
whir round to a curio-shop where we buy
postcards, carved elephants, shawls and fans.
* * * *
We skipped the Casino and forgot the
Torture-Chamber; but we saw the
Cathedral. No need to tell you of the lame
and the blind at the gates, or of the thick,
sweet sunlight flowing steadily over the
dusty trees all day outside. Following a
little girl into the doorway we were sud-
denly surrounded by darkness; the great
depth from the roof to the floor made the
sound of our movements almost echoless.
The light was dim and floated above us like
the wave on the surface of a well. I
noticed two women (nuns, I think), in
white robes and white hoods, praying at
separate shrines. Their attitude, their
absolute stillness, and the heavy mist of
incense which enveloped them, affected me
strangely. A priest in a black gown came
through a shadow and passed us without
once turning his head, so that we were left
with the silent women and the tall cross
shining over ns. We walked before the
altar into the room where the vestments and
ornaments are kept; saw the gold and the
silver, the rich dresses, the candles six feet
long, and, in a glass jar, the heart of a
bishop ! But it really looked more like the
heart of a bullock, the proportions were so
great.
Later we ascended one of the towers,
going up a spiral stairway of stone without
any railing to hold to, until we came out
upon a square platform under the bells,
whence we were able to see the city and the
mountain and the ships in the bay.
* * * *
The day is not so bright now as it
has been, but it has left us thirsty and
full of nervous excitement. Having been
warned not to drink of the wine of the island,
we go to an English hotel, where, seated in
the street under a tree, we sip German lager
out of clean glasses, admiring the scenery
all the while, and becoming perhaps by this
time a shade more critical.
If the mountain is blue behind that
queer tov/er, it is also monotonous because of
its pei’petual presence; it creates the back-
ground of everything—we can never get rid
of it. Moreover, we have discovered that
the atmosphere has a trick of giving houses
and hills alike an appearance of flatness
which is positively theatrical. The build-
ings are apparently only thin strips of can-
vas stretched on wood, without any inside
or backs to them; they look as though they
were ready to rattle to pieces under the
first stroke of someone’s lusty fist. The
mountain and the sky seem one piece of a
painted cloth, and nobody would feel surprised
if the whole affair were to suddenly wind up
into the air to make room for the next act.
But the guide is getting nervous about us ;
he is so afraid we might miss the steamer
that he comes down from his seat by the
driver and implores us to hurry.
Again the three grey horses, again the long
cobbled stretch to the sea. Boys in tatters
run after us. “Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay! Ta-
ra-ra boom-de-ay! One penny, Meester!
One penny!”
There is just time to call at the last wine-
shop—the usual, flat, dusty-looking house
with all its shutters up. The only entrance
lies through a high, narrow door, thence
down a dark passage, and so to a room fur-
nished with chairs, tables, and a squat bar-
counter. Here we find the ship’s doctor in
his white uniform, busy over a bottle of some-
thing that looks like muscat, but isn’t. .He
orders two more and invites us to drink.
So, forgetting all warnings, we. accept and
inquisitively lick it out of little silver-rimmed
glasses.
A blind Spaniard is thrumming on a
guitar, and the hot night air is fogaed with
the smoke of cheap, black cigars. The shop-
keeper, in English clothes, straw hat and
full wide shirt, keeps up a continuous flow
of talk that is perfectly maddening. He is a
fat. clean-shaved man, brown and oily, with
arms that are never still. He shows silk
blouses and skirts, but nobody will take
anything, although it would be worth the
money he asks if only it would silence him.
Failing to wheedle us into buying liis
blouses he gives us a cigar each out of his
own box, and endeavors, for a change, to
sell us some more of his wine. “Fair and
square, sir—-yes, sir, that’s what I am.
John Bull is my name. I am known all
over England. Ask them in England if they
ever heard of John Bull!”
We say good-bye and hurry outside to our
three grey horses, only just arriving in time
at the quay; to catch the last launch. The
men in the boats are still clinging to the
sides of the steamer, and the canary that
would have been sold in the morning for five
shillings is now down to eighteen-pence, for
the bargaining goes on even while we are
moving, and many long ropes with baskets
on the ends of them are lowered from the
tnffrail to be drawn up again filled with
fruit and vegetables.
An hour later there is no evidence of
Las Palmas, unless it can be traced in the
banana-skins in the scuppers and the faint
glow beating up against the night far away
over the water.
HUGH McCRAE.
“East is East.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AH MAT, Malay, had been curry-cook
to a great Rajah somewhere. How
he came to be now merely cook-boy in
a little island hospital only Ah Mat and his
lady friend Kadijah knew. Wizened and
ugly but spotlessly clean was Ah Mat, with
childlike smile and little toes that ran
trippingly when one called.
You and I and none else but these two
may know that Kadijah had been one of the
Rajah’s two hundred wives who lived in
great luxury behind his velvet-curtained
purdah.
Beneath the innocence of her eyes lay
in those days many imaginings, one being
that she would yet be chief wife. Zadieka,
who occupied that envied position, thought
otherwise. Kadijah, with those long, gold
eyes and that aura of complete simplicity,
was the one wife whom she feared. So she
saw to it that the Rajah came not to her.
In a hundred ways she made life in the
purdah unbearable for Kadijah; and it was
when things were at their worst that
Kadijah discovered the curry-cook, Ah Mat,
who brought her sweets to eat and word of
the outside world.
One orchid-scented evening Zadieka came
upon them as they' sat together under the
frangipanis near the fountain. And now
Kadijah’s life was in the hollow of her
hand, and by to-morrow’s daylight the gold
gleams of those eyes would be out for ever!
She was foolish enough to say so; where-
upon Ah Mat took her dainty little
throat in his two hands and crushed the
taunts and the breath out of it. He and his
Kadijah just managed to escape as the
Rajah’s bodyguard came running up.
With them they took the Red Pearl of
great price, which always spilt blood, as all
great gems must. The Rajah’s chief wife
had worn it strung in a golden mesh upon
her bosom. •
* * * *
Ah Mat hid the pearl, and became cooky-
boy in the little island hospital far from his
own Malaya; and whilst he was at work his
dear Kadijah secretly sought the gem and
sought in vain.
Down the poincianas track that flamed
with crimson flowers and led through the
tennis courts to the hospital came Kadijah
one golden afternoon. On her little head she
carried a pandanus basket of sun-ripe grana-
dillas for Ah Mat, her lord. Rose-striped
was her silken sarong, gold were the buttons
of her dainty white kabaia, gold were her
eyes, and gold the clasp that held her long
black hair. Outlined against the cobalt blues
of Torres Strait, it was all very beautiful.
The young subs, from the garrison were
playing off a tournament on the beach court
this sunspread eve as Kadijah in i 1 0„ ,
passed by. Willis, the 0.C., who w», g ° lda
nearly fell off his stool in his
both the vision and the plav. Ho k j See
eyes for beauty. Wayne, the senior l w ,°
tern, to whose care Mrs. Willis— eo no
sick of a fever—had commended her w 0
frowned slightly; and the IZmt f “ ■
men from New Guinea, drinking tea S
the crotons with some tennis girls smiHa

adoration mostly took the shape of wonder*
ful curries that he composed and toT.
Simon the Cingalee meaa-boy, s ‘°* »
Ills sahibs. And when there were guests u
Mat scurried through his work at the hosni
tal and loaned himself to the mess
extra help. dII
Two moons passed.
This guest-night Ah Mat had made n
curry fit for the gods of Malaya, and himself
served it wearing his whitest linens and his
most childlike smile. One tiny norti™
colored red he gave to Willis. j l]gt
before coffee came round the O.C. casually
mentioned that he was wanted at barracks
but wouldn’t be long, and Wayne would look
after them till he came back.
At midnight the guests departed, and still
the O.C. had not reappeared.
A little later Wayne went out. First to
barracks. None there had seen the OG
Then down the Lovers’ Walk to the end of
the island where Ah Mat and Kadiiah
lived on the beach beneath the palm patch
The gold-sprinkled beauty of the tropic
night almost took Wayne’s breath away as he
came down the hill that faced the Strait.
The moon rose in a copper crescent between
two peaks of Waiben. Beyond the Outer
Isles the purpling waters curved to a foamy
film about the reef. The whole world was
heavy with the mingling of many scents. ‘
The hut was strangely quiet. As Wayne
lifted the latch and went in. he saw Kadijah,
the beautiful, fallen on her face, with Willis's
gold watchcliairi linked in her fingers. And
Willis lay not far off.
“Murder! God!” said the senior sub.
Yet there was no wound upon Willis!
Back to the quarters Wayne went for Jack-
son, his junior, and together they carried
back their dead. Then they fetched the regi-
mental surgeon, and together they made it
that Willis was found dead in his bed of a
sudden heart-failure. So Willis had the
biggest and most important burying the
island ever saw, and his widow shrined him
in her heart for ever.
And Ah Mat, Malay, squatting between
decks on the s.s. Falc, which had sat beside
the wharf that night waiting for a tide to
take her out. fingered the Red Pearl that
always brought blood. And, fingering it,
smiled.
All of which goes to prove the wisdom of
having a subordinate with his wits about
him ; the folly of leaving one’s husband on a
desert isle ; and (where Ah Mat and Kadijah
come in) that East is East all the way
round.
S.S. SEVERN.
